# Publisher’s Note

Dr. Sundara Shanmuganar has offered Tamil readers, in the form of this book, the fruit of his many years of research into the captivating savour of *Cilappatikāram*.

Through this work we can appreciate with particular clarity the epic artistry and poetic skill of Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ, the fitting construction of his characters, and the brisk movement of his story.

This outstanding critical study of *Cilappatikāram*, the epic of the three branches of Tamil, has itself been created almost like a new work of literature.

Because the book is written in prose, everyone can read and understand it. Professor Sundara Shanmuganar cites passages from *Cilappatikāram* throughout and communicates the essence of the epic to the public in Tamil that is pleasant, simple, and touched with humour.

Readers of this fine study of *Cilappatikāram* will certainly feel the desire to read and enjoy the whole epic.

Under thirty-one headings, from “The Epic of the Three Crowned Kings” to “Solutions to Certain Problems,” the author explains the profound substance of the epic with relish.

Besides citing many works in support of his interpretations, the author establishes his arguments by supplying numerous incidents drawn from worldly experience.

Dr. Sundara Shanmuganar, who has given us this book, deserves the praise and appreciation of Tamil people.

I publish it through Vānati Patippakam in the confidence that Tamil readers will welcome it warmly. Receive it with pleasure.

**Vānati A. Tirunavukkarasu**

# Entrance

## *Cilappatikāram*

Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ, who belonged to the period following the Cankam age and was attached to the Jain faith, heard the story of Kaṇṇaki from Cāttaṉār, the grain merchant of Madurai. Since Kaṇṇaki’s anklet holds the central place in that history, he composed a book connected with the anklet and named it *Cilappatikāram*.

Just as *irumpu* + *pātai* becomes *iruppuppātai* (“railway”) and *karumpu* + *vil* becomes *karuppuvil* (“sugarcane bow”), *cilampu* + *atikāram* becomes *Cilappatikāram*. *Atikāram* is that which grows or develops continuously. The *Piṅkala Nikaṇṭu* also gives “book” as one meaning of the word *atikāram*. Thus the work received the title *Cilappatikāram*, “the book concerning the anklet.” It is also known in shortened form as *Cilampu*.

This is one of the Five Great Epics of Tamil. Among them, the twin epics *Cilappatikāram* and *Maṇimēkalai* deal with histories that took place in Tamil Nadu. Of those two, *Cilappatikāram* is foremost. Cēraṉ Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ built a temple of stone for Kaṇṇaki; his younger brother Iḷaṅkō built this temple of words.

## *Silambō Silambu!*

Subramania Bharati praised the epic as “the *Cilappatikāram* that seizes the heart.” It possesses beauty of sound and the power to intensify feeling.

This work, a revolution of womanhood, begins in the mode of happiness but finally ends in tragedy. Even so, through portions such as the Canto of Benediction, Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ supplies a somewhat artificial auspicious conclusion.

Some passages spoken in the author’s own voice employ a rarefied style, whereas the words placed in the mouths of the characters move in styles suited to them. It is a dramatic epic—need anything more be said about the flavour of its language?

Because events belonging to different times are placed wherever the circumstances require them, one may be tempted to say that *Cilampu* contains invented fictions. Yet, compared with many other epics, its false inventions are few. It is difficult to write an epic with no imaginative element at all. A work written entirely without imagination would not be an epic but a history book. This, however, is an epic.

Though *Cilampu* is an epic of ordinary citizens, it lacks nothing in literary beauty. A separate book ought to be written about the arts within it.

## How This Book Took Shape

Many critical books and essays on *Cilappatikāram* had already appeared. Why, then, did I write this book so late?

Many years ago, while studying for the Tamil M.A. examination, I filled two notebooks with notes. Later, because I lay bedridden for many years with a brain tumour, and because those notebooks remained hidden in a place where I could not see them, I was unable to turn the notes into a book. Recently, while searching for something else, I happened to find the notebooks in a bundle of papers lying on a loft. I shortened the notes to some extent, added a few new ideas, and made this book from them. One should not allow notes to go to waste, should one?

Although I studied for the Tamil Vidwan and Tamil M.A. examinations, and although I taught the epic in college, only now do I feel the inward satisfaction of having studied *Cilappatikāram* with at least some degree of method. *Cilappatikāram* is a vast ocean difficult to cross. Only a few pearls from it are introduced in this book. I am also conscious of the limits of space.

## Criticism

Some of my critical views may differ from those of others. For the Tamil M.A. examination, I chose literary criticism as my optional subject. I have read several books on criticism in both Tamil and English. No one can define conclusively, “This alone is criticism; criticism must be performed only in this way.” Each person’s principles may differ. My own criticism too may therefore take a form of its own.

The book has been given the shortened title *Tittikkum Tiṟaṉāyvu*. I ask readers to expand this as “criticism that makes [the work] taste sweet.” In other words, it means criticism that makes *Cilappatikāram* delightful. I have tried, to the extent of my knowledge and ability, to make *Cilappatikāram* taste sweet. That is all.

It may also be understood as an inquiry into Iḷaṅkō’s skill in making *Cilappatikāram* delightful.

To support my views, I have supplied a number of literary quotations, common expressions, and incidents from life. I have also included some phrases and anecdotes for humour. I ask readers not to regard them as crude. Does one not need an occasional change of flavour?

## Repetition of What Has Already Been Said

An idea connected with several people or several matters may recur in the sections dealing with each person or matter. According to the principle, “Repeating what has been said is no fault when it brings forth another meaning,” I ask readers kindly to forgive such repetitions.

I have done what I could after passing the age of seventy while continuing to bear the troubles associated with a brain tumour. Please accept it.

I offer my deep thanks to the respected book expert A. Tirunavukkarasu, who published this book handsomely and well. I also thank Sabanayagam Press. Greetings.

Puducherry–11  
15 September 1992  
**Sundara Shanmuganar**


---

# 1. The Epic of the Three Crowned Kings

In ancient times, when monarchy prevailed, kings fought one another. Even in the twentieth century, when republican government is practised, there is no shortage of war. Nations fight one another at the instigation of their leaders. Although the natural impulse toward aggression exists in everyone, political leaders often seem not to accept the responsibility of sublimating it, redirecting it, and overcoming it.

Tamilakam offers a particular example. In antiquity, the three crowned dynasties—the Cōḻas, Pāṇḍyas, and Cēras—along with lesser rulers and chieftains who were either allied with them or independent of them, fought one another almost without pause. If we read the books written throughout the world under the heading “history,” we find that they are filled largely with accounts of wars.

In ancient Tamilakam, poets such as Auvaiyār appear to have travelled as emissaries between kings and laboured to preserve peace. We also find poems in which poets praised the rulers who supported them. But if there was one exalted poet of unity who regarded the Cēra, Pāṇḍya, and Cōḻa—the three crowned kings—with equal vision, we may say that it was Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ, the poet of *Cilappatikāram*.

Aṭikaḷ divided his work into three great books: the Pukār Book, belonging to the Cōḻas; the Madurai Book, belonging to the Pāṇḍyas; and the Vañci Book, belonging to the Cēras. For this reason his *Cilappatikāram* possesses the distinction of being “the Epic of the Three Crowned Kings.” There are many further proofs.

## The Canto of Summer

In the Canto of Summer, the poet describes the arrival of the early summer season. He sings that early summer, the joyful companion of Māraṉ, the Love-god who rules Madurai, Uṟantai, Vañci, and Pukār in Tamilakam—whose boundaries are Tiruvēṅkaṭam in the north and Kumari in the south—has arrived:

> “In the cool, well-watered land whose Tamil boundary lies  
> between the hill of the Tall One and the sea of the bangle-wearing maiden,  
> in towered Madurai, in exalted Uṟantai,  
> in bustling Vañci and Pukār,  
> early summer has come—the delightful companion  
> of King Māraṉ, famed in speech,  
> who sits enthroned and rules them.” (1–7)

When the poet merely wished to say that early summer had arrived, there was no necessity to mention the capitals of all three crowned kings. Yet he names Madurai of the Pāṇḍyas, Uṟantai and Pukār of the Cōḻas, and Vañci of the Cēras. He has used this place as an opportunity to express his feeling of unity.

“The Tall One” is Tirumāl. “The bangle-wearing maiden” is Kumari. The word *pauvam*, meaning sea, tells us that the southern boundary, like the eastern and western boundaries, was ocean. At this point the commentator Aṭiyārkkunallār explains at length how the cruel sea swallowed the Kumari ridge.

## Metrical Craft

One feature of Iḷaṅkō’s metrical design deserves attention here. He often places alliteration corresponding to the first metrical foot in the third or fourth foot; when he does not use alliteration, he supplies second-syllable rhyme. The quoted passage makes this clear. *Neṭiyōṉ–toṭiyōḷ* form second-syllable rhyme; *Tamil–taṇ* form alliteration; *māṭa–pīṭār* form second-syllable rhyme; *kali–oli* form second-syllable rhyme; *aracu–uraicāl* form second-syllable rhyme; and *maṉṉaṉ–makiḻ* form alliteration. The same feature can be observed elsewhere.

## The Canto of the Memorial Stone

Through the three crowned kings, Kaṇṇaki is said to have imparted three lessons. Through the Cōḻa she declared that women’s chastity can flourish only when rulers govern without allowing the straight sceptre to deviate. Through the Pāṇḍya she showed that just kings cannot remain alive when their sceptre bends. Through the Cēra Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, conqueror of the northern mountain, she demonstrated that kings do not abandon their anger until they have fulfilled the vow they uttered. The poem says:

> “The ancient good saying of cool Tamil declares:  
> ‘Unless mighty rulers administer justice,  
> chastity cannot flourish among women of great renown.’  
> Because she was the Pattini whom the earth worships and praises,  
> she gave that lesson to the king  
> whose crown bears the ātti garland;  
> she gave the southern guardian king the lesson  
> that life cannot continue when the straight sceptre bends;  
> and, so that the kings of the northern quarter might know  
> that sovereigns do not lay aside fierce anger  
> until their declared vow is fulfilled,  
> she gave that lesson to the conqueror of the western quarter.” (207–217)

“The king whose crown bears the ātti garland” means the Cōḻa. “The guardian of the southern land” means the Pāṇḍya king who guards the southern part of Tamilakam. “The conqueror who lives in the western quarter” is Cēraṉ Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, ruler of western Tamilakam. Here too Aṭikaḷ brings all three great dynasties together.

## The Canto of Benediction

### The Benedictions

In the Canto of Benediction, women take turns singing about the three crowned kings. Under the heading “Benediction,” we read:

> “Long live, long live  
> the ancient line of the lord of Madurai,  
> encircled by the ever-flowing waters of the Vaigai!” (13)

> “Long live, long live  
> the ancient line of the lord of Vañci,  
> encircled by the cool Porunai  
> of ever-flowing waters!” (14)

> “Let us sing, let us sing  
> the lord of the Kāviri land,  
> O flower-haired women of Pukār!” (15)

Thus the lord of Madurai, the lord of Vañci, and the lord of the Kāviri land are all blessed. The water-richness of their countries is also celebrated. The Cōḻa is lord of the Kāviri whose waters keep coming—*varupuṉal*, “ever-flowing water.”

### The *Ammāṉai* Songs

*Ammāṉai* is a game in which three women toss counters or a ball while singing and dancing. The first woman raises a question; the second answers it; the third brings the thought to its conclusion. After the benedictions there are four *ammāṉai* songs about the Cōḻa. We may take the first as an example:

> “Who is the mighty one who ruled the world  
> ringed by swelling waters  
> and guarded the lofty fortress  
> of the king of the gods—tell me, *ammāṉai*!  
> The one who guarded that lofty fortress  
> is the Cōḻa who hurled down the three suspended forts  
> in the high sky—see, *ammāṉai*!  
> Sing the Cōḻa’s city of Pukār, *ammāṉai*!” (16)

The three women end each line of their song with “*ammāṉai, ammāṉai*” as they dance. There are three more such songs. We may next turn to the Pāṇḍya.

### The Ball Songs

*Kantukam* means ball; *vari* means song. These are songs sung while playing ball. The section contains three four-line songs. The third and fourth lines of one song recur as the third and fourth lines of all three:

> “We strike the ball as we move, crying,  
> ‘Long live, long live the southern king!’  
> We strike the ball, crying,  
> ‘Long live the chest adorned with the gods’ garland!’” (21)

These lines close all three songs.

### The Swing Songs

The Cōḻa receives the *ammāṉai* songs; the Pāṇḍya receives the ball songs. Third, three swing songs are sung for the Cēra. *Ūcal* means swing. The closing lines of the three songs are:

> “Shall we sing the emblem of the curved bow and swing?” (23)
>
> “Shall we sing how he felled the kadamba and swing?” (24)
>
> “Shall we sing the emblem of the mighty bow and swing?” (25)

They refer to the Cēra’s bow emblem and his victory in felling the kadamba tree.

At the end of the Canto of Benediction, one pestle song is sung for each of the three dynasties. A *vaḷḷai* song is sung about a leader while pounding some substance in a mortar with a pestle. The songs are:

### The Pestle Songs

> “The women of Pukār,  
> beneath the shade of flowering kāñci trees,  
> pound splendid pearls  
> with sweet sugarcane as their fine pestle.  
> Their true song is the song  
> of the broad, mountain-like shoulders of the Cempiyan,  
> whose firm chariot bears the discus-banner  
> and whose garland is thick with fresh flowers.  
> It is the song that makes maidens cry aloud!” (26)

> “The women of towered Madurai  
> pound song-worthy pearls  
> with a coral pestle.  
> Their true song is the song  
> of the fish-banner of the Pañcavaṉ,  
> whose shoulders shine with the garland  
> received from the king of the gods.  
> It is the song that crushes the hearts  
> of those who wear neem garlands!” (27)

> “The women of Vañci  
> pour excellent jewelled pearls  
> into a sandalwood mortar  
> and pound them with a pestle of elephant tusk.  
> Their true song is the fame  
> of the Cēra who crossed and destroyed his enemies  
> and felled the kadamba.  
> It is the song that spreads over and covers the earth;  
> it is the song that captures hearts with the palmyra wreath!” (28)

These are play-songs. The women are imagined as pounding with pestles made of sugarcane, coral, and elephant tusk; the mortar itself is made of sandalwood. Is this not literature fit to be savoured? Māṇikkavācakar’s *Tiruvācakam* likewise contains a section called *Tiruppoṟcuṇṇam*. Each of its twenty songs ends with the refrain, “Let us pound the sacred golden powder.”

When we examine this Canto of Benediction, we are tempted to wonder whether Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ used the whole canto chiefly to praise the excellence of the three crowned kings.

## The Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai*

At the very opening of the Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai*, the poem declares that the fish emblem of the Pāṇḍyas, the tiger emblem of the Cōḻas, and the bow emblem of the Cēras were engraved upon Mount Himālaya:

> “Upon the brow of Himālaya where the carp was drawn,  
> beside it were drawn the tiger and the bow …” (1–2)

## The Canto of Explanation

The Canto of Explanation is spoken to Kaṇṇaki by Madurāpati, the guardian goddess of Madurai. Here too the excellence of all three dynasties is expressed.

### The Cēras

The poem celebrates these Cēra achievements: Utiyaṉ Cēralātaṉ’s provision of the Great Meal to warriors during the Bhārata war; Palyāṉai Celkeḻu Kuṭṭuvaṉ’s aid to the Brahmin Pālaik Kautamaṉār; Neṭuñcēralātaṉ’s victory in felling the kadamba; and the engraving of the bow upon Himālaya. The verses include:

> “The broad hand of the firm-speared king  
> who provided the Great Meal;  
> the great and sacred seat he gained …” (55–56)

> “Hearing the liberality of the Cēra of the clustering spear …” (62)

> “The strong and tall-speared Cēral  
> who gave a dwelling in heaven  
> to the Brahmin of resonant Tamil …” (63–64)

> “Long live the guardian who felled the kadamba of the sea!  
> Long live the king who carved the bow on the mountain cleft!  
> Long live the Poṟaiyaṉ of the cool, flower-filled Porunai!  
> Long live the king Māntarañ Cēral!” (81–84)

We may next consider the Cōḻas.

### The Cōḻas

The poem records the greatness of two Cōḻas: Cibi, who mounted the balance and cut flesh from his own body for the sake of a dove; and Manu-nīti Cōḻa, who killed his own son and rendered justice to a cow whose calf had been killed. The verse says:

> “The one who entered the balance for a dove,  
> the one who rendered justice to a cow—  
> the king of Pukār, whose fields bloom beside flower-rich waters.” (58–59)

We may next consider the Pāṇḍyas.

### The Pāṇḍyas

During the reign of Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ, it is said that no sound was heard except the sound of the Vedas—not even the sound of the bell of judicial appeal. He was a conqueror who understood the just order of the straight sceptre, not a cruel ruler whom his citizens accused:

> “Apart from the sound of Vedic tongues,  
> he never heard the tongue of the appeal-bell.  
> None but kings who refused to bow at his feet  
> could accuse his sceptre before his people.” (31–34)

### The Concluding Statement

The concluding section of the Canto of Explanation speaks at length of Pāṇḍya excellence. The Pāṇḍya line possessed righteousness, valour, and power. Ancient Madurai grew in refinement. It possessed splendid festivals, a flourishing population, abundance of food, the wealth bestowed by the great Vaigai, the water-richness of unfailing skies and new rain, *vari* dances, *kuravai* dances, and many other forms of prosperity. It produced Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ, who, when his sceptre failed, abandoned the royal throne with his queen, fell dead, and thereby straightened the bent sceptre. The passage says:

> “Among the three crowned kings,  
> those of the broad-armed, weapon-bright Pāṇḍya line  
> possessed righteousness, valour, and power.  
> Their ancient, mighty city grew in excellence;  
> festivals multiplied in splendour;  
> the immortals came there.  
> In their land of unceasing delight  
> the people prospered and food increased;  
> their mighty Vaigai poured forth wealth;  
> the unfailing sky shed new rain.  
> The two dramatic forms, *ārabhaṭi* and *sāttuvati*,  
> the timely *vari* and *kuravai*,  
> and all these together with stores of other wealth  
> appeared in a distinctive and harmonious order.  
> Having crossed the army of the northern Āryas  
> and brought all southern Tamilakam beneath his sight,  
> Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ  
> died upon the royal throne  
> with his queen of faultless chastity …” (1–18)

Thus, by looking upon all three crowned dynasties with equal vision and celebrating each, Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ has made his *Cilappatikāram* worthy of the title “Epic of the Three Crowned Kings.” If such a vision of unity found a place in the present world, the world of suffering might become a world of happiness.

## An Epic of Citizens

Because the epic honours all three crowned kings, it may be called an Epic of the Three Crowned Kings. Yet its hero and heroine, Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki, are ordinary citizens. It may therefore also be singled out as an Epic of Citizens.


---

# 2. A Continuous Poem Containing Literature, Music, Drama, and Narrative

Because *Cilappatikāram* contains the three Tamil components—*iyal* (literature), *icai* (music), and *nāṭakam* (drama)—and because its subject matter, or story, proceeds continuously, the work is specially described as “a continuous poem of literature, music, drama, and narrative substance.”

The very word drama implies a connected story in which literature and music are combined with acting. Tamil is called *muttamil*, “threefold Tamil,” because it consists of literature, music, and drama. Since drama contains all three, drama alone may in a condensed sense be called *muttamil*. Let us nevertheless examine separately the places in *Cilappatikāram* where these three components appear.

## Literature

*Iyal* is the portion that communicates thought without music or acting. The *āciriyam*, *kali*, and *veṇpā* metres in *Cilampu* belong to this literary component. So do the construction of the story; the counsel and moral instruction spoken by Kavunti, the Jain ascetics, Māṭalaṉ, and others; the many emotional modes; principles of royal justice; Pattini worship; the establishment of the three truths; the vindication of justice; descriptive passages; information about countries, cities, and rulers; and the many lessons taught by the work.

For the category called *toṉmai*—“antiquity joined with explanatory prose”—mentioned by Tolkāppiyar, Nacciṉārkkiṉiyar cites *Cilappatikāram* as an example.

## Music

The Canto of the Dance Debut explains the qualifications of the music-master, the percussion master, the flute-master, and the yāḻ-master. Music also appears in Mādhavi’s performance in the moonlit courtyard in the Canto of Summer; the songs of the *Kāṉal Vari*; Kōvalaṉ’s singing with the minstrels in the Canto of the Halt Outside the City; the music of united lovers; the Hunter’s Song, the Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai*, and the Mountain *Kuravai*; Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s musical feast for his warriors in the Canto of the Memorial Stone; and, in the Canto of Benediction, the three women’s *ammāṉai* songs for the Cōḻa, ball songs for the Pāṇḍya, swing songs for the Cēra, and pestle songs praising all three dynasties. These and many other passages constitute the musical component.

## Drama

The very presence of literature and music is already a constituent of drama.

The story of this epic has been performed as street theatre, stage drama, and motion-picture drama. The Canto of the Dance Debut describes the construction of a stage. Its characters are well suited to drama. Kōvalaṉ is the hero; Kaṇṇaki is the heroine. Mādhavi functions as the woman who brings harm to Kaṇṇaki, and the goldsmith as the villain who destroys Kōvalaṉ.

Marriage, love, separation, sorrow, anger, courage, elopement, concubinage, Kavunti’s companionship, Mātari’s refuge, false accusation, murder, Kaṇṇaki’s heroic uprising, the burning of Madurai, the deaths of the Pāṇḍya and his queen, the worship of Kaṇṇaki, the renunciation of some characters, the deaths of others, and the Cēra’s war are all visual elements suited to drama.

The epic also possesses the dramatic flavour of preliminary signs that announce beforehand what is to come. Today this is praised as “direction.”

It is a fine dramatic step to praise in advance the integrity and excellence of rule of the very Pāṇḍya who will later have Kōvalaṉ killed. Other dramatic elements include the construction of many sections as alternating dialogue; repetition of the same line; and the insertion of songs and prose explanations at intervals.

The Canto of the Sea-Bath lists eleven dances: *koṭukoṭṭi*, *pāṇṭaraṅkam*, *alliyattokuti*, *mal*, *tuṭi*, *kuṭai*, *kuṭam*, *pēṭu*, *marakkāl*, *pāvai*, and *kaṭaiyam*.

The Canto of Summer names eight modes of dramatic enactment: *kaṇkūṭu vari*, *kāṇ vari*, *uḷ vari*, *puṟa vari*, *kiḷar vari*, *tērcci vari*, *kāṭci vari*, and *eṭuttukōḷ vari*.

Among the five landscapes, the agricultural *marutam* appears in the Canto in which the City Celebrates Indra’s Festival; the seashore *neytal* in the *Kāṉal Vari*; the arid *pālai* in the Hunter’s Song; the pastoral *mullai* in the Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai*; and the mountain *kuṟiñci* in the Mountain *Kuravai*.

## The Dramatic Acts

Forty years ago I read an English book titled *The Art of Play Writing*. As I read it, I found myself wondering: “*Cilappatikāram* is structured according to these very principles. Could someone perhaps have formulated this grammar after looking at *Cilappatikāram*?” Let that question rest.

Shakespeare’s plays have five acts. On that model, *Cilappatikāram* may be divided into five stages:

1. **Beginning:** From the marriage in the Canto of Auspicious Benediction through the *Kāṉal Vari*.
2. **Development:** From the *Kāṉal Vari* through the Canto of Refuge. One might also say that the turning point begins within this development.
3. **Climax:** The Canto of the Killing Ground, the sixteenth of the epic’s thirty cantos.
4. **Reversal:** From Kaṇṇaki’s transformation and the destruction of Madurai through her journey to the Cēra land.
5. **Conclusion:** From the descent of the gods and Kōvalaṉ to take Kaṇṇaki upward, through Kaṇṇaki worship, the Canto of Benediction, and the Canto of the Granted Boon.

Condensed further, these become three divisions: the beginning is the Pukār Book, the middle is the Madurai Book, and the conclusion is the Vañci Book.

In brief, the epic begins as comedy and ends as tragedy. Its tragic ending opens the ceremonial gateway to the Vañci Book.

## Repetition

Repeating exactly what has already been said is normally regarded as the fault called “repetition of what has been said.” Against this stands the grammatical maxim: “Even when what has been said is repeated, it is no fault if it produces another meaning.” I remember reading in *The Art of Play Writing* that repetition is a dramatic device used to create emphasis, correction, and intensity when emotion rises. *Cilappatikāram* possesses this feature.

Its use in the *Kāṉal Vari* is discussed under another heading. Let us now see how Kaṇṇaki employs it.

When Kaṇṇaki learns that her husband has been killed, she says in the Canto of Circling the City:

> “I shall see my beloved husband—this is one thing.  
> If I see my beloved husband,  
> I shall hear from his mouth words free of evil—this is one thing.  
> If I leave without hearing his faultless good words,  
> the southern king has done what causes unbearable grief—this is one thing.” (19:10–14)

And again:

> “Are there women? Are there women—  
> women who bear the suffering that befalls  
> the husbands they have taken?  
> Are there women? Are there women?  
> Are there worthy people? Are there worthy people—  
> worthy people who take up and raise a child that another bore?  
> Are there worthy people? Are there worthy people?  
> Is there a god? Is there a god  
> in Kūṭal, where the king escaped the edge of the sword?  
> Is there a god? Is there a god?” (51–59)

And:

> “She stood; she thought; tears fell from her long carp-like eyes.  
> She stood; she thought; she did not wipe the tears from her long carp-like eyes.” (73–74)

In the Canto of the Lawsuit, Kaṇṇaki addresses the gatekeeper of the Pāṇḍya palace:

> “Gatekeeper! Gatekeeper!  
> Gatekeeper of the ruler who has failed in kingly justice,  
> his wisdom emptied, his heart devoid of discernment!  
> Announce this:  
> ‘A woman stands at the last gate,  
> holding in her hand one of a matched pair of anklets,  
> a woman who has lost her husband.’  
> Announce it! Announce it!” (20:24–29)

The gatekeeper goes to the Pāṇḍya and reports:

> “A woman stands at the last gate,  
> holding in her hand an anklet of golden workmanship,  
> a woman who has lost her husband—  
> a woman who has lost her husband stands at the last gate.” (20:42–44)

Such repetition gives drama a distinctive beauty of sound. All these are dramatic features. Drama needs beauty of sound as well as beauty of word and meaning, does it not?

Moreover, just as an audience learns the events of a play through the speech of its actors, almost all the story in *Cilampu*, apart from descriptions of nature, is arranged so that we learn it through the speech of the characters.

There is another feature. Modern plays and films sometimes reveal events from the past in the middle or at the end of the drama. *Cilampu* contains the same arrangement. For example, Kōvalaṉ’s youthful acts of generosity, compassion, and courage are disclosed through Māṭalaṉ in the fifteenth canto, the Canto of Refuge, shortly before Kōvalaṉ’s death. These are all excellent dramatic devices.

It is therefore entirely fitting that *Cilappatikāram* should be crowned with the jewelled title: “the continuous epic poem of literature, music, drama, and narrative substance.”


---

# 3. The Twin Epics

It is customary to call two things “twins” when they possess some kind of similarity or connection. Twin children, the Twin Poets, a two-bullock cart, and twin cities such as Hyderabad and Secunderabad are examples.

Accordingly, in Tamil literature the title “the Twin Epics” refers to *Cilappatikāram* and *Maṇimēkalai*. *Cilappatikāram* concerns Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki. *Maṇimēkalai* concerns Maṇimēkalai, the daughter born to Kōvalaṉ and Mādhavi, the woman who captured his heart. Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ composed the former; Cāttaṉār, the grain merchant of Madurai, composed the latter. Let us consider the connections between them.

Kōvalaṉ, Kaṇṇaki, Cittirāpati, Vayantamālai, Mādhavi, and Maṇimēkalai appear as characters in both works.

One is the story of the daughter; the other is the story of her parents. Mādhavi even refers to Kaṇṇaki as a parent or mother of Maṇimēkalai, Mādhavi’s own daughter. This represents an elevated culture. In the Canto in which the Town’s Slander Is Reported in *Maṇimēkalai*, Mādhavi tells Vayantamālai, who has been sent by Cittirāpati:

> “Maṇimēkalai is the daughter  
> of the great Pattini  
> who kindled roaring fire  
> in the guardian king’s great city.” (2:54–55)

This resembles the noble kinship language of the *Rāmāyaṇa*, in which Kaikeyī is called Rāma’s mother and Rāma Kaikeyī’s son, while Kausalyā is called Bharata’s mother and Bharata Kausalyā’s son. The two works may therefore be called a family epic.

Cāttaṉār met Iḷaṅkō, who had accompanied Cēraṉ Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ to view the wealth of the mountain. He reported the killing of Kōvalaṉ, the deaths of the Pāṇḍya and his queen, and the burning of the city. He added that he knew how Madurāpati, the city goddess, had later appeared before Kaṇṇaki and told her the story of the curse from her former birth and that she would see Kōvalaṉ on the fourteenth day. The same news was told to Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ.

Iḷaṅkō immediately said, “Let us compose an epic under the name *Cilappatikāram*.” Cāttaṉār asked Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ himself to compose such a work, common to all three crowned kings, and Aṭikaḷ composed *Cilappatikāram*. Cāttaṉār in turn composed the epic *Maṇimēkalai*.

The end of the prologue to *Cilappatikāram* says:

> “Aṭikaḷ of celebrated speech graciously composed  
> the poem with songs set amid prose,  
> and Cāttaṉ, the grain merchant of Madurai, heard it.” (87–89)

The end of the prologue to *Maṇimēkalai* says:

> “As Prince Iḷaṅkō graciously listened,  
> Cāttaṉ, the prosperous grain merchant,  
> set forth the renunciation of Maṇimēkalai,  
> in the ample power of Tamil,  
> and made it known in thirty songs.” (95–98)

Both passages deserve notice.

Still more significant are the closing lines of the “Statement on the Work” at the end of *Cilappatikāram*:

> “The matter of its discourse,  
> which is fulfilled in *Maṇimēkalai*,  
> is complete;  
> *Cilappatikāram* is complete.” (17–18)

This means that *Cilappatikāram*, whose subject reaches completion when joined in narration with the continuous poem *Maṇimēkalai*, has come to its end.

We may infer from this that Iḷaṅkō and Cāttaṉār discussed their plans and composed the Twin Epics while informing one another of their work.

Many further features correspond in the two epics. Both contain thirty cantos. In each, every canto ends with the terminal word *eṉ*.

Other shared details include: different birds seated like troops in an encampment; the beating of a drum before an elephant; the spirit-square; the story of Mucukunta; the story of Toṭittōṭ Cempiyan who destroyed the suspended forts; Jain and Buddhist affiliations; accounts of many religions; the loss of a husband figured as the loss of the sun; morning scenes; evening scenes; poetic descriptions of sun and moon; seashores and groves; the excellence of countries, cities, and rulers; grief at Maṇimēkalai’s renunciation as though a fine jewel had fallen into the sea; and the declaration that chaste women do not worship another deity. The list would grow if expanded.

Despite all these connections, it may seem surprising that Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ appears to have been affiliated with Jainism while Cāttaṉār was affiliated with Buddhism. Yet today fathers and sons may belong to different political parties; elder and younger brothers may belong to different parties; close friends and relatives may differ politically while still joining one another in many activities. Iḷaṅkō and Cāttaṉār should be understood in the same way.

It is noteworthy that Iḷaṅkō praises Cāttaṉār as “Cāttaṉ, master of cool Tamil” and “Cāttaṉ, poet of the good book.”


---

# 4. Common Ownership of the Divine

The world speaks of common ownership in economics. It may speak of common ownership in many other matters too. But to speak of and practise common ownership in theology and religion is a great revolution. Tirumūlar said, “There is one clan and one God” (2104). Some shout themselves hoarse that, just as all rivers merge in the sea, all religious doctrines end in the idea of one God and therefore religious conflict is unnecessary. But has this principle succeeded?

It is astonishing and painful that every major religion practised in the world contains several, sometimes many, divisions. When the image of one religion’s deity is carried in procession through a street, followers of another religion may shut and bolt their street doors. In the face of that practical reality, what more need be said?

With this background, let us turn to Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ’s *Cilappatikāram*. We may call Iḷaṅkō a great advocate of common ownership. Just as he made the work an epic common to the three crowned kings, he made it an epic common to all deities. Though born in a royal lineage, he embraced renunciation; is this not the highest qualification of one who has renounced? Let us examine, canto by canto, what he says concerning the divine.

## The Prologue

The prologue mentions the Silver Hall in Madurai where Śiva, whose matted hair bears the koṉṟai garland, is enthroned:

> “In the public hall of the matted-haired One crowned with lovely koṉṟai,  
> I lay at midnight in the Silver Hall.” (40–41)

Śiva is said to possess five halls: the Golden Hall, Silver Hall, Ruby Hall, Copper Hall, and Painted Hall. Chidambaram is the Golden Hall; Madurai the Silver Hall; Tiruvalangadu the Ruby Hall; Tirunelveli the Copper Hall; and Courtallam in Tirunelveli district the Painted Hall. One of these five, the Silver Hall of Madurai, is named in the prologue.

## The Canto of Domestic Virtue

Kōvalaṉ praises Kaṇṇaki’s beauty. He says that Murukaṉ of the six faces split his long spear lengthwise and gave the two halves as her eyes. This follows the convention of comparing women’s eyes to spears:

> “Was it not because the six-faced One,  
> though possessing no ordinary mode of birth,  
> has the nature of being seen in two forms,  
> that he split one long, radiant spear  
> and gave it as the two rain-cloud eyes  
> with red outer corners in your face?” (49–52)

The *Cīvakacintāmaṇi*, in the section on Patumaiyār, similarly says that Patumaiyār’s eyes resembled the two portions of Murukaṉ’s sharp spear:

> “She whose eyes were the two halves  
> of Murukaṉ’s uplifted, keen spear.” (126)

Thus Murukaṉ appears in the Canto of Domestic Virtue.

## The Canto in Which the City Celebrates Indra’s Festival

A festival was held for Indra at Kāvirippūmpaṭṭinam. At that time special worship was also conducted in the temples of Śiva and other gods:

> “The temple of the Great One whose body is unborn;  
> the splendid temple of the six-faced red-speared god;  
> the temple of the strong one whose body is white as a bright conch;  
> the temple of the Tall One of blue body;  
> the temple of the king beneath the garlanded white parasol …  
> and, in another quarter, festivals rich and distinct  
> for many different gods.” (169–173, 178)

Iḷaṅkō’s identification of Śiva as “the Great One whose body is unborn” is apt. Texts say that Śiva performed many sacred sports while appearing in human bodies. Yet he was not born to parents like others; he suddenly assumed human form and performed those acts.

Among the ninety-six kinds of minor Tamil literature is *Piḷḷaittamil*, “Tamil on the Divine Child.” Such a work imagines a deity, great person, or benefactor as a small child and composes ten sections of ten poems each, making one hundred poems. *Piḷḷaittamil* works have been sung for many gods. Śiva alone has no such work. Since he was not born as a child, no one composed a *Piḷḷaittamil* on him. In keeping with this tradition, Iḷaṅkō calls Śiva “the Great One whose body is unborn.”

The six-faced red-speared god is Murukaṉ. The strong god whose body is white as a conch is Baladēva. The tall blue-bodied one is Tirumāl. The king beneath the garlanded white parasol is Indra.

Calling Indra alone “king” follows Tolkāppiyar’s tradition. In the *Akattiṇaiyiyal*, Tolkāppiyar names the deities of the mullai, kuṟiñci, marutam, and neytal landscapes:

> “The forest world where Māyōṉ dwells;  
> the dark-mountain world where Cēyōṉ dwells;  
> the sweet-water world where the King dwells;  
> the great-sand world where Varuṇaṉ dwells—  
> these may be spoken of, in the stated order,  
> as mullai, kuṟiñci, marutam, and neytal.” (5)

The first is mullai, belonging to Tirumāl; the second kuṟiñci, belonging to Murukaṉ; the third marutam, belonging to Indra; and the fourth neytal, belonging to Varuṇaṉ.

Why is Indra alone called “king”? The Sanskrit word *Indra* is understood to mean king or leader. Thus Narēndra is leader of people; Surēndra, leader of gods; Rājēndra, king of kings; Gajēndra, leader of elephants; and so on. Tradition holds that there is a celestial world inhabited by many gods, of whom Indra is chief, hence Dēvēndra. Iḷaṅkō therefore follows Tolkāppiyar’s usage in calling him “king.”

## The Canto of Seeing the Country

### The Shrine of the Jewel-Coloured Lord

After separating from Mādhavi, Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki secretly left Pukār before dawn. On their way, they circled the temple of blue-jewel-coloured Tirumāl, who rests in mystic sleep upon the serpent couch:

> “They passed after circling the shrine  
> of the Jewel-Coloured Lord,  
> who reclines in mystic sleep  
> upon the splendid serpent.” (9–10)

### Buddhist Monasteries

Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki also passed, in reverence, the seven Buddhist monasteries built by Indra. There, sky-roaming ascetics came and proclaimed the gracious words of the Buddha beneath the Bodhi tree:

> “They passed the seven monasteries of Indra,  
> where wanderers of the air came and proclaimed  
> the sacred words of the Righteous One  
> beneath the splendid shade of the green-leaved Bodhi,  
> whose five great branches rose high.” (11–14)

### The Platform of Arukaṉ

Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki circled and worshipped the shining stone platform of Arukaṉ, erected by Jain lay devotees:

> “They worshipped and circled the radiant stone platform  
> raised together by the world’s ascetics.” (24–25)

### Names of Arukaṉ

On the road, Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki joined Kavunti Aṭikaḷ, a Jain woman ascetic. Jain wanderers praised Arukaṉ, the Jain deity, by the following names:

> “The Knower, the Righteous One, the One beyond the bounds of knowledge;  
> the Concentrated One, Jina-Indra, the Perfected One, the Blessed One;  
> First of Dharma, Leader, Dharma itself;  
> Substance, Pure One, Ancient One, Poet;  
> Supreme Jina, God, Lord of the auspicious path of liberation;  
> the Highest, the Virtuous, the Light in the world beyond;  
> the Principle, the Saint, the Wanderer, the Cause;  
> the Accomplished One, the Great One, the Noble, the Radiant Light;  
> Lord, Teacher, Possessor of natural virtue, our King;  
> the One of unfailing fame, the great sovereign of virtues;  
> Śaṅkara, Īśa, Self-born, Four-faced;  
> the One who produced the scriptures, Arukaṉ, sage of grace;  
> Master of melody, Possessor of eight qualities, Supreme Reality beyond;  
> Celestial One, First of the Vedas …” (176–189)

So many names are given to Arukaṉ. Among them, “Blessed One,” “Lord,” and “Possessor of eight qualities” also appear in the invocation to God in the *Tirukkuṟaḷ*. The names “the Highest,” “Śaṅkara,” “Īśa,” and “Four-faced” ordinarily designate Śiva and Brahmā. Since Jainism separated from Hinduism, Śiva, Tirumāl, and Brahmā also appear within its vocabulary.

Pavaṇanti, the Jain monk who composed the grammar *Naṉṉūl*, invokes the Four-faced One at the opening of its Book of Letters:

> “Worshipping the Four-faced One  
> seated in the lovely shade  
> of the flower-laden aśoka,  
> I shall clearly expound letters.”

At the opening of the Book of Words he invokes Acyuta, Tirumāl:

> “Worshipping the feet of Acyuta  
> beneath the full-moon triple parasol  
> that shades the three worlds,  
> I shall proclaim words.”

The extant *Naṉṉūl* has no Book of Meaning. Some say Pavaṇanti composed one but it was lost. If that is true, perhaps he invoked Śiva at its beginning.

Among Arukaṉ’s names is also “Lord of the *śivagati*.” *Śivagati* means liberation. It is noteworthy that Jains too use this expression for final release.

Kavunti Aṭikaḷ later praises Arukaṉ by further names: the One who subdued the three; the One who conquered Desire; the One who conquered the five; the One who embraced gracious righteousness; Arukaṉ the righteous; the Knower; the One who walked upon the flower; and the Lord of endless bliss. “The One who walked upon the flower” recalls the phrase “the One who went upon the flower” in the invocation of the *Tirukkuṟaḷ*.

Because *Cilappatikāram* uses names such as Blessed One, Lord, Possessor of eight qualities, the One who walked on the flower, Conqueror of the five, One who subdued the five gateways of sense, and Righteous One or Ocean of righteousness, some claim that Tiruvaḷḷuvar was a Jain. Let that debate rest. Let us call Tiruvaḷḷuvar common to every religion and every faith; let us read the *Tirukkuṟaḷ* as the common scripture.

## The Canto of Seeing the Forest

### Worship of Arukaṉ

At the opening of this section, Arukaṉ is worshipped beneath the aśoka tree:

> “They worshipped the Knower of primordial form,  
> seated in the rich shade of the aśoka  
> whose clusters hang low.” (3–4)

### The Sacred Appearance of Tirumāl

After Kōvalaṉ, Kaṇṇaki, and Kavunti passed Uṟaiyūr and rested in a young grove, a Brahmin from Māṅkāṭu arrived. Kōvalaṉ asked who he was and where he came from. The Brahmin replied:

“My village is Māṅkāṭu, and I am a Brahmin. I worshipped Araṅkaṉ, who rests in mystic sleep on the island in the Kāviri. I also saw and worshipped the sacred form of dark Tirumāl upon Tiruvēṅkaṭam. He wore golden cloth and a jewelled necklace and held discus and white conch in his two hands, like a black cloud bearing lightning and rainbow, while at evening the sun shone on one side and the white moon on the other.” The poem says:

> “I came because my heart was troubled, saying,  
> ‘Show my eyes the form of the Lord of Fortune’s breast  
> reclining on the great island  
> amid the broad-waved Kāviri;  
> and the standing form of the red-eyed Tall One  
> high upon the summit of swelling-watered Vēṅkaṭam,  
> where sun and moon shine on either side;  
> like a good dark cloud wearing lightning as a garment  
> and the radiant rainbow as an ornament;  
> holding in lotus hands  
> the hostile-destroying discus and milk-white conch;  
> wearing upon his chest the beautiful radiant necklace;  
> appearing resplendent in flower-gold cloth.’  
> I am from rain-rich Māṅkāṭu.” (39–53)

Iḷaṅkō’s description of Tirumāl at Tiruvēṅkaṭam recalls parallel passages in other literature. Let us compare them in the method of literary comparison.

In *Maṇimēkalai*, in the Canto in which the goddess Maṇimēkalā appears, Cāttaṉār imagines the maiden Pukār wearing a white earring on one side and a gold earring on the other: the moon shines in the east and the setting sun in the west.

> “Pukār, the flower-creeper surpassing poets,  
> wore the aged moon in the eastern quarter  
> and the falling rays in the western quarter  
> as a white silver earring and a golden earring …”

A similar idea appears in *Paripāṭal*: Tirumāl holds in one hand the discus like the orb of the sun and in the other the white conch like the orb of the white moon:

> “In hands bearing discus and conch,  
> like the two distinct orbs  
> adorning the two skies in their proper season …” (13:7–9)

The phrase “I came because my heart was troubled, saying, ‘Show my eyes’” is especially delightful: the Māṅkāṭu Brahmin’s eyes themselves tormented his mind with the desire to see and worship Tirumāl at Śrīraṅgam and Tiruvēṅkaṭam.

The Brahmin further points out a mountain on the road. One must worship the Tall One, Tirumāl, who dwells upon that lofty mountain, place his sacred feet in one’s heart, and circle the mountain three times:

> “If you worship the Exalted One on the lofty mountain,  
> place his red feet in your thought,  
> and circle the mountain three times …” (105–107)

Tirumāl is called “the Exalted One.” Because he assumed the cosmic Trivikrama form to measure the world, he is traditionally called both the Tall One and the Exalted One. The passage also points to the people’s custom of circling a temple, mountain, or other sacred object three times.

### Koṟṟavai

The three travellers reached a shrine of Koṟṟavai. She is described as possessing an eye in her forehead, as a goddess worshipped by the celestials, as the lady of heaven, and as Aiyai:

> “They reached the shrine of Aiyai—  
> the maiden with an eye upon her brow,  
> the goddess of the celestials,  
> the faultlessly glorious lady of the heavenly land.” (214–216)

## The Hunter’s Song

The Hunter’s Song gives Koṟṟavai many names and attributes:

> “O brow that wears the moon’s white crescent!  
> O coral-mouthed one with bright sword-like teeth,  
> whose sleepless eye opens in the split forehead!  
> O dark-throated one who drank poison!  
> O fierce one who strung a serpent as bowstring  
> and bent the long mountain as a bow!  
> O breasted one girdled with a perforated-fanged serpent!  
> O bearer of the trident in your bangle-wearing hand!  
> O spirit who clothes yourself in elephant hide  
> and wears tiger skin as a girdle!  
> O Koṟṟavai of the victorious sharp sword,  
> whose small feet lament with anklet and warrior’s ring!  
> O maiden standing upon the head  
> of the demon whose two forms met in one body!  
> O Amari, Kumari, Gauri, Samari,  
> Trident-bearer, Nīli, younger sister of Māl,  
> Aiyai, red one, fierce-sworded broad-handed one,  
> maiden of the leaping stag, green-bangled maiden,  
> maiden of the subtle arts, maiden of rare beauty!” (54–71)

The crescent-bearing brow, forehead eye, poison-darkened throat, bending of Mount Mēru as a bow, trident, elephant hide, and tiger skin are all Śiva’s attributes. Their application to Koṟṟavai suggests that Koṟṟavai is identified with Śivai, Umā, the consort of Śiva. It is astonishing that Iḷaṅkō remembered and supplied so many names.

### Koṉṟai and Basil

Koṟṟavai is said to have worn a garland in which koṉṟai flowers and holy basil were woven together and to have danced in maiden form, protecting the gods while the demons perished:

> “Wearing upon her shoulders  
> a dense flower-garland woven together  
> of koṉṟai and basil,  
> she danced in maiden form  
> while demons withered and gods were saved.” (Song 10)

Koṉṟai belongs to Śiva and basil to Tirumāl. By joining them in one garland, the poem expresses a common vision free of Śaiva–Vaiṣṇava division. The popular saying shines here: “Hari and Śiva are one; dust fills the mouth of one who does not know it.”

The same view appears at the end of the Hunter’s Song. Koṟṟavai is said to have drunk the poison no one else would consume and granted grace to the gods. She is also said to have broken the twin maruta trees created through the plot of her maternal uncle Kaṃsa and to have kicked and destroyed the rolling cart sent by him:

> “You drank the uneaten poison and remained, granting grace.” (22:1)
>
> “Walking between the maruta trees,  
> kick down in grace the rolling cart  
> contrived by your maternal uncle.” (22:3–4)

Śiva drank the poison, but the deed is here attributed to Koṟṟavai. Breaking the maruta trees and kicking the cart belong to Kaṇṇaṉ, the divine incarnation of Tirumāl, yet these too are transferred to Koṟṟavai. Once again we see a common vision without Śaiva–Vaiṣṇava difference.

For comparison, consider a few statements from other works.

### The *Kampa Rāmāyaṇam*

Śiva and Tirumāl, the One appearing as two, each possessed a bow. To discover whose bow was stronger, the Four-faced Brahmā caused them to fight. The story appears in the Paraśurāma section of the Book of Childhood:

> “The Four-faced One set the two—  
> who are the One primordial source  
> higher than all—upon their fierce, strong bows  
> and stirred up a crowded battle.” (28)

We may also compare two verses of Pūtat Āḻvār’s *Third Tiruvantāti* in the *Divya Prabandham*:

> “His names are Hara and Nārāyaṇa;  
> his mounts are bull and bird;  
> his scriptures are revelation; his temples mountain and water;  
> his works destruction and protection;  
> his weapons spear and discus;  
> his forms fire and dark cloud—yet he is one.” (5)

> “The holy One of the golden body and braided matted hair,  
> and the Tall Māl who once measured the world—  
> though they ever move as two bodies,  
> each is forever present within the body of the other.” (98)

## The Canto of Taking the Stone

### On Crown and Shoulder

When Cēraṉ Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ set out on his northern campaign, he placed upon his crown the garland of sacred feet given at Śiva’s temple. Later he wore upon his shoulder the garland given at Tirumāl’s temple:

> “As some stood praising with the remnant offering  
> of the One who reclines in mystic sleep  
> in the golden-roofed hall,  
> the king had already placed upon his jewelled crown  
> the beautiful red feet of the red-matted god  
> who concealed the clear water.  
> Therefore he received the other offering  
> and bore it upon his handsome, jewelled shoulder  
> as he proceeded in majesty.” (62–67)

The passage shows that both Śiva and Tirumāl had temples and that Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, having first placed the Śiva temple’s gift upon his crown, wore Tirumāl’s gift upon his shoulder. It is therefore clear that he did not practise religious discrimination.

The Hunter’s Song of this work explains the glory of Koṟṟavai; the Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai* the excellence of Tirumāl; and the Mountain *Kuravai* the greatness of Murukaṉ.

Thus *Cilappatikāram* stands as a common temple of words in which every deity is enthroned.

## Religions within the Family

After the deaths of Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki, Mādhavi and Maṇimēkalai embraced Buddhism. Of the fathers of the pair, Kōvalaṉ’s father Mācāttuvāṉ became a Buddhist, while Kaṇṇaki’s father Māṉāykaṉ became a Jain. Religion therefore did not destroy kinship. Even today one may see one family among relatives following Śaivism and another Vaiṣṇavism.

Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ worked very carefully without allowing himself to be trapped in sectarianism. Cāttaṉār, author of *Maṇimēkalai*, openly propagated Buddhism. If asked which religion Iḷaṅkō gave first place to, one may answer Jainism: he propagated it through the Jain wanderers and Kavunti. He is therefore personally regarded as Jain-affiliated. His elder brother Cēraṉ Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, however, is described in several places as Śaiva-affiliated, though he too gave space to every religion.

Cāttaṉār was a private individual and could therefore propagate Buddhism openly. Iḷaṅkō belonged to the royal family and was the younger brother of the reigning Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ. He therefore left among the people a principle of religious common ownership.


---

# 5. The Structural Design of the Epic

Definitions have been offered of what elements make a work an epic, what makes a minor epic, and what makes a great epic. Different people may define these matters differently; no one can force a single definition upon everyone. It is enough if most of the indispensable major structures are present. On this basis, let us examine some elements of epic construction in *Cilappatikāram*.

## Benediction

“Let us praise the moon; let us praise the sun; let us praise the great rain; let us praise flower-rich Pukār” may be treated like an invocation and salutation to the divine. Because this section begins auspiciously, it is titled the Canto of Auspicious Benediction.

## Announcement of What Is to Come

The declarations that righteousness becomes Death to rulers who fail in justice, that the great praise the celebrated Pattini, and that past deeds ripen and compel their fruit announce what is to come. The prologue’s naming of the thirty cantos that follow serves the same purpose.

## The Four Aims

All four—righteousness, wealth, pleasure, and liberation—appear in the work. The teachings of Kavunti, Māṭalaṉ, the Jain wanderers, and others represent righteousness. Kōvalaṉ’s attempt to seek wealth, the Pāṇḍya’s governance, and Cēra military victories belong to wealth and polity. The joy of Kaṇṇaki and Kōvalaṉ, the festivities of Mādhavi and Kōvalaṉ, and the townspeople’s happy recreation belong to pleasure. Kaṇṇaki and Kōvalaṉ’s ascent to the higher world, the Cēra’s sacrifice, and worship at Kaṇṇaki’s shrine belong to liberation.

## Peerless Hero and Heroine

Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki, born in great merchant families, are peerless epic protagonists.

## Descriptions of Mountain, Sea, Country, and City

The taking of the stone from Himālaya and the Mountain *Kuravai* provide mountain settings. The Canto of the Sea-Bath, in which the people of Pukār bathe in the sea, concerns the ocean. Descriptions of the Cōḻa and Pāṇḍya lands, Madurai, and the Cēra realm supply country and city descriptions.

## Seasons and Times

The arrival of summer in the Canto of Summer represents the major season. The Canto Celebrating the Evening represents a minor division of time.

## The Two Luminaries

The moon drives away the mischievous evening and rules among the stars. In the Canto of Seeing the City, the sun rises as birds call and lotuses open, awakening Madurai. In the Canto in which the City Celebrates Indra’s Festival, the sun rises from the eastern mountain and removes Mother Earth’s dark covering with its hands of rays. At Vēṅkaṭam in the evening, the moon appears in the east and the sun in the west.

## Marriage, Coronation, Groves, and Water-Play

The marriage is that of Kaṇṇaki and Kōvalaṉ. After the death of Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ, conqueror of the northern army, his younger brother Veṟṟivēl Ceḻiyaṉ is crowned.

The *Kāṉal Vari* shows the people of Pukār enjoying the grove. During Indra’s festival, they play in the water in the Canto of the Sea-Bath.

## Drinking, Children, Quarrel, and Union

The citizens of Pukār rejoice in drink. Maṇimēkalai is born to Kōvalaṉ and Mādhavi. Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ appears as a child to comfort Mālati, and a naming festival is held for Maṇimēkalai.

Kōvalaṉ and Mādhavi, the Pāṇḍya and his queen, and the citizens of Pukār experience lovers’ quarrels and later union.

## Counsel, Embassy, March, and Victory

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ holds council. Mādhavi sends Vayantamālai and Kōcikaṉ as messengers to Kōvalaṉ. Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ marches north and wins victory. The internal division into thirty cantos is itself an epic structural feature.

## Emotional Modes

The eight embodied emotional flavours named in *Tolkāppiyam*—laughter, sorrow, abasement, wonder, fear, pride, anger, and delight—are all present in *Cilappatikāram*.

### Laughter

Separated from her husband, Kaṇṇaki gives a false smile to gladden her father-in-law and mother-in-law. When her husband returns from Mādhavi, she welcomes him with a smile. When Cāliṉi praises her, Kaṇṇaki grows shy and smiles.

After Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki have travelled only one *kāvatam* from Pukār, Kaṇṇaki’s feet hurt. Sighing, she gently shows her teeth in a smile and asks, “Where is ancient Madurai? How much farther is it?” Kōvalaṉ laughs and replies that Madurai is nearby. Tolkāppiyar says laughter arises from ridicule, youth, innocence, or folly. These smiles may be attributed to Kaṇṇaki’s innocence.

Elsewhere the work tells how some northern kings, afraid of Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, escaped in various disguises. That passage is highly comic and may be classified as laughter arising from ridicule.

### Sorrow

The lamentations of Kaṇṇaki, Mālati, and others represent sorrow.

### Abasement

A man and woman who mocked Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki are transformed into jackals and suffer degradation.

### Wonder

The Jain wanderers’ arrival through the sky, Kavunti’s curse turning the mockers into jackals, Kaṇṇaki’s wrenching off her left breast and burning Madurai, and knowledge of former and later births all produce wonder.

### Fear

Fear appears when an elephant seizes an aged Brahmin with its trunk; when Mātari and others see evil omens; and when Kōvalaṉ is accused by the state of theft and murder.

### Pride or Heroic Exultation

Kōvalaṉ subdues the elephant and shines as a warrior of compassion and a benefactor of the poor. Kōvalaṉ and Mādhavi possess skill in playing the yāḻ; Mādhavi excels in dance and song; Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ wins in the north.

### Anger

Kaṇṇaki’s wrath destroys the Pāṇḍya, his queen, and Madurai. Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s wrath makes Kanaka and Vijaya bear the stone. Veṟṟivēl Ceḻiyaṉ imposes the death penalty upon the goldsmiths.

### Delight

Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki enjoy domestic virtue. Through the splendour of his wealth, Kōvalaṉ joins Mādhavi and experiences pleasure. The people rejoice in sea-bathing and recreation in groves.

In addition, the epic contains the three crowned kings, doctrines of several religions, temples of many gods, numerous public halls, many natural scenes, commerce, many performances of dance and song, the Hunter’s Song, the Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai*, and the Mountain *Kuravai*. By all these features, *Cilappatikāram* unquestionably deserves the great name of a structurally complete great epic.


---

# 6. The Working of Past Deeds

## The Power of Fate

*Cilappatikāram* advances with the assistance of *ūḻviṉai*, the ripening force of past action. Most people in our country possess great faith in it. Hinduism, Jainism, and Buddhism all emphasise it. In the *Tirukkuṟaḷ*, Tiruvaḷḷuvar devotes ten couplets to a chapter titled “Fate,” and may be said to instil belief in the power of past deeds.

However much people plan and however carefully they attempt an action, events proceed according to the strength of fate; nothing is stronger than fate. One couplet alone is enough:

> “What power is greater than fate?  
> Whatever else one plans,  
> fate arrives first.” (380)

Yet Vaḷḷuvar also says that people who work continuously and energetically without weariness may push fate aside and defeat it:

> “Those who labour without wavering  
> and never sink in effort  
> will see even fate turn its back.” (620)

The particle “even” in “even fate” marks exceptional greatness. Saying that they conquer even unconquerable fate implies that fate is ordinarily unconquerable.

## What Is Fate?

What is *ūḻ*? The usual explanation is this: if one did good in a former birth, good happens in the present birth; if one did evil in a former birth, evil happens now. Likewise, if one does good in this birth, good will happen in the next; if one does evil now, evil will happen in the next.

## How Fate Was “Discovered”

How can anyone know in this birth whether one did good or evil in a former birth? Similarly, how can anyone know now whether good or evil will happen in the next birth?

The ancients reflected for many days and years: a person who does good in this life suffers, while one who does evil prospers—why? They found no properly fitting answer. With no alternative, they reached an inference: one who receives good now may have done good in a former birth; one who suffers now may have done evil then. Likewise, a bad person who now receives good will suffer in a later birth, while a good person who now suffers will receive good later. It was a conjecture, an estimate, an assumption. They then crowned such circumstances with the name “fate” and placed the burden upon people generation after generation.

## Tiruvaḷḷuvar’s Difficulty

Even Tiruvaḷḷuvar struggles at one point. Since the prosperity of a malicious, envious person and the ruin of an upright person free from envy contradict the expected moral order, he says the situation must be pondered:

> “The prosperity of the envious-hearted  
> and the ruin of the upright  
> are matters to be examined.” (169)

It was on this basis that the ancients invented something nonexistent and ill-fitting, called it fate, and loaded it upon the heads of succeeding generations.

## A More Fitting Explanation

Within a single lifetime, one who does good earlier may receive good later; one who does evil earlier may receive evil later. The name *ūḻviṉai* may properly be applied to good and bad actions done earlier and yielding results later in the same life.

Auvaiyār says in *Koṉṟaivēntaṉ*, “What is done in the forenoon ripens in the afternoon” (74). Tiruvaḷḷuvar similarly says that if one harms another in the morning, harm will come of itself in the afternoon without anyone deliberately causing it:

> “If one causes pain to another in the morning,  
> pain will come of itself to oneself in the afternoon.”

He does not merely connect the earlier and later parts of one lifetime, but the morning and afternoon of a single day. This may be called a genuine and very great kind of fate.

Thus the earlier and later portions of a life, or even the morning and afternoon of one day, provide one reasonable solution.

There is another. Whether good or bad happens to good people, or bad or good to evil people, events may occur naturally, accidentally, and unexpectedly. Death in a traffic accident, or loss of property or life through cyclone, flood, earthquake, volcano, war, or murder, are harms that may occur naturally or accidentally. Inheriting the property of a childless person or suddenly becoming rich through a lottery are benefits that may likewise arrive by chance. To bring in a separate force called fate and connect it with these events is like tying a knot between a bald head and a knee—an artificial and unfitting connection.

With this foundation, let us turn to *Cilappatikāram*.

The epic emphasises past deeds in many places and even presents a story of a former birth. Yet, viewed by scientific analysis, the events appear to be consequences arising later from what was done earlier in the same day or life, or else natural and accidental occurrences. Let us enter the text and examine them one by one.

## The Prologue

The prologue itself refers to fate. Because the time had come for Kōvalaṉ’s deed to ripen, the Pāṇḍya ordered his death without inquiry:

> “Because it was the time when the deed would bear fruit,  
> the neem-garlanded king failed to discern anything.” (27–28)

Three truths are said to become known through *Cilappatikāram*: righteousness becomes Death to rulers who fail in governance; the great praise the renowned Pattini; and past action ripens, comes, and forces its fruit:

> “Righteousness becomes Death to those who fail in rule;  
> the great praise the Pattini of celebrated speech;  
> and past action ripens, comes, and compels its fruit …” (55–57)

## The *Kāṉal Vari*

Kōvalaṉ suspects that Mādhavi’s yāḻ song contains a deceptive double meaning. His fate joins the suspicion, and he releases his embrace of the woman whose face is like the full moon:

> “He placed his thought upon the yāḻ music;  
> because his fate came and ripened,  
> his embracing hands loosened  
> from the woman whose face was the full moon.” (52:4–5)

## The Canto in Which the Dream Is Told

Tēvanti, Kaṇṇaki’s Brahmin friend, tells her that in a former birth she failed to observe a vow connected with her husband:

> “You are not abandoned by destiny;  
> in a former birth you broke a vow for your husband—  
> may that fault now perish.” (55–56)

The old force of past deeds is also said to bring matters together so that, before sunrise, Kōvalaṉ decides at dawn to leave Pukār with Kaṇṇaki:

> “Old action brought the end together;  
> he resolved upon the journey in the night  
> before the dense rays spread their feet.”

These are the closing words of the final *veṇpā*.

## The Canto of Seeing the Country

Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki set out toward Madurai because fate drove their minds:

> “Fate pressed them onward; their minds urged them forth.” (4)

While they rest in a grove, a Jain wanderer appears. Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki, together with Kavunti, fall at the ascetic’s feet and pray that their ancient deeds be destroyed:

> “When the wanderers who proclaim dharma appeared,  
> they fell at their feet with discerning Kavunti, saying,  
> ‘May our ancient old deeds be shattered.’” (163–165)

One wanderer tells Kavunti that when fate has ripened and arrived, no one can dismiss it merely by saying, “Go away.” Like a planted seed that sprouts, matures, and yields fruit, action must bear its result:

> “See, Kavunti of surpassing excellence:  
> harsh action will not depart when told ‘depart’;  
> like a seed once sown, it comes to meet us,  
> and when the time of attachment arrives  
> it cannot be cast aside.” (170–173)

The *Tirukkuṟaḷ* similarly says that things not allotted by good destiny will not remain however painfully guarded, while things allotted will not leave even if poured away:

> “What is not one’s portion will not become one’s own though guarded;  
> what is one’s portion will not depart though carried out and poured away.” (376)

A *Nālaṭiyār* verse offers another comparison. When rain does not fall, no one can command it to fall; when it pours abundantly, no one can command it to stop. Likewise, one cannot prevent fate from bringing harm or turn away the good it grants:

> “What must be suffered cannot be removed from those who must suffer;  
> what must be received is likewise fixed.  
> When the rain fails, none can make it fall;  
> when it is abundant, none can restrain it.” (104)

## The Canto of Seeing the City

Kavunti tells Kōvalaṉ that the ignorant fail to accept and follow the instruction of the great. When evil action ripens and arrives, they suffer greatly because of their ignorance:

> “People empty of learning do not accept the natural order;  
> when evil, painful action ripens,  
> they suffer greatly with folly as the cause.” (30–32)

She further asks whether it was not harsh fate that made Naḷa, ruined by gambling, leave his wife in the deep forest at midnight:

> “Was it not harsh action  
> that made him abandon the jewelled woman  
> in the forest wilderness  
> during the middle watch of night?” (54–56)

While Kavunti, Kōvalaṉ, and Kaṇṇaki stay outside Madurai, the Brahmin Māṭalaṉ arrives. Praising Kōvalaṉ, he says: “As far as I know, you have performed good righteous deeds in this birth. The reason you have come here with Kaṇṇaki to suffer in isolation may be an action from a former birth.”

> “I know the good deeds you have done in this life.  
> Is it the fruit of a former life  
> that you have come here to suffer alone  
> with this noble shoot of a great jewel?” (91–93)

## The Canto of the Killing Ground

Before Kōvalaṉ leaves for the marketplace to sell the anklet, he speaks emotionally to Kaṇṇaki:

“Kaṇṇaki, is the cause of our suffering some enchantment practised by another, or our own powerful old fate? My mind is confused; I understand nothing.”

> “Is it sorcery? Is it harsh fate?  
> My mind is confused; I know nothing.” (61–62)

The goldsmith tells the Pāṇḍya that the thief who stole the anklet has been caught. Because it is the time for the old evil deeds of both Kōvalaṉ and the Pāṇḍya to bear fruit, the king orders the “thief” killed. Kōvalaṉ, already caught in the net of matured evil action, is slain:

> “Because it was the time when the deed would bear fruit,  
> the neem-garlanded king failed to discern anything.” (148–149)

> “He approached Kōvalaṉ,  
> who stood caught in the mature net of evil action.” (156–157)

Because Kōvalaṉ’s old evil action ripens, the Pāṇḍya’s straight sceptre bends into tyranny and Kōvalaṉ falls cut down:

> “The guardian’s straight sceptre bent, and Kōvalaṉ fell,  
> as his ancient fate ripened.” (216–217)

The canto ends with this *veṇpā*:

> “Both kinds of action will reach you;  
> therefore approach only good righteousness.  
> Through Kaṇṇaki’s husband, the sceptre that never bent on earth  
> was bent by an act returning as the fruit of the past.”

Because the Pāṇḍya possessed old evil action, his sceptre bent through Kōvalaṉ. The poet therefore advises the people of the world: both good and evil actions come to us; always perform good and righteous deeds.

## The Canto of Explanation

Madurāpati, guardian goddess of Madurai, tells Kaṇṇaki that once old action begins to ripen and act, present austerity cannot help those without rectitude:

> “When the action from your former birth comes and ripens,  
> austerity performed now cannot help the unrighteous.” (171–172)

## The Mountain *Kuravai*

After Madurai and the Pāṇḍya are destroyed, Kaṇṇaki reaches Neṭuvēḷ Hill. When the mountain people ask who she is, she replies that harsh old fate ripened, destroying Madurai and its king, and that she is the woman of cruel destiny who lost her husband there:

> “When harsh action came and ripened,  
> bringing ruin to fragrant Madurai and its king,  
> I lost my husband there and came away—  
> I am a woman of cruel fate.” (5–6)

## The Canto of the Water Consecration

The Brahmin Māṭalaṉ tells Cēraṉ Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, who has returned with the stone borne on the heads of Kanaka and Vijaya, the story of Kōvalaṉ. At the shore of Pukār, he says, old evil action ripened; Kōvalaṉ found a contrary meaning in Mādhavi’s *vari* song, separated from her, and returned to Kaṇṇaki:

> “During the lovers’ quarrel, as fate rose and ripened  
> through the modes sung by the maiden Mādhavi,  
> he separated without reunion,  
> and with the creeper of his clan  
> entered ancient, towered Madurai …” (58–61)

## The Canto of the Memorial Stone

When Māṭalaṉ urges Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ to perform a righteous sacrifice, he says that the soul travels according to the path of deeds already performed; this is the lofty doctrine spoken by those whose vision is true:

> “The soul travels along the path of the deed it has done—  
> this is the doctrine of those whose vision does not lie.” (167–168)

This may be compared with the *Puṟanāṉūṟu*:

> “As a raft follows the course of water,  
> precious life follows its appointed course—  
> so we have understood from the vision of the discerning.” (192:9–11)

## The Canto of the Granted Boon

Mālati nurses the child of a co-wife. When an old evil deed ripens, Death takes the infant’s life:

> “Mālati nursed the child of a co-wife;  
> an old deed ripened, and Death took the child’s life;  
> she grieved for the infant.” (74–76)

Thus *ūḻviṉai* is mentioned in more than about twenty places in *Cilappatikāram*.

In the Canto of Explanation, Madurāpati is said to tell Kaṇṇaki the story of Kōvalaṉ’s former birth. In Kaliṅga, Vasu ruled Ciṅkapuram and Kumaraṉ ruled Kapilapuram. They were enemies and tried to defeat each other. No one moved within a six-*kātam* zone between their territories.

A jewel merchant named Caṅkamaṉ secretly travelled with his wife Nīli from Kapilapuram to Ciṅkapuram and traded in gems. Parataṉ, a servant of the king of Ciṅkapuram, falsely denounced Caṅkamaṉ as a spy for the enemy and caused the king to kill him.

Nīli, wife of the dead Caṅkamaṉ, suffered greatly. On the fourteenth day she climbed a mountain and cursed: “May the man responsible for my husband’s death be falsely accused and killed in his next birth. May his wife attain the state I have attained.” She then died.

That Parataṉ was reborn as Kōvalaṉ. In accordance with Nīli’s curse, he was falsely accused and killed. His wife Kaṇṇaki ended her life on a mountain summit on the fourteenth day. This is familiar to all.

The tale appears to be a constructed story intended to make us believe in past action and its power.

## Performances with the Sounding Anklet

Only because past action ripened and compelled its fruit did the renowned Pattini come to be praised by the great. Although many temples of Kaṇṇaki in Tamil Nadu have been converted into temples of Draupadī or other goddesses, priests in goddess temples still follow an old custom: they hold anklets in both hands, make them sound, and sing narrative songs in performance. The growth of this art in Tamil Nadu is connected with Kaṇṇaki’s anklet. Is this too a fruit of fate ripening and compelling its result?


---

# 7. Dreams

*Cilappatikāram* contains a canto titled “The Canto in Which the Dream Is Told.” Many people have expressed many views about dreams. A writer named Poṉṉavaṉ even composed a work titled *Kaṉā Nūl* (“Book of Dreams”), consisting of thirty *kaṭṭaḷaik kalittuṟai* verses linked in an *antāti*. Some common views about dreams are these:

- While a person sleeps, the body remains on the bed but the life or soul leaves, performs various acts, then returns and rejoins the body.
- A person’s thoughts or desires blossom as dreams during sleep.
- Events and sensations previously experienced through the five senses reappear in dreams with many transformations.
- A good dream is followed by good; a bad dream by bad.
- A dream seen at dawn comes true exactly—*Cīvakacintāmaṇi* 219.
- Dreams have no connection with events that later occur in waking life.
- A dream is a sign that announces during sleep what will happen later in the day.
- Thinkers such as Plato held that dreams reveal human likes and dislikes.
- Thinkers such as Aristotle held that dreams arise because certain internal organs remain active and stimulate the sleeper.

Although many people have offered different theories, each person may use the dreams he or she has experienced—that is, personal experience—to infer which view is correct.

With this foundation, let us turn to *Cilappatikāram* and examine its three major dream episodes.

## 1. The Canto in Which the Dream Is Told

While living in Pukār, Kaṇṇaki tells her Brahmin friend Tēvanti of a dream:

“My heart is troubled by the dream I saw. My husband took my hand and led me somewhere. At last we entered a great city. There, people placed upon us a statement that should never have been made; they laid upon me a false and cruel accusation like a scorpion. I heard many say that some evil had befallen my husband Kōvalaṉ. Unable to bear it, I went before the ruler of that city and argued my case. As a result, harm came to the ruler and to the city. The harm was so terrible that I have no wish to tell you more. Later my husband and I attained an exalted state. If I tell you that, it will provoke laughter.”

The poem says:

> “My heart is troubled by a dream.  
> He took my hand, and we entered a great city.  
> In the city we reached, the townspeople placed upon us  
> a word that should never be uttered,  
> setting upon me a scorpion-like charge.  
> I heard that some evil had befallen Kōvalaṉ.  
> I went before the guardian and stated the case.  
> Then harm came to the guardian and the city.  
> It seems a fiery evil, O close-bangled one;  
> if you hear of the good state later attained  
> by me and the man joined to me,  
> it will become a cause for laughter.” (45–54)

To “set a scorpion” upon someone means to impose cruelty like placing a scorpion on the body. Some people may throw a false scorpion made of wax or another material upon a person to frighten them. Here “scorpion” means a cruel accusation that brings pain like a scorpion’s sting.

In ordinary life, one who sees a bad dream may be ashamed or afraid to reveal it. People are often unwilling to report a dream in which harm has befallen another.

The dream foreshadows the false theft charge placed upon Kōvalaṉ as though a scorpion were set upon him. The “fiery dream” indicates the coming destruction of the Pāṇḍya and his queen. Kaṇṇaki’s mind is unwilling to speak of it.

The meaning of “If you hear of the good state, it will cause laughter” is that Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki will finally be welcomed by the gods and ascend to the higher world. Because such a blessing is difficult to obtain, Kaṇṇaki fears that if she claims it for herself, people may mock her: “Ah! Do these two possess such immense ambition?”

After Kōvalaṉ is killed and Kaṇṇaki falls into great grief, she remembers this dream and thinks events have unfolded according to it:

> “‘I shall see the evil king and ask him this,’ she said.  
> She rose; she remembered the distressing evil dream.  
> She stood; she thought; tears fell from her long carp-like eyes.  
> She stood; she thought.” (*Ūrcūḻ Vari*, 71–74)

Did Kaṇṇaki truly see such a dream? Or did Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ invent it for epic effect, as foreshadowing? The second is probably true. Can anyone dream all these details beforehand without omission, as though they had been dictated in advance?

## 2. The Canto of Refuge

After Kōvalaṉ has walked around Madurai and returned to the settlement outside the city, he tells the Brahmin Māṭalaṉ of a dream:

“I dreamed as vividly as waking that, through a base little man, in the guarded king’s city, Kaṇṇaki suffered trembling grief; that my garment was stripped from me and I rode upon a pig; that Kaṇṇaki and I attained the state of those who have severed attachment; that Mādhavi placed Maṇimēkalai before the Righteous One, while the god of Love threw down his flower-arrows upon empty ground and stood helpless in grief.”

> “Kōvalaṉ said: ‘Through a base little man,  
> in the guarded king’s fortified city,  
> the woman of fragrant fivefold hair suffered trembling grief;  
> my garment was taken, and I rode a tusked beast;  
> with the jewelled woman of lovely curling hair  
> I attained the state of those who have severed attachment;  
> the god of Love threw his great flower-arrows  
> upon empty ground and stood helpless in grief;  
> before the Righteous One beneath the splendid Bodhi,  
> Mādhavi gave Maṇimēkalai.  
> At midnight I saw this dream as though awake.
