> It will swiftly happen here.’” (95–106)

The “base little man” is the goldsmith; the fortified city is Madurai; “fragrant fivefold hair” refers to Kaṇṇaki; the tusked beast is a pig. Attaining with the jewelled woman the state of those who have severed attachment means reaching with Kaṇṇaki the higher world attained by ascetics without attachment. The Love-god becomes helpless because Maṇimēkalai can no longer be used for erotic pursuit. The Righteous One beneath the Bodhi is the Buddha.

The passage recalls an old worldly practice: a person guilty of a grave crime was shaved, marked on the face with black and red spots, insufficiently clothed, mounted upon a donkey, and paraded through the town. Here a pig replaces the donkey. The image signifies that Kōvalaṉ will be subjected to cruel humiliation; Kaṇṇaki will thereby suffer; and Mādhavi will make Maṇimēkalai renounce the world.

At the end Kōvalaṉ says, “It will swiftly happen here”—that is, in Madurai. This rests upon the belief that events follow dreams. Kōvalaṉ may have had a dream with some relation to these events; or this too may be Iḷaṅkō’s invention for the flavour of epic foreshadowing.

## 3. The Canto of the Lawsuit

On the night before Kōvalaṉ is killed, the Pāṇḍya queen Kōpperuntēvi tells her companion of an evil dream:

“Friend, the king’s straight sceptre and white parasol fell to the ground. The bell of appeal was struck. The eight directions trembled. Darkness swallowed the sun. A rainbow appeared at night. Stars fell in broad daylight. I saw these things in a dream. I shall report the dream to the king.”

> “There … I shall see the parasol and sceptre fall;  
> I shall hear the sweet-voiced bell at the gate tremble—see, friend!  
> All eight directions will shake;  
> darkness will swallow the sun—see, friend!  
> At night the streaming bow-banner will appear;  
> in burning daylight fierce-rayed stars will fall—  
> I shall see these things, friend!” (1–7)

“Friend” renders *ellā*. The gate-bell is the bell of judicial appeal hung at the palace entrance for petitioners. *Ira* means night. “The bow-banner streams” means that the rainbow, Indra’s bow, appears like a many-coloured banner. Repeating “I shall see—see, friend!” three times gives literary and epic relish.

The queen then tells the Pāṇḍya the same dream:

> “The straight sceptre and white parasol  
> overturn and fall upon the dense earth;  
> at our king’s victorious gate  
> the bell trembles, and my heart trembles;  
> at night the bow appears, by day stars fall,  
> and the four directions shake.  
> Some suffering is coming—  
> let us tell the king.” (9–12)

The same information is stated twice in song: once for the companion to hear, and again for the king and others.

The sceptre and parasol do not merely fall as though lying down; they overturn headfirst. The appeal-bell had always hung at the palace gate but had never been struck because there was no defect in the king’s government. In the dream its sound is heard. The text does not merely say the bell moved and sounded: it “trembled,” making the heart tremble. The wording is full of meaning.

It is easy to explain “a rainbow at night” today. A rainbow normally appears in daylight. In the morning, if the sun shines in the east while rain falls opposite it in the west, sunlight passes through the rain and is dispersed, producing a curved many-coloured form still farther west. In the afternoon, when the sun is in the west and rain falls opposite it in the east, the same dispersion produces a rainbow in the east.

Thus a rainbow normally appears in daylight. Even if it rains at night, there is no sunlight to produce one. A dream of a rainbow at night was therefore treated as a sign of approaching sorrow.

Other literary works too present an evil dream seen before a king’s death as a sign of that death. By the method of literary comparison, let us consider some examples, beginning with the *Kampa Rāmāyaṇam*.

### Trijaṭā’s Dream in the *Kampa Rāmāyaṇam*

Trijaṭā tells Sītā the evil dreams she has seen:

“Mother, listen. Rāvaṇa’s head was slick with oil. Wearing red clothing, he rode southward toward the land of Yama in a chariot yoked to donkeys and ghosts. Rāvaṇa’s sons, relatives, and others also went south and did not return. The sacrificial fire he maintained went out, and termites swarmed over the place. An untrimmed jewelled lamp suddenly blazed with immense light. Thunder split Rāvaṇa’s palace.

“Listen further. Not only male elephants but female elephants too entered must. Drums sounded by themselves though no one struck them. The sky thundered explosively though there were no clouds. Stars fell. The sun seemed to shine in a night that had no daylight. The celestial garlands worn by men gave off the smell of flesh. Festive arches broke. Elephants’ tusks snapped. Water in full ceremonial pots foamed like liquor. Stars rose after splitting the moon. The sky rained blood. The mace, discus, sword, and bow began fighting one another.

“The marriage pendants of women broke by themselves and fell upon their breasts though no one touched them. The hair of Maṇṭōtari, daughter of Maya and wife of Rāvaṇa, came loose. Two lions descended from a mountain with a host of tigers, entered the forest where elephants lived, encircled them, and crushed them to death. A peacock in the forest left the forest and went away.” The two lions signify Rāma and Lakṣmaṇa; the mountain tigers are the monkey armies; the elephants are the rākṣasas; the elephant-forest is Laṅkā; and the peacock is Sītā.

“Listen further. Lakṣmī carried a sacred lamp shining with the light of a thousand lamps from Rāvaṇa’s house to Vibhīṣaṇa’s house.” The implication is that Rāvaṇa will perish and Vibhīṣaṇa will come to power.

This appears in the Canto of the Vision in the Sundara Kāṇḍa of the *Kampa Rāmāyaṇam* (40–53).

### The *Cīvakacintāmaṇi*

A similar sign appears in the *Cīvakacintāmaṇi*. While pregnant with Cīvakaṉ, his mother tells her husband, King Caccantaṉ, of an evil dream. Caccantaṉ understands in advance that he may die. He therefore trains his wife beforehand to sit in a mechanical peacock and fly away from the palace. As the dream foretold, Caccantaṉ is killed by his minister Kaṭṭiyaṅkāraṉ.

### Shakespeare’s *Julius Caesar*

A similar episode occurs in Shakespeare’s play *Julius Caesar*. One night Caesar cannot sleep and tosses in bed because circumstances are unfavourable. His wife Calpurnia sees an evil dream and cries out in terror. When Caesar consults religious interpreters, they advise him that it would be better not to go out. Calpurnia insists strongly, falls at his feet, and begs him not to leave the palace the next day. She sends word through Antony that the king is unwell and cannot attend the senate. Despite all this, the very courageous Caesar goes to the senate and is killed. This is a foreshadowing dream.

### Abraham Lincoln

Abraham Lincoln, President of the United States, is said to have learned through a dream that he was going to be assassinated and to have recorded it in his diary. This too is foreshadowing.

### Kiḷḷi Vaḷavaṉ’s Enemies in the *Puṟanāṉūṟu*

A *Puṟanāṉūṟu* poem attributed to Kōvūr Kiḻār and addressed to the Cōḻa Kiḷḷi Vaḷavaṉ who died at Kuḷamuṟṟam expresses such a dream vividly. The Cōḻa’s enemies see an evil dream and think of the suffering about to reach them. Fearing they may die, they kiss and fondle their children beforehand to satisfy their longing. They hide the evil dream from their wives, thinking the women will be frightened.

In the dream, fiery meteors fall in all eight directions; dry trees catch fire; the sun appears in many places; evil birds utter harsh cries; teeth fall to the ground without being struck; oil is poured upon their hair; they ride on pigs; their clothing is removed; and their weapons overturn and fall together with the racks on which they had been placed. These details appear in *Puṟanāṉūṟu* 41, beginning “Even Death waits for its time.”

From all these examples, it is clear that people held—and still hold—the belief that evil follows an evil dream. We also see what kinds of dreams were thought to announce death in advance.

All three dreams in *Cilappatikāram* indicate a death that will occur later. The comparative examples given here resemble those in *Cilappatikāram*. The full poems may be consulted in their respective works; quoting them all here would greatly increase the number of pages.


---

# 8. Omens

An *nimittam* is an omen: a sign that indicates beforehand some good or evil that will occur later. One who knows and explains such signs is called a *nimittikaṉ*, an interpreter of omens. Royal courts sometimes maintained such a person. The word *cokiṉam* is used in the *Puṟapporuḷ Veṇpāmālai* with the same meaning:

> “The bamboo-shouldered woman feared  
> when the words overheard and the omens differed,  
> and so the prescribed act was undertaken.” (264, introductory verse)

The old commentary on introductory verse 310 also uses the word:

> “Because of a variation in the omen, the woman whose shoulders resembled tall bamboo grieved when the spear-bearing hero failed to return.”

*Tolkāppiyam*, *Patiṟṟuppattu*, and *Puṟapporuḷ Veṇpāmālai* also use the name *uṉṉam*:

> “The condition of the omen-tree that stood against the opposing king …” (*Tolkāppiyam*, Poruḷ, Puṟam 5:8)

> “Though the thin-stemmed omen-tree drooped  
> before Nārmuṭi Cēral of garland-rich strength …” (*Patiṟṟuppattu* 40:16–17)

If the *uṉṉam* tree flourished, it was a good omen; if it drooped, a bad one. Yet the king did not fear even when it drooped.

> “Our king is the enemy of the thin-stemmed omen-tree,  
> with its small leaves and flowers like gold.” (*Patiṟṟuppattu* 61:5–6)

Even when the golden-flowered, small-leaved tree revealed a bad omen, the king is said to have marched against it as though against an enemy.

> “The fame of the ankleted king, difficult to approach,  
> grew abundant when joined to the omen-tree.”

And:

> “O omen-tree, unfurl all your branches!  
> The enemies who would not bow before  
> the close-ankleted lord of the unapproachable army  
> have been crushed in conflict;  
> the kings bore every assault, but battle has ended.” (*Puṟapporuḷ Veṇpāmālai*, Omen-State 243)

The song tells the tree: “The enemies are destroyed; our king has won; therefore you may flourish even more.” Because the *uṉṉam* tree served as an object of divination, *uṉṉam* itself came to mean omen.

The cry of the black bird called *kāri* was also considered evil. A *Puṟapporuḷ Veṇpāmālai* poem says that the bird cried in the enemy’s territory, thereby indicating victory for the speaker’s king:

> “Among the gem-like cattle of the hostile men  
> wearing veṭci flowers,  
> the black bird cries in distress.” (3:3–4)

The same belief appears in the *Cīvakacintāmaṇi* and other works. The *Puṟanāṉūṟu* also says that if old birds depart and new birds arrive, it is an evil omen.

Good and bad were sometimes inferred from whether words accidentally overheard contained something auspicious. This practice is called *viricci* or *vāyppuḷ*. Going to the edge of a small settlement to listen for chance words is called *pākkattu viricci* in the *Puṟattiṇaiyiyal* of *Tolkāppiyam* (58). The terms *viricci* and *vāyppuḷ* also occur in the *Puṟapporuḷ Veṇpāmālai*.

Some believe that a cat or jackal must not cross one’s path. The old saying deserves remembrance: “What does it matter whether the jackal goes right or left, so long as it does not leap upon you and tear you?” Some also treat the approach of a solitary Brahmin or a widow as an evil omen. That is not right.

Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ, well acquainted with all these beliefs, introduces several omens into his work.

## The Canto in Which the City Celebrates Indra’s Festival

On the day of Indra’s festival in Pukār, Kaṇṇaki’s left eye twitches and Mādhavi’s right eye twitches. One omen-system holds that, for women, twitching on the left brings good and twitching on the right brings harm; for men the reverse applies.

The good approaching Kaṇṇaki is that Kōvalaṉ will soon leave Mādhavi and return to her. The harm approaching Mādhavi is that Kōvalaṉ will soon leave her:

> “Kaṇṇaki’s dark eye and Mādhavi’s red eye  
> hid the fullness within and shed secret tears;  
> in corresponding order, the left and the right twitched  
> on the festival day of the king of the gods.” (237–240)

The line must be read in ordered correspondence: Kaṇṇaki’s eye twitched on the left and Mādhavi’s on the right. *Eṇṇumuṟai* is the Tamil equivalent of “respectively.”

## The Canto of Seeing the Country

It is harmful to the land if the dark planet Saturn smokes and takes a hostile position in enemy houses; if the comet called Dhūmakētu, which ought not to appear, becomes visible; or if Venus, which should rise in the east, appears in the south. Even under such conditions, the Kāviri is said never to fail in water—though one might add, “in those days.”

> “Though the dark one smokes, though the smoke-banner appears,  
> though broad-rayed Venus travels through the southern quarter …” (102–103)

Such occurrences are evil omens.

## The Hunter’s Song

A warrior sets out wearing veṭci flowers to seize the enemy’s cattle. He also receives Koṟṟavai’s favour. The black bird cries in the enemy settlement, foretelling harm to them:

> “When a warrior of the small, fortified town  
> sets out to seize cattle,  
> it seems the white-sworded Lady of the Battlefield too  
> desires him to wear the veṭci flower.  
> If she desires him to wear the veṭci flower,  
> the black bird in the hostile town  
> seems to announce it in a harsh voice.” (12)

The untimely call of birds such as the black bird was treated as a bad omen.

## The Canto of the Killing Ground

If a bull comes forward to gore someone leaving home, that too is an evil sign. Kōvalaṉ takes one anklet from Kaṇṇaki and leaves Mātari’s house to sell it in the market. A bull attempts to gore him; somehow he escapes. He does not recognise the obstruction as a bad omen, because he does not belong to a community trained to interpret it. Instead of returning home, he continues to the market, is accused of theft, and is killed:

> “Leaving the house of the many-cowed herdsmen,  
> he went through the street with a faltering gait.  
> A humped bull confronted him; he did not know it as a fault,  
> for he was not of the community qualified to understand.” (98–101)

The phrase “he went through the street with a faltering gait” movingly suggests that he walked weakly under the weight of anxiety.

In ordinary life, some people who see an evil sign after setting out on an errand return home. Kōvalaṉ’s written destiny was different. What could be done?

Thieves too are said to examine omens before stealing. Even if a rare treasure seems likely to fall easily into their hands, they will not proceed if the departure omen is bad:

> “Unless the omen is favourable,  
> they will not enter at all,  
> even when a rare treasure comes into their hands.” (178–179)

One may wonder whether thieves also consulted omens. More than others, thieves had reason to do so. Because theft in those days could receive the death penalty, they needed to return successfully without being caught.

## The Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai*

In the cowherd settlement, Mātari discusses several evil signs with her daughter Aiyai:

- Milk in the pot, though treated with culture, does not set as curd.
- Tears flow from the bull’s gentle eyes.
- Butter does not melt when heated.
- Goat-kids do not leap and play.
- The cattle tremble and cry as though lamenting.
- Their neck-bells break loose and fall.

The prose-song prelude says:

> “The milk in the pot does not set;  
> tears fall from the gentle eyes of the hump-backed bull;  
> something is coming.”

> “The fragrant butter in the hanging pot does not melt;  
> the kids do not leap and play;  
> something is coming.”

These omens prepare the audience for news of Kōvalaṉ’s death.


---

# 9. Mythological Stories

Many ancient literary works contain mythological stories that modern scientific minds cannot believe. *Cilappatikāram* too has a share in this tradition. Let us consider several such stories.

## The Demons and Mucukunta — Canto of the Sea-Bath

Demons troubled King Mucukunta, and a spirit is said to have protected him:

> “When hosts of fierce demons gathered  
> and attacked the king who guarded the great city  
> with the strength of the cruel-striped tiger,  
> they were defeated by the ankleted ruler.  
> Their hearts grew dark; they pressed their deceitful assault.  
> The great spirit that removed that deception,  
> sent by the god to the lord of the straight spear,  
> remained in the place where it received sacrifice …” (7–13)

A large story lies concealed in this brief passage. Garuḍa had carried away the nectar of immortality. Indra entrusted Pukār to King Mucukunta, who promised to guard it, and left a spirit to assist him. The demons fought Mucukunta and were defeated. They then discharged an arrow that created darkness everywhere. By its power the spirit removed the darkness and helped Mucukunta. When Indra returned, he commanded the spirit to remain as Mucukunta’s bodyguard. It therefore stayed in Pukār.

Aṭiyārkkunallār, the old commentator on *Cilappatikāram*, tells this story and quotes a supporting poem. In summary, the poem says that Indra, setting out to recover the nectar taken by Garuḍa, wondered who would guard the city. Mucukunta rose and promised, “Until you return, it is my duty to sustain it.” Indra gladly placed the praised spirit at his disposal. The fierce demons gathered, fought, lost, and then decided that only deceit could defeat him. They loosed an iron-headed arrow that buried even the sun’s two eyes in dense darkness. The great spirit removed the deception.

The final line of that unidentified poem—“the great spirit that removed the deceit”—is identical with line 11 of *Cilappatikāram*. This deserves notice.

## Urvasi, Jayanta, and Agastya

Another story follows. Urvasi danced at Indra’s command. During the performance she and Indra’s son Jayanta exchanged looks of desire; dance and music consequently lost their order. Sage Agastya cursed Urvasi to be born on earth:

> “Nārada’s skilfully discerned song upon the vīṇā  
> and the formal song of the dancing woman  
> filled the ears of the thousand-eyed one.  
> Urvasi failed in the drama; auspiciousness was lost.  
> ‘Let the vīṇā and this woman remain upon earth,’  
> said the curse she received.” (18–23)

The full story is this. Agastya came to Indra’s court. Indra commanded Urvasi to dance with song. As she danced, Urvasi and Jayanta gazed at one another with desire. Dance and song departed from proper rule; Nārada therefore plucked a hostile string on his vīṇā. Unable to tolerate the disorder, Agastya grew angry. He cursed Nārada’s vīṇā to lie upon earth as wood, Urvasi to be born in the human world, and Jayanta to appear there as bamboo. When they pleaded with him, he prescribed release from the curse.

Aṭiyārkkunallār relates the story and gives a supporting poem. It says that when the sage who loved the great Potiyil mountain entered the court, the immortals worshipped him. Indra ordered the ornamented Urvasi to dance with song. The stage-master arranged costume, performance, music, and dance according to the manuals. But when Urvasi saw Jayanta’s face, desire weakened her dance, and all the instruments and song lost order. The sage perceived the mutual desire in both hearts and cursed Indra’s son to become bamboo. Jayanta bowed and begged for release; the sage declared that at the end of Urvasi’s curse he would become the ceremonial staff taken from the mountain and mounted upon the stage. He also cursed Nārada’s vīṇā to become measureless wood, then granted that it would return to music after sounding upon earth.

That poem is in *nēricai āciriyappā*. Like the cantos of *Cilappatikāram*, it ends with the word *eṉ*—another point worth noting.

## Other Mythological Allusions

The epic contains many further mythological stories or references:

- Śiva burns the three cities at the request of the gods.
- Kaṇṇaṉ defeats Kaṃsa and Bāṇāsura.
- Murukaṉ defeats Sūraṉ.
- Koṟṟavai defeats the demons.
- Kāma performs the eunuch-dance.
- Mālati loses the child of her co-wife; a spirit carries it away; Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ comes as the child and later marries Tēvanti.
- A Pāṇḍya hurls his spear and makes the sea recede.
- A Pāṇḍya throws his bracelet upon Indra’s crown.
- A Pāṇḍya seizes and chains a cloud.
- Naḷa leaves his wife and enters the forest.
- Rāma, separated from his wife, goes into the forest.
- Indra cuts the wings of mountains.
- Kaṇṇaṉ breaks the kuruntu tree, uses a calf as a throwing-stick to bring down fruit, marries Nappiṉṉai, churns the ocean, eats earth, eats butter, measures the worlds with his feet, and goes as messenger for the Pāṇḍavas.
- Murukaṉ splits Mount Krauñca and cleaves the sea to kill Sūraṉ.

Thus *Cilappatikāram* contains numerous mythological narratives.


---

# 10. The Aesthetic of Grief

In the *Meyppāṭṭiyal*, Tolkāppiyar identifies eight embodied emotions. Grief is one of them. *Avalam* and *aḻukai* both mean lamentation or sorrow. Tolkāppiyar says that the emotional flavour of grief arises from four causes: humiliation, loss, displacement, and poverty:

> “Humiliation, loss, displacement, and poverty—  
> these four constitute the unfailing grounds of grief.” (5)

Here we may consider only grief arising from loss. “Aesthetic flavour” does not refer only to pleasure; it includes the many feelings produced by many causes. *Cilappatikāram* has no shortage of the aesthetic of grief.

Great losses include the loss of a good life, indispensable possessions, and life itself. Kaṇṇaki first loses her married life; finally she loses her husband, and later her own life. In connection with this, Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ and Kōpperuntēvi die. Kōvalaṉ’s father and Kaṇṇaki’s father lose domestic life and become ascetics. Their mothers cannot bear the loss of their children and die. Kavunti Aṭikaḷ, who accompanied Kaṇṇaki on the road, dies through a fast; Mātari, who gave refuge, enters fire. Mādhavi and Maṇimēkalai abandon sensual pleasure and take up renunciation. The burning of Madurai is a vast loss.

Among all these, the loss caused by Kōvalaṉ’s death under a false accusation occupies the greatest place in the epic. Evil omens appear in the cowherd settlement. News of Kōvalaṉ’s death is conveyed to Kaṇṇaki. More than death itself, the false charge that he was a thief torments her.

The Canto of the Garland of Suffering paints Kaṇṇaki’s grief like a picture. Her laments melt the heart:

“I do not see my beloved; my heart melts like the inside of a furnace. I do not see the man I loved—is this deception? My heart reels. Must I perish in misery because the king has committed a fault? O lord of burning rays, is my husband a thief?” Thus she grieves and cries aloud.

Looking at her husband’s lifeless body, she wails: “Does your fragrant body lie covered in dust? Does your jewelled chest sink into the earth?” She embraces the body. Sobbing, she asks: “Are there women in this city? Are there worthy people? Is there a god?”

She goes to argue her case before the Pāṇḍya. Addressing the gatekeeper, she attacks the king through him: “Gatekeeper! Gatekeeper of the ruler who has failed in justice, whose wisdom is emptied and whose heart has lost discernment!”

Coming before the Pāṇḍya, she startles him: “Unexamining king, I have something to say.” She boils with anger: “King of Korkai, who has failed to pursue the good path!” When the Pāṇḍya asks, “Who are you, woman whose eyes stream with water?” she speaks proudly of her country, city, community, and name.

She recounts and praises the stories of the seven chaste women of Pukār. Then she vows: “If I too, born in that city, am truly a chaste woman, I will not spare you; I will destroy Madurai together with its king.” She twists off her left breast, throws it upon the city, and causes it to burn.

At last she says, “I entered by the eastern gate with my husband; I leave by the western gate without him.” She reaches the Cēra country, ends her earthly life, and enters heaven:

> “‘I entered the eastern gate with my husband;  
> let me, now destitute, leave by the western gate.’  
> Helpless, she wandered through night and day.” (23:182–184)

This passage is deeply moving.


---

# 11. Arts in the Epic

It is commonly said that there are sixty-four arts. That is one conventional classification; new arts may arise in every age. Each art has treatises of its own, but descriptions of the arts also appear incidentally in works devoted to other subjects. It is therefore unsurprising that several arts occupy a large place in *Cilappatikāram*, an epic that combines literature, music, drama, and continuous narrative. Music and dance-drama receive even greater attention in it than literary art itself.

The *Canto of the Debut Performance* describes the qualifications of several kinds of teachers and the nature of the theatre. Let us examine them.

## 1. Mādhavi’s Training in Dance

Mādhavi appeared as though she were Urvaśī, sent down from Indra’s world by the curse of the sage Agastya. She lacked nothing in the three accomplishments of dance, song, and beauty. Beginning at the age of five, she trained with the dance-staff for seven years, and at twelve made her debut before the Cōḻa king.

## 2. Qualifications of the Dance Teacher

The dance teacher must possess the following capacities:

1. He must know the conventions of *akakkūttu* and *puṟakkūttu*—the inner and outer dramatic forms—including such paired oppositions as satirical and laudatory performance, courtly and popular performance, and Tamil and Āryan performance.
2. He must coordinate dance with the fourteen components of song.
3. He must know the eleven dance forms beginning with *alliyam*.
4. He must know the twenty-eight expressive configurations appropriate to inner drama.
5. He must know the components of outer drama, beginning with *tēvapāṇi*.
6. He must understand the instrumental patterns appropriate to the different divisions of dance.
7. When presenting inner or outer dance in accordance with song, rhythm, and tempo, he must know the subtle use of single-hand and double-hand gestures.
8. In inner dance, he must prevent single-hand gestures from entering the double-hand system, and double-hand gestures from entering the single-hand system.
9. In outer dance, he must ensure that representational gesture does not intrude during pure dance, and that pure dance does not intrude during representational gesture.
10. He must keep the footwork of *kuravai* dance distinct from that of *vari* dance.

These are among the qualities required of a master of dance.

## 3. Qualifications of the Music Teacher

1. He must know, and be proficient in, yāḻ music, flute music, tonal colour, rhythmic divisions, vocal music, the technique of the *taṇṇumai* drum, and the eleven dance forms.
2. He must set composed forms so that they acquire both musical coherence and emotional flavour (*rasa*).
3. He must know the four modes of movement appropriate to the two classes of song, *centuṟai* and *veṇṭuṟai*, and must preserve the correct music for native words, altered literary words, regional words, and northern loanwords, understanding every musical distinction without fault.
4. He must grasp the poet’s intended meaning, the expressive gesture prescribed by the dance teacher, and the song appropriate to both.

These are among the qualifications of the music teacher.

## 4. Qualifications of the Poet

1. He must be accomplished in all three branches of Tamil—literature, music, and drama—so that the whole Tamil country within its four boundaries may recognize his mastery.
2. He must know the two dramatic traditions, the courtly and the popular.
3. He must know the character of the eleven *paṇs* and compose songs suited to rhythm.
4. He must compose in faultless language.

These are the qualifications of the writer of songs.

## 5. Qualifications of the Taṇṇumai Drummer

1. He must know every dance and the song-types beginning with *paṇṇal*.
2. He must know the prescribed fundamental rhythmic patterns. The number printed in the source at this point appears corrupt.
3. He must understand the phrase-components formed from Tamil letters, excluding northern letters, and all three branches of Tamil.
4. He must know the variants of mode, rhythm, and tempo, together with their merits and defects.
5. He must understand the four categories of diction beginning with native words.
6. He must maintain the form without allowing it to slacken.
7. He must know how to sustain what should be sustained and release what should be released.
8. His playing must accord with the yāḻ, flute, and voice.
9. He must supply what other instruments lack and restrain their excess.
10. He must display beautiful hand technique.

These are the qualities expected of the *taṇṇumai* player—the drummer corresponding broadly to the maddaḷam or mṛdaṅgam performer.

## 6. Qualifications of the Flute Teacher

1. He must know *cittirappuṇarppu* and *vañcanaippuṇarppu*, two specialized methods of musical joining.
2. His knowledge must equal that of the song teacher.
3. He must understand ascent, produced by lifting and closing the finger-holes beginning with the index finger, and descent, produced beginning with the little finger.
4. He must know the 103 modal characteristics and the fourteen *kōvai* arrangements.
5. He must coordinate with the *muḻavu*, *taṇṇumai*, yāḻ, and voice.
6. Of the four movements—*mutal-naṭai*, *vāram*, *kūṭai*, and *tiraḷ*—the first is slow and the last highly compressed. Leaving these aside where necessary, he must be able to render the measured *vāram* song with proper order of word and music.
7. He must sound each letter distinctly and grammatically, without damaging its form.

These are among the qualifications of the flute master.

## 7. Qualifications of the Yāḻ Teacher

1. The fourteen *kōvai* arrangements must shine and accord with the nature of the song.
2. He must arrange the seven *pālai* modes through paired strings.
3. By twelve transformations, he must generate the twelve *pālai* modes.
4. He must combine them suitably within the sixteen *kōvai* arrangements associated with women’s register.
5. He must bring forth all the *pālai* modes in correct order.
6. He must understand the tonal conditions called strength, softness, and balance.
7. He must join the strings without disturbing their arrangement or diminishing the modal character.
8. He must make music with syllables appropriate to the joining.

These are the qualifications of the yāḻ teacher.

## 8. Nature of the Theatre

### I. The Site

1. The theatre must be built on land free from defects.
2. Shrines, monasteries, Brahmin settlements, wells, tanks, and groves should be nearby.
3. The ground must be firm and free of pits, hollows, and deep cavities.
4. Its soil should have a pleasant smell and taste.
5. It must contain no bones, husk, stones, salt, slime, or dust.
6. It should stand in the centre of the town, on a street along which chariots pass.

These are the qualities required of the site.

### II. The Stage

The stage should be seven measuring-rods wide, eight rods long, and one rod high. The measuring rod itself is defined as twenty-four thumb-breadths of a well-proportioned man. There must be two entrances, one for entering and one for leaving. The rod should be made from bamboo cut on a sacred mountain such as Potiyil, with one span between each node.

### III. Stage Arrangement

1. Paintings of the guardian spirits of the four social groups should be displayed for public worship.
2. Lamps should be arranged so that the shadows of the pillars do not fall across the stage.
3. A single-faced curtain (*orumukha eḻini*) should be fixed at the left pillar.
4. A double-faced curtain (*porumukha eḻini*) should be fixed between the two right-side pillars.
5. A concealed descending curtain (*karantuvaral eḻini*) should be installed above.
6. An elaborately painted canopy should cover the stage.
7. Pearl garlands of white, gold, and red—associated respectively with the Moon, Jupiter, and Mars—should hang and sway as strands, loops, and festoons.

These are the arrangements required in the theatre.

## 9. Nature of the Talaikkōl

1. The shaft of a royal umbrella captured from a defeated enemy should become the *talaikkōl*, the ceremonial staff of honour.
2. Nine kinds of gems should be set at its joints, with plates of Jāmbūnada gold between them.
3. A special place must be prepared for the staff in the king’s palace.
4. It must be regarded as Jayanta and worshipped according to sacred ritual.
5. On an auspicious day, water from a holy river must be brought in a golden vessel and used to bathe it.
6. It must be decorated with garlands and other ornaments.
7. With an elephant present, drums sounding, and the king and the Five Great Councils accompanying it, the staff must be conveyed by chariot and elephant. It is placed in the hand of a singer standing in the chariot, and carried in procession around the city.
8. On reaching the theatre, it must be installed facing the stage.
9. Only then may the dance begin.

These are the ceremonies connected with the *talaikkōl*.

## 10. Manner of Performance

1. Before the dance begins, the king, ministers, and other dignitaries must sit in their assigned places.
2. The performing area must be apportioned by rods: three rods for the central dance area, one for the dancers, one for the singers, one for the intervening space, and one for the instrumentalists.
3. Mādhavi mounted the stage with her right foot first and stood by the double-faced curtain at the right pillar.
4. The senior women of the chorus stood beside the left pillar, near the single-faced curtain.
5. They first sang prayers to the deity, asking that evil depart and good arise. Both the one-beat and two-beat *vāram* invocations could be sung.
6. At the close of the invocation, all the instruments sounded.
7. The yāḻ followed the flute; the voice and *taṇṇumai* followed the yāḻ; the pot-drum followed the *taṇṇumai*; and the instrument called *iṭakkai* joined the *muḻavu*.
8. Like a hawk and its shadow, all instruments had to move in perfect correspondence. Instrument, song, and dance likewise had to agree.
9. Within this arrangement, Mādhavi danced the eleven forms according to the dramatic treatise, without error.
10. Without disturbing the measure of the *pālai* mode, she fitted words and music to each dramatic form so that song, percussion, and dance became one.
11. She performed all the regional dances in both the slow opening movement and the measured *vāram* movement.
12. She then completed the *Vaiśākha* posture.
13. Appearing like a golden creeper, she followed the treatise exactly, making gesture and emotion visible and never departing from the fourteen prescribed dramatic components.
14. Having performed triumphantly, she received the title marked by the *talaikkōl* and became an acknowledged debutante. The king rewarded her with a green garland valued at 1,008 *kalañcus* of gold.

This gives us the general nature of the performance.

So far we have briefly examined some of the artistic practices described in the *Canto of the Debut Performance*. A full explanation would grow enormously. Let us now glance very briefly at artistic details in other cantos.

## Arts in Other Cantos

### Kāṉal Vari

Mādhavi inspected the yāḻ, tested it by ear, plucked it to examine its tone, and then handed it to Kōvalaṉ. After he played, she played in turn.

### The Summer Canto

After Kōvalaṉ left her, Mādhavi sang the *Mēṟcempālai* mode by voice on the moonlit terrace, but the music faltered under emotion. She then took the *cakōṭa yāḻ* and played the modes called inner-state *marutam*, outer-state *marutam*, near-state *marutam*, and expanded *marutam*; again the music became confused by grief.

### The Canto of Resting in the Outlying Settlement

Kōvalaṉ played the yāḻ with the bards. His handling of the *ceṅkōṭṭu yāḻ* is described with particular distinction. Of the four principal types of yāḻ, he took the *ceṅkōṭṭu* and firmly fastened two of its six components, the *tantirikaram* and the *tivu*, before playing.

### The Canto of Seeing the City

Madurai possessed “two great streets of those skilled in eight times eight arts”—that is, streets of people accomplished in the sixty-four arts. It also had specialists in the arts associated with the nine gems.

### The Canto of Explanation

Astronomical knowledge existed in that age. It had been foretold that Madurai and its kingship would perish by fire on a Friday in the dark fortnight of the month of Āṭi, when the star Kārttikai coincided with the eighth lunar day:

> In the month of Āṭi, in the great dark fortnight,
> on the eighth day in the fiery asterism,
> on a Friday, the bright flame would consume it.  
> *(23:133–135)*

This passage belongs to the art of astronomy.

### The Canto of the Water Consecration

Here we encounter astrology. One evening, while Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ stood on the bank of the Ganges, he saw the crescent moon. At once the astrologer said that thirty-two months had passed since they left Vañji. Astronomy and astrology are closely related arts.

### The Canto of the Memorial Stone

After his victory, Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ offered his warriors a feast of yāḻ music performed by women minstrels. They embraced the small yāḻ with its curved stem, harmonious strings, and resonating body, and sang the *Cempālai* mode with *kural* as its base, then *Paṭumalaippālai* with *tuttam* as base, followed by *Cevvaḻippālai* and the lovely, sweet *Kuṟiñci* mode. This was a banquet for the ear.

When Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ sat with his queen Vēṇmāḷ on the moonlit palace terrace, a skilled actor named Cākkaiyaṉ delighted them by performing the dance called *Koṭṭic Cētam* or *Koṭukoṭṭi*. This dance was believed to have been performed by Śiva. Its marvel was that Śiva danced while holding Umā on the left half of his body: her anklets and shoulder ornaments did not tremble, her girdle made no sound, her breasts did not move, her earrings did not sway, and her hair did not loosen. Cākkaiyaṉ reproduced that feat.

When I think deeply about all these arts, my head spins. Remembering that I myself possess mastery in none of them makes fear and shame compete within me. When even understanding these subtleties is difficult, where is the question of mastering them? Some such artists still live today. They should be honoured properly. To those concerned only with filling their stomachs, and to money-making machines—human robots—what difference is there between art and an eggplant? Along with creating and developing new arts, people have a duty to protect the old arts from extinction.


---

# 12. Wars in *Cilampu*

Although the wars mentioned in *Cilappatikāram* have been referred to here and there under several headings, they may be gathered together in this chapter.

The Cōḻa king Mucukunta fought the demons. The broad-shouldered Cempiyaṉ destroyed the suspended fortresses in the sky.

Karikāla Cōḻa marched north, defeated the northern rulers, struck the Himalaya with his royal goad, planted the tiger banner there, and returned.

The Pāṇḍya king Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ, while still young, defeated seven enemies at Talaiyālaṅkāṉam. The Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ who “crossed the Āryan army” conquered as far as the Himalaya and the Ganges.

One Cēra king defeated his enemies at sea and felled their sacred *kaṭampu* tree.

The Golden-handed Pāṇḍya hurled the weapon called the *vaḷai* at Indra’s crown and shattered it.

Neṭuñcēralātaṉ campaigned northward as far as the Himalaya, won victory there, received the title “He whose boundary is the Himalaya,” and ruled the whole of Nāvalam Island—India—under one language of command.

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ performed the battlefield sacrifice at the red field of the Koṅkus; defeated the forces that opposed him when he went to bathe his mother in the Ganges; and, when nine collateral Cōḻa princes opposed his brother-in-law Kiḷḷi at Nērivāyil, defeated them and secured Kiḷḷi’s throne. At Mōkūr he cut down the neem that served as the guardian tree of Paḻaiyaṉ and wore the garland of victory. He conquered Viyalūr and fought a fierce battle at Iṭumpil, defeating his enemies.

Later, the northern kings Uttaraṉ, Vicittiraṉ, Uruttiraṉ, Pairavaṉ, Cittiraṉ, Ciṅkaṉ, Taṉuttiraṉ, and Civētaṉ joined Kanaka and Vijaya in battle against Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ. He defeated them all, placed on the crowns of Kanaka and Vijaya the black stone from which Kaṇṇaki’s image was to be carved, and compelled them to carry it south.

Besides these wars of kings, the epic also makes scattered reference to battles fought by Śiva, Viṣṇu, Murukaṉ, and Koṟṟavai.

If every battle were described in detail, the discussion would expand into a large book of its own.


---

# 13. Commerce

Human society cannot live without trade—within a town or beyond it, within a country or with foreign lands. No one can produce every necessary thing. In ancient times people exchanged what each had produced by barter. Barter gradually assumed several forms and finally developed into the commercial system known today. During the Sangam age and the period adjoining it, goods were carried overland on foot and in carts for exchange within the country. Ships also brought commodities from other lands by sea and carried other goods away in return.

## The Canto of Establishing Domestic Virtue

In ancient times Pukār, capital of the Cōḻas, was a great commercial centre. Varied commodities from newly encountered countries were brought there both overland and by ship and accumulated together. The *Canto of Establishing Domestic Virtue* says:

> Rare and precious goods from unfamiliar lands,
> gathered together as though those lands themselves had assembled,
> were brought and amassed by ship and by land.  
> *(5–7)*

“Unfamiliar lands” means newly reached countries. One land may produce one commodity in abundance and become famous for it. Because the products of many countries were gathered at Pukār, the city looked as though many nations themselves had assembled in one place.

## The Canto Celebrating the Evening

### Commodities from the Four Directions

In the evening, Kōvalaṉ and Mādhavi and the other people enjoyed themselves on moonlit terraces. In summer they did not burn the crystalline resin brought from the west or the dark *akil* wood brought from the east. Instead, they ground sandalwood from the southern mountain on a circular stone brought from the north and applied the paste:

> Leaving aside the white resin from the western quarter
> and the dark *akil* from the eastern quarter,
> on the lustrous round stone born in the northern mountain
> they ground sandal born in the southern mountain.  
> *(35–38)*

In cold weather resin and *akil* were burned; their smoke gave both firm warmth and fragrance. In summer sandalwood was rubbed on a circular grinding stone and applied. I know that households once possessed such stones because there is one in our own house. In my youth, after bathing, one applied sacred ash to the forehead, placed a spot of sandal paste in the centre, and then a smaller spot of vermilion within that. The household sandal-grinding stone has now become rare.

The following lines of *Neṭunalvāṭai* invite comparison:

> They burned white resin with dense-grained *akil*.  
> *(56)*

> The lustrous round stone brought by northerners
> lay with sandal from the southern land.  
> *(51–52)*

It is astonishing that the two works correspond as though their authors had agreed beforehand.

The passage shows that resin came from the west, *akil* from the east, grinding stones from the north, and sandalwood from the south, all through trade.

A doubt may arise: Pukār stood on the seashore, with the sea to its east; how could *akil* come “from the east”? It could have been shipped by sea from an inland region lying eastward. It might also have come from countries beyond the Bay of Bengal. Goods from the other three directions need not all have come by ship; they could have arrived overland.

## The Canto in Which the City Celebrated the Indra Festival

### Maruvūrppākkam

Pukār had two divisions, Maruvūrppākkam and Paṭṭiṉappākkam.

Under French rule Puducherry too had two sectors, the European quarter and the Tamil quarter. The European settlement lay along the shore and the Tamil settlement to its west, separated by a canal. Traces of this arrangement remain. Even after the Europeans departed, their quarter remained clean; the Tamil quarter cannot be praised to the same degree. Following the psychological method of moving from the near to the distant, the present to the past, the easy to the difficult, and the known to the unknown, we may proceed from Puducherry to Pukār.

Just as Puducherry had a European quarter by the sea, Pukār had Maruvūrppākkam, where people from many countries stayed. Paṭṭiṉappākkam lay to its west. *Pākkam* is one of the words for a settlement, while *paṭṭiṉam* means a city; Paṭṭiṉappākkam therefore indicates the large, urban part of the settlement.

What does Maruvūrppākkam mean? *Maruvu* means to join, mingle, or become associated. The word occurs in the same sense in the *Tirukkuṟaḷ*: “Associate with the friendship of the faultless” (800). The quarter where numerous foreigners came, joined, and mingled was called Maruvūrppākkam. In parts of the old South Arcot district, the first ceremonial meal taken by a bridegroom in the bride’s house, or by a bride in the bridegroom’s house, is called *maruvu-uṇṇutal*, “eating after joining.” The two families are usually called *campantikaḷ*, a Sanskrit-derived word. It would be more beautiful to call them in Tamil *maruviṉōr* or *maruviṉavar*—those who have joined.

### What Maruvūrppākkam Contained

Maruvūrppākkam contained the splendid and profitable residence of the Yavanas, so attractive that it detained passers-by; lodgings where migrants who had brought goods by ship from many lands lived together; streets where vendors cried out and sold coloured pastes, lime powder, sandal paste, flowers, incense, and perfumes; districts selling innumerable textiles, gems, grains, prepared foods, fish, meat, salt, betel, and many other things; and quarters occupied by craftsmen of numerous trades. Iḷaṅkō sings:

> Near the harbour mouth stood the dwellings of the Yavanas,
> rich beyond measure and arresting all who saw them;
> within the shining waterfront lived together
> migrants whose wealth had come by ship.
>
> Through the city streets moved sellers of pigments,
> lime powder, cooling sandal, flowers, incense,
> and every desired fragrance.
>
> There were the quarters of subtle-fingered weavers
> who worked in silk, wool, and cotton thread;
> broad streets where wealth lay bewilderingly heaped—
> fine cloth, garments, pearl garlands, *akil*,
> flawless pearls, gems, gold,
> and rare treasures beyond measure or reckoning.
>
> There was the grain street, where classified wares
> and heaps of grain were amassed;
> and the meat-rich quarter crowded with sellers of *piṭṭu*,
> bakers of cakes, vendors of toddy,
> fisherfolk selling fish, merchants in white salt,
> betel sellers, perfume dealers, workers in many fats,
> and sellers of oil.  
> *(9–27)*

The poem continues at length. *Kāḻiyar* are sellers of *piṭṭu*; *kūviyar* are bakers; *pācavar* sell betel; *ōcunar* sell oil. People from China, Arab lands, Greece, Egypt, Rome, and elsewhere were referred to as Yavanas or Cōṉakars.

This passage makes the commercial prosperity of Pukār unmistakable.

### Merchants’ Flags

Merchants who brought many goods by ship from many countries spread them for sale on the white sand of the shore. In each place where a particular commodity was heaped, they planted a flag announcing what was sold there:

> Migrants whose wealth came by ship,
> on the white sand beside the spreading waves,
> raised flags in the market streets to proclaim their wares,
> along the ordered merchant quarter.  
> *(130–133)*

Some merchants today advertise by writing on broad rectangular cloth banners. The custom of cloth advertising seems to have existed then as well. The phrase *mālai-c-cēri* deserves attention: *mālai* means order or arrangement, and *cēri* here denotes the separate section in which each class of goods was sold.

It is painful that Kāvirippūmpaṭṭiṉam, which flourished with such distinction, was swallowed by the sea. So far we have considered the commercial wealth of the Cōḻa city of Pukār. We may now turn to that of the Pāṇḍya city of Madurai.

## The Canto of Seeing the City

Kōvalaṉ left Kaṇṇaki in the care of Kauṇti in the outlying settlement and went to look around Madurai. He saw a market street selling the nine gems; a quarter where flags advertised gold; streets selling hundreds of varieties of cloth woven from cotton, silk, and different kinds of hair or wool; and vendors who moved about at every hour with pepper bundles, balances, measures, and loads of grain. He also saw many other commercial establishments and quarters of the city:

> Experts who distinguished every kind of gem
> gathered in the secure and prosperous street
> that knew no fear of hostile attack.
>
> Dealers who tested the shining metal and dispelled doubt
> raised bright flags in the splendid street,
> selling the four renowned forms of gold—
> Cātarūpam, Kiḷicciṟai, Āṭakam, and Cāmpūnatam.
>
> In the cloth street lay hundreds of folded varieties,
> impossible to classify fully,
> woven from ordinary thread, hair or wool,
> and the finest silk thread.
>
> Bearers of balances, men with broad weighing pans,
> and keepers of measuring vessels moved everywhere;
> without regard to hour, with sacks of black pepper,
> they heaped grain in the grain street.  
> *(199–211)*

The street of gem experts was so well protected that it knew no fear of enemies. Sellers of the four kinds of gold displayed flags to advertise their trade.

The canto explains the properties of the nine gems and the expertise required to examine them. Trade will be considered further under the chapter on Madurai. The commercial prosperity of ancient Tamilakam inspires great wonder.


---

# 14. Customs and Conventions

We can identify in *Cilappatikāram* a number of customs and conventions observed among the people of its age.

A courtesan’s first formal dance debut began only after the ceremonial *talaikkōl* had been taken in procession through the city and worshipped. The *Canto of the Debut Performance* says:

> The *talaikkōl*, saluted and ritually worshipped …  
> *(3:120)*
>
> … was carried in procession through the city,
> brought inside, and placed before them.  
> *(3:128)*

When entering a mansion or entering a wedding house for the first time, women placed the right foot first. Mādhavi mounted the stage in this way:

> Placing her right foot first, she ascended the stage.  
> *(3:131)*

There was belief in dreams and omens; an entire canto is entitled *The Canto that Reveals the Nature of the Dream*.

> The dark eye of Kaṇṇaki and the reddened eye of Mādhavi
> throbbed in due order, the one on the left and the other on the right.  
> *(5:27)*

This reveals belief in bodily omens. Mādhavi’s eyes are called red because she was then enjoying union.

Warriors sometimes offered their own lives so that their king might gain victory:

> “May the victorious king obtain triumph!”—
> they made the noble altar receive the auspicious offering,
> while the thunderous cry rose from those whose lives became the sacrifice.  
> *(5:85–87)*

On the fifth day after a child’s birth, the period of birth impurity was ended and a naming ceremony was held. This happened after Mādhavi gave birth to Maṇimēkalai:

> The tender-shoot-bodied maiden Mādhavi
> bore a milk-mouthed child; and after
> the five days of purification had passed,
> the venerable courtesans said,
> “Let us establish an auspicious name for Mādhavi’s daughter.”  
> *(15:22–26)*

Before an elephant came through the streets, a drum was beaten to warn the people:

> Attendants bearing the royal broad-handed emblem
> caused the drum to rise and sound.  
> *(3:124–125)*

> With the mahout dismounted and drums beaten everywhere,
> the furious charging elephant came to seize him.  
> *(15:46–47)*

Certain people received the title *Eṭṭi*. Cāyalaṉ, for example, was an *Eṭṭi*-title holder.

When people are weighed down by private grief and someone asks, “Why do you look so troubled?”, they sometimes conceal the truth by saying, “Nothing—just a slight headache.” Queen Kōpperuntēvi became jealous because the Pāṇḍya king Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ was absorbed in the dancers’ music and performance. Yet she hid her resentment and claimed that a headache was troubling her. Do people not still do this? Is “headache” not one of the familiar reasons entered on an office leave application? Even the queen followed the practice:

> Thinking that the forms of the Madurai women’s dances,
> the divisions of their songs, and the effects of their modes
> had captured the guardian king’s heart,
> the chief queen concealed the resentment within her,
> placed the blame upon a troubling headache,
> and withdrew without joining him.  
> *(16:131–136)*

Sacrifice to deities existed then. There were many forms of worship, including reverence for chaste wives. Jainism and Buddhism were widely followed.

Belief in fate and former birth has been discussed at length elsewhere. There was also belief in spirits, ghosts, and demons, including the belief that a spirit would strike and devour wrongdoers: “the spirit-square where the spirit strikes and eats” (5:134).

Courtesanship existed. During festivals men and women wandered about happily and sought pleasure.

Although there were many religions, many people acted without sectarian discrimination. When Kōvalaṉ left Pukār with Kaṇṇaki, he worshipped at temples of Viṣṇu, the Buddha, and the Arhat:

> He passed after circling the shrine of the jewel-hued Lord
> who lay in rare sleep upon the beautiful serpent.  
> *(10:9–10)*

> The green-leaved Bodhi with its five high-spreading branches …  
> *(10:11)*
>
> He passed the seven Indra monasteries …  
> *(10:14)*
>
> At the shrine of the Arhat established in five forms …  
> *(10:18)*
>
> he worshipped the radiant stone seat and circled it.  
> *(10:25)*

The first is Vaiṣṇava, the next Buddhist, and the third Jain. Kōvalaṉ evidently made no religious distinction.

There is another instance. Before Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ left to campaign in the north, he worshipped Śiva at Vañji and wore upon his head the flower garland given there. When he later reached the temple of Viṣṇu, he wore the sacred remnant—the garland offered there—upon his shoulder. This is described in the *Canto of Taking the Auspicious Sword and Umbrella*.

## Marriage Customs

Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki married according to a particular custom. Kaṇṇaki was twelve years old—“a maiden of two times six years”—and Kōvalaṉ sixteen—“a youth of two times eight.” The commentators on *Tolkāppiyam* also mention these ages.

In his commentary on the aphorism beginning “birth, lineage, manliness, and age” in the *Meyppāṭṭiyal*, Pērāciriyar explains that womanhood and manhood mature respectively at twelve and sixteen.

Iḷampūraṇar, commenting on *Kaḷaviyal* 2, says that equality of age means correspondence of maturity: after childhood, the male is sixteen and the female twelve.

Modern law does not permit marriage below eighteen for a woman or twenty-one for a man. Child marriage occurred in the old period. I remember reading somewhere that Mahatma Gandhi, speaking of his own child marriage, said that he thought a little girl had merely been brought to play with him. Child marriage was once compulsory among Brahmins and gradually infected other communities. Today, by contrast, some “young old women” who have passed thirty-five find it difficult to obtain a bridegroom. Alas, cruel society!

Because the parents of the *Cilappatikāram* couple were exceedingly wealthy, they seated ornamented women upon elephants and sent them around the city to announce the wedding:

> Seating jewelled women upon the backs of elephants,
> they proclaimed the marriage to the great city.  
> *(1:43–44)*

This appears to have been a custom among the upper classes. Could it be done today?

A day when the Moon joined the star Rohiṇī was considered auspicious, and the marriage took place then. Rohiṇī is one of the twenty-seven stars beginning with Aśvinī. Myth makes these stars daughters of Dakṣa and wives of the Moon. The Moon loved Rohiṇī above the others, provoking Dakṣa’s anger. Viewed through astronomy, the basis of the story becomes intelligible.

A venerable Brahmin guided Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki according to Vedic rule, and they circled the fire. Women carried sandal, incense, lime powder, lamps, and sprouting ceremonial pots. The women blessed Kaṇṇaki, who resembled Arundhatī, and “raised her onto the auspicious bed”:

> Praying, “May evil depart,” and scattering small flowers,
> they raised upon the auspicious, lovely bed
> her who was like Arundhatī in this wide world.  
> *(1:62–64)*

Commentators merely gloss the phrase as “placed her on the bed.” I, Sundara Shanmuganar, venture a timid suggestion: why should it not mean that on the very night of the wedding they placed her on the bed and arranged the first night? Otherwise, why not say that they seated her on a chair? *Amaḷi* means a bed. Since a bride may hesitate in shyness on the first night, the poet says they “raised” her onto it.

Some families conduct the first night on the wedding day; others do not. Some say that it is best to unite the couple at once rather than guard them for several days under a regimen of abstinence. Others prefer that they first become acquainted and lose their shyness. Some couples begin direct action without the elders’ knowledge before an auspicious day can be selected. In some families an auspicious day is chosen for the first night and a feast is held as though it were a separate festival.

The marriage of Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki raises a controversy. *Cilampu* says that a priest conducted rites with a sacred fire. Some call this an Āryan, not a Tamil, method and demand its abolition. Others say it may be followed precisely because it appears in *Cilappatikāram*.

Every family may have its own custom. The presence of one rite in *Cilappatikāram* does not prove that all Tamils of the period followed an Āryan method. In 1960 I presided over the marriage of P. Sāmiyappaṉ at Elukāṉvalacu in the former Periyar district. When I asked about their old practice, they said that from generation to generation they had never employed a Brahmin; a respected Tamil elder conducted the marriage. In 1969 I presided over the marriage of Kumara Ñāṉapūraṇam at Kuruntampaṭṭu in Ramanathapuram district. There too they said that a Tamil elder had always conducted their weddings.

Thus the rite described by *Cilappatikāram* may have belonged to one section of society. It cannot bind everyone.

## Other Customs

Shops raised announcement flags to show what was sold there:

> In the splendid street, dealers who tested bright gold
> raised shining flags and removed the buyer’s uncertainty.  
> *(14:203–204)*

Foreign merchants such as Yavanas were numerous in Tamilakam.

Those who circumambulated an object did so three times. Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ circled Kaṇṇaki’s sanctuary three times:

> Following the rightward course, he came around three times,
> worshipped, and stood before the sovereign of the world.  
> *(30:155–156)*

Men seem to have ridden mules. The *Canto of the Sea Festival* says that Kōvalaṉ rode one to the shore:

> The sky-generous-handed woman mounted the *attiri*.  
> *(6:119)*

*Attiri* means a mule, though some call it a kind of horse. Some village horses look very much like donkeys; perhaps it was such an animal.

Kōvalaṉ tells Māṭalaṉ of his dream and fears that, because dreams seen in the latter part of the night are said to come true, some harm will quickly befall him:

> In the midnight darkness I saw a dream as vivid as waking;
> I fear that it will swiftly come upon us here.  
> *(15:105–106)*

Some commentators interpret “midnight watch” as the last watch. The phrase itself does not literally mean the last watch; they seem to infer it from “will swiftly come.” A verse in the *Dream Treatise* provides internal evidence. A dream in the first watch bears fruit within a year; one in the second within eight months; one in the third within three months; one in the fourth or final watch within ten days:

> What is fashioned in the first watch bears fruit in one year;
> what is seen in the second, they say, arrives in eight months;
> what falls in the middle, the third watch, in three months;
> but the dream of the final watch yields its fruit within ten days.  
> *(3)*

The fourth watch extends from about three to six in the morning. Because the poem says “swiftly,” commentators probably concluded that Kōvalaṉ meant this last watch.

In moments of public joy, kings released prisoners and remitted taxes. The victorious Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ did so:

> “Open the prison fortress everywhere;
> in every good town marked by taxation, grant remission.”
> He dispatched Aḻumpil Vēḷ and the revenue accountants
> throughout the ancient land ringed by sounding seas.  
> *(28:203–206)*

The *Canto of Explanation* says that the Pāṇḍya king acted similarly:

> “Open every prison fortress;
> remit the dues of all people burdened by taxation.”  
> *(23:126–127)*

Another custom concerned serving food to one’s husband: the wife washed his feet and sprinkled water on the floor before placing the leaf. Kaṇṇaki did so when feeding Kōvalaṉ. Merchant families apparently performed certain rites before eating:

> With her fragrant-flower hand she received
> the water from her beloved’s feet in a fired-clay bowl.  
> *(16:38–39)*

> She sprinkled water and smoothed the ground with her hand …  
> *(16:41)*

> For those who came after kings, she completed once
> the proper rite according to the rare scripture and their tradition.  
> *(16:44–45)*

## Spirit Possession

Women were believed to become possessed by a deity and utter oracles. In the *Hunter’s Song*, the deity possesses Cāliṉi, priestess of the hunter community:

> Loud-mouthed Cāliṉi, bound by an ancient debt,
> became filled with the god; the hairs of her body stood erect …  
> *(12:7–8)*
>
> In the assembly at the centre of the settlement she shifted her feet and danced.  
> *(12:11)*

The *Canto of Granting the Boon* says that the deity Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ possessed Tēvanti and spoke:

> Tēvantikai rose, possessed by the deity,
> before the lord of the kuṟiñci land rich in fresh shoots.  
> *(30:45–46)*

> “I am Pācaṇṭaṉ; I have entered the Brahmin woman.”  
> *(30:69–70)*

Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ thus spoke through Tēvanti to Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ. The poem also says that Kaṇṇaki manifested through Tēvanti and addressed Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ:

> She appeared in splendour upon Tēvantikai.  
> *(30:172)*

Kaṇṇaki is said to have spoken many things through Tēvanti’s mouth.

Possession, ecstatic dancing, and oracle-giving continue today. People ask questions of the possessed woman, who says whatever comes to her mind. If she cannot answer, she may cry, “Hey! Are you testing me? Are you testing me? I am going back up the mountain!” and abruptly end the possession. Scientists are said to regard some emotionally induced possession as a form of hysteria. A conclusion is possible only after further investigation.

## The Custom of Greeting a King

One must also mention the custom of beginning an address to an elder or king with a blessing, and sometimes ending with another blessing. *Cilappatikāram* follows this convention in several places.

Before telling the Pāṇḍya king that Kaṇṇaki waited outside, the gatekeeper began: “Long live our king of Koṟkai, long live!” (20:30).

Before speaking to Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ about Maṇimēkalai, Tēvanti blessed him:

> May the prince’s victory rise without diminution,
> and may the land pour forth great abundance.  
> *(30:6–7)*

When the hill people began telling Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ about Kaṇṇaki, they said:

> We are your servants through seven births; may your victory live!  
> *(25:56)*

They concluded:

> May you live for many hundreds of thousands of years!  
> *(25:63)*

The Tamil *nūṟāyiram*, “one hundred thousand,” is the native equivalent of the Sanskrit-derived *lakṣam*.

Before recounting earlier events to Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, Māṭalaṉ praised him:

> “King of kings, live long!”  
> *(30:118)*

He ended:

> Guarding the world age after age,
> may you live long, great one!  
> *(30:145–146)*

When Cāttaṉār, author of *Maṇimēkalai*, finished telling Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ what had happened at Madurai, he said:

> I have related it here without omission;
> may your victorious triumph flourish through age after age,
> generation after generation.  
> *(25:91–92)*

Before Villavaṉ Kōtai addressed Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, he too blessed the king:

> “May your victory live for many years!”—
> thus Villavaṉ Kōtai spoke to the king.  
> *(25:150–151)*

The king was blessed not only in direct address but also when his command was publicly proclaimed by drum:

> Long live our king, the great one among rulers;
> may he guard the world through every age!  
> *(25:181–182)*

Only after this blessing did the herald announce the order. Blessing kings was a settled convention.

This reminds me of a song we sang before class when I was a schoolboy:

> Long live, long live King George the Fifth;
> long may he live!

Such are the transformations of time.

## A Courtesan’s Debut and Choice of Patron

*Cilampu* reveals another rare convention. A young woman born into the courtesan community and trained in dance and music appears to have gained the right to choose a patron or enter professional artistic life only after formally displaying her art before the king. After Mādhavi’s debut, she announced that whoever purchased the emerald garland given by the king might obtain her, and sent it to the market through a companion. Kōvalaṉ, wandering there, bought the garland and reached Mādhavi. People say, “Honour has boarded a ship and sailed away”; here, before going that far, the honour of Mādhavi and Kōvalaṉ climbed onto the marketplace itself. It was a method lacking both culture and civility.

*Maṇimēkalai* also indicates this performance convention. When Mādhavi and Maṇimēkalai did not attend the Indra festival at Pukār, public gossip arose:

> Because Mādhavi did not come with Maṇimēkalai,
> unappeased distress and gossip rose from mouth to mouth.  
> *(2:4–5)*

Mādhavi’s mother Cittirāpati summoned Vayantamālai and asked her to tell Mādhavi that the whole city was talking and to bring her back:

> Calling Vayantamālai to come, she said,
> “Go tell her of the gossip raised in the prosperous great city.”  
> *(2:8–9)*

The twin epics therefore show that such a convention existed. It is said to survive here and there today in a diminished form: in the past the debut had to occur before the king; today it may take place before some prominent leader. With time this too will diminish and disappear. Time is by nature changeable.

## The *Muṭaṅkal*—Letter

Today the letter overcomes distance and time through the postal service; then came the telephone and later television, making communication immediate and uninterrupted. This is science’s divine play. Among Tamil literary works, *Cilappatikāram* is the first to introduce the writing of a *muṭaṅkal*, a letter. Mādhavi sent two letters to Kōvalaṉ, one through Vayantamālai and one through Kōcikaṉ. The custom had already begun in that age.

## Worship of the Chaste Wife

It appears possible to say that *Cilappatikāram* first introduced Pattini worship into Tamil literature. Hero-stone worship should not be confused with it. Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ built a sanctuary for Kaṇṇaki and inaugurated the worship of the chaste wife. Kanaka, Vijaya, and the other released northern kings, along with other rulers freed from prison, the Koṅkus of Kuṭaku, the king of Māḷuva, and Kayavāku of sea-girt Laṅkā, prayed that the goddess Kaṇṇaki should appear and grant grace in their countries too:

> Before the sovereign of the world stood
> the Āryan kings released from harsh captivity,
> the other kings freed from the great prison-fort,
> the Koṅkus of Kuṭaku, the king of Māḷuva,
> and Kayavāku, king of sea-girt Laṅkā.
> They worshipped and prayed:
> “As at the auspicious festival performed
> by the king whose boundary is the Himalaya,
> come also to our countries and bestow your presence.”
> A voice arose: “I have granted the boon.”  
> *(30:156–164)*

This makes it clear that northern kings too built temples for the Pattini. Kaṇṇaki worship, once spread across Nāvalam Island—India—began to recede after the Mahābhārata story became established in the south. Researchers say that some Draupadī Amman temples in Tamil Nadu were formerly Kaṇṇaki temples: Draupadī occupied Kaṇṇaki’s place.

The female deities worshipped in Tamil village-goddess temples were originally human women. Examine the stories of the many goddesses called Māriyammaṉ, believed to cause rain, and this becomes apparent. One story says that Tiruvaḷḷuvar’s wife became Māriyammaṉ. Others say that Nāgavalli, wife of the sage Bhṛgu, or Rēṇukā, wife of Jamadagni, became Māriyammaṉ. Paraśurāma, Rēṇukā’s son, is said to have cut off her head for a grave offence. Therefore in some places only a neck or severed head is worshipped. This is one story behind “Neck Māriyammaṉ.” There is no need to retell the story of Draupadī, imperishable chaste wife shared by five husbands.

From all this we may accept without doubt that Kaṇṇaki was worshipped in a temple as the Pattini goddess. The convention began in the period represented by *Cilappatikāram*.

Near the border of Madurai district stands the Maṅkaḷatēvi temple, now within Kerala. Newspaper readers know that the Tamil Nadu government has asked Kerala to transfer that territory. The Maṅkaḷatēvi temple is identified as the Kaṇṇaki temple. *Cilampu* calls it “the sanctuary of the auspicious maiden” (30:88).

## Tying the Tāli

Whether the bridegroom tied a *tāli* around the bride’s neck in the age of *Cilappatikāram* remains a question.

The first canto, *The Auspicious Blessing Song*, contains the phrase “the auspicious ornament rose through the broad city” (1:46). Traditional commentators explain this as “the auspicious cord went in procession through the town.” The auspicious cord is the *tāli*. On that basis, one may say that the custom existed.

## Capital Punishment for Theft

A harsh custom of the period must also be mentioned: thieves could receive the death penalty. Today capital punishment is imposed only for murder, and even some murderers escape with life imprisonment. In that age it applied not only to killers but to thieves. When Kaṇṇaki argued that the execution of her husband was unjust, Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ replied that killing a thief was not tyranny but just rule:

> “To put a thief to death is not a crooked sceptre;
> behold, it is the victory of the bright-speared just sceptre.”  
> *(20:64–65)*

A proverb says, “The thief’s wife and the king’s wife are widows.” Kings often died in war, leaving their wives widowed; thieves were executed, leaving theirs widowed too. This explains the proverb.

It is said that in some Arab countries theft is punished by cutting off a limb, and that in some Arab lands and in the Soviet Union, property remains where it is left because no one touches it. Travellers report so. Mutilation also existed in our country. *Nālaṭiyār* says that if a man desiring another’s wife is caught, his legs may be cut off, while disgrace and misery follow:

> If seen, it disgraces the clan; if caught, the legs are cut;
> while committing the unmanly act, there is fear;
> and it yields long torment in hell.
> O debased adulterer, tell me:
> what measure of pleasure did you find in it?  
> *(14)*

The words “if caught, the legs are cut” deserve attention.

I remember reading some years ago that two government officers in Russia—the Soviet Union—were executed for taking bribes. Tiruvaḷḷuvar also accepted severe punishment. Killing cruel criminals is like removing weeds that destroy a crop:

> A king’s punishment of those cruel in killing
> is like a farmer’s removal of weeds from the green crop.  
> *(Tirukkuṟaḷ 550)*

This is also an age in which some demand the abolition of capital punishment. Every matter receives its answer in the proper time.

## Evening Worship and Dress

As day ended and evening approached, women mixed jasmine with paddy, scattered it through the house, lit lamps, and worshipped. They then put on clothing suited to the night:

> Throughout the spacious houses, slender-waisted women
> scattered opening jasmine with lustrous paddy,
> lit jewelled lamps when daylight died,
> and assumed a form of dress suited to the night.  
> *(9:1–4)*

Here *nakar* means house and *nikar* means radiance. Nakkīrar records the same convention in *Neṭunalvāṭai*:

> Scattering paddy and flowers, they joined their hands in worship
> and celebrated evening in the prosperous market-town.  
> *(43–44)*

Even today some women other than the poor wear nightdress. Some also wear during the day a long gown resembling the clothing of Catholic nuns, thereby diminishing the visible grace of womanhood.

It was believed that a woman abandoned by her husband could regain him by worshipping after scattering a mixture of Bermuda grass, *ciṟupūḷai* flowers, and paddy. Tēvanti performed this for Kaṇṇaki and blessed her:

> Thinking in her young heart that some misfortune
> had befallen the good Kaṇṇaki, she approached,
> scattered *aṟuku* grass, *ciṟupūḷai*, and paddy,
> and said, “May you regain your husband.”  
> *(9:41–44)*

This custom does not seem to be followed everywhere today.

When Kauṇti learned that Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki had died, she fasted to death; Mātari entered fire. When relatives or intimate companions die, some people still choose death in such ways. The practice therefore existed then as it does now.

Thus numerous customs and conventions prevailed in that age.


---

# 15. Depiction of Natural Scenes

It is natural for literature to depict and describe scenes of nature. There are even small works devoted entirely to such description. Need it then be asked whether natural description finds a place in the great epic *Cilappatikāram*?

In the *Auspicious Blessing Song*, the Moon sustains the world, the Sun circles Mount Meru, and the great rain pours down water.

Mountains, sea, rivers, clouds, birds, animals, varieties of trees, groves, countries, and cities are described here and there throughout *Cilampu*.

In the *Canto of Seeing the Country*, Kauṇti explains the conditions of the route: the fierce heat of summer, the cruelty of the arid tract, the scorching of trees such as *marām*, *ōmai*, *uḻiñcil*, and bamboo, and the cries of deer unable to find water.

In the *Summer Canto*, the arrival of summer is announced by the messenger-breeze from Mount Potiyil. The cuckoo, belonging to Kāma’s army, calls out, and many kinds of flowers bloom in abundance.

The songs of *Kāṉal Vari* are rich in natural scenery. The *Hill-Kuravai* depicts the wealth of the mountains.

In the *Canto in Which the City Celebrated the Indra Festival*, the descriptions of Paṭṭiṉappākkam and Maruvūrppākkam in Pukār occupy a distinguished place.

The seashore description in the *Canto of the Sea Festival* touches and delights the heart.

In the *Canto of Seeing the City*, Madurai is painted as a verbal picture.

Though descriptions of Pukār and Madurai are not strictly scenes of untouched nature, Iḷaṅkō Aṭikaḷ must have used his imaginative eye to envision what those great cities contained.

## The Description of Evening

Evening is finely portrayed in the *Canto Celebrating the Evening*.

The Sun sets. The earth-woman grieves because she cannot see her husband, the Sun. Then, wearing the sea as her garment, she cannot yet see the princely Moon either; her face pales in all four directions, her heart grows cold, and she turns in distress. There is a short interval after sunlight fades and before moonlight begins to shine. That moment is personified in this way.

The phrase “her face paled and her heart grew cold” carries two levels of meaning:

> The faces of the directions grew pale; the red-flower eyes
> poured full streams of water, and the whole body grew wet with dew.  
> *(4:5–6)*

Because the Sun has disappeared, the face formed by the directions pales. The flower-eyes shed liquid—that is, flowers release honey. The earth’s body begins to become wet with dew.

When this hour arrives, those separated from their lovers suffer; those not separated rejoice.

Cowherds play the *Mullai* mode on their flutes. Bees place their mouths upon jasmine flowers and blow as though upon instruments.

The breeze scatters flower fragrance everywhere; women light jewelled lamps.

Just as the Pāṇḍyas defeated their enemies even while young, the white crescent defeats the petty rebel called evening and rules the stars. The word *kuṟumpu* means rebellious minor chiefs. Evening is personified as such a rebel; the meaning is that evening begins to pass away.

Those like Mādhavi, joined with their lovers, revel in joy. Those like Kaṇṇaki, separated from their lovers, sink more deeply into sorrow. Kāma moves about in triumphant delight. This is the sequence of events at evening.

Iḷaṅkō devotes an entire canto to this alone. Did he perhaps do so because the epic needed to reach a total of thirty cantos?


---

# 16. Similes and Metaphors

Many similes and metaphors occur in *Cilappatikāram*. If one says “lotus-face” meaning “a face like a lotus,” it is a simile. If one says “the face-lotus,” meaning “the lotus which is the face,” it is a metaphor. Let us examine some examples.

## Similes

### The Canto of Establishing Domestic Virtue

Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki seated together on the bed appeared like the Sun and Moon united:

> Like the sight of the two lights seated together,
> the lights that illumine the whole world encircled by the ancient sea.  
> *(2:30–31)*

The plural sense in “the lights together” indicates two rays or luminaries. However great the claims to equality made by women today, when we consider Kōvalaṉ’s early amorous play and Kaṇṇaki’s modest restraint, we may take Kōvalaṉ as the Sun and Kaṇṇaki as the Moon. Since the two heavenly lights are never actually joined, this is an impossible-object simile, called *abhūta-upamā* in Sanskrit.

The closing *veṇpā* of the canto contains two similes. The couple appeared like Kāma and his wife Rati. They entwined their bodies and floated inseparably in pleasure like a male and female serpent twisting around each other:

> Like two pure serpents joining and immersing in one another,
> they clasped hands like Kāma and his wife …

The serpent simile possesses strong suggestive value.

### The Canto Celebrating the Evening

When a great emperor is absent, cruel minor rulers oppress the people and exact taxes. In the same way, when the Sun disappeared, evening arrived:

> At the painful hour when tribute-bearing subjects suffer,
> placing hands upon their heads,
> when the royal army with its victory is absent
> and intrusive kings take possession of one region
> together with rebellious communities …  
> *(4:8–12)*
>
> evening came and settled upon the prosperous ancient city.  
> *(4:20)*

The Sun is the emperor; evening resembles a petty ruler. When the Sun leaves, evening comes.
