The final *veṇpā* gives another comparison. A king’s umbrella gives cool shade to his subjects and burning heat to his enemies; similarly, the night Moon gives delight to Mādhavi and pain to Kaṇṇaki:

> Shade to those who are joined, scorching to those who are not,
> like the guardian king’s white umbrella,
> the night in which the sky-going Moon spread its light
> and opened flowers revealed itself differently
> to united Mādhavi and to Kaṇṇaki.

It is conventional to describe good rule by saying that people live happily in the shade of the king’s umbrella. This does not mean that the entire land literally lies under one umbrella. Even in common speech people say, “We live only in your shade; you must protect us.” Compare the word “shaded” in *Puṟanāṉūṟu*:

> Even to those singular rulers whose white umbrella
> shaded the sea-girt world without sharing it …  
> *(189:1–2)*

If the umbrella gives coolness to the king’s own people, it is appropriate to say that it gives the opposite—heat—to enemies. In the same way, the Moon gives pleasure to Mādhavi, who is united with Kōvalaṉ, and pain to Kaṇṇaki, who is separated from him. The Moon’s torment of separated lovers appears in innumerable literary works.

### The Canto that Reveals the Nature of the Dream

When Mālati fed milk to the child of her husband’s other wife, the child choked and died. Terrified, she prayed to the gods to restore it. A demoness named Iṭākiṉi swallowed the body, and Mālati lamented “like a peacock struck by thunder”:

> She yearned and wept like a peacock struck by thunder.  
> *(23)*

This simile seems difficult. It is generally said that when rain approaches, peacocks spread their tails and dance. But the line suggests that a peacock is frightened and distressed by thunder. Is that correct?

A hymn from Ñāṉacampantar’s Tiruvaiyāṟu *Tēvāram* comes to mind. Peacocks dance; clouds thunder; monkeys, seeing the dance and hearing the booming sound, fear that rain is coming, climb to the tops of trees, and look at the clouds:

> Women who came in procession danced;
> the *muḻavu* thundered; thinking it rain,
> a few monkeys, confused, climbed trees
> and gazed at the clouds—such is Tiruvaiyāṟu.  
> *(3:1)*

The women in procession are the dancing peacocks; the temple drum is thunder. The monkeys mistake the women’s gait for that of peacocks and the drum for thunder. The literary charm of Ñāṉacampantar’s verse is delightful.

What this hymn suggests is that peacocks dance joyfully when thunder announces rain. There appears to be a contradiction between Iḷaṅkō and Ñāṉacampantar. How is it to be resolved? Perhaps a peacock becomes afraid when thunder is exceptionally violent and continuous. A specialist in birds and animals may be able to answer.

### “Passageway of the World”

In the *Canto of Seeing the Country*, Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki pass through the outer gateway of Pukār. Because merchants and travellers from across the world came and went by that route, Iḷaṅkō calls it the “passageway of the world.” Bombay is called the “Gateway of India”; Pukār’s gateway is honoured as the “Gateway of the World.” The road leading outward begins at the towered gate and resembles a great river beginning in a mountain. The gateway is the mountain; the street is the river:

> Leaving together the passageway of the world,
> which resembled a great river taking its source in a mountain …  
> *(10:26–27)*

Rivers usually rise in mountains. “Taking the mountain as its head” means having the mountain as the place of origin. Compare *Paṭṭiṉappālai*: “Kāviri, with the mountain as her head and the sea as her end” (6). Long streets are similarly likened to rivers in Sangam literature:

> The broad, long street lay like a river.  
> (*Neṭunalvāṭai* 30)

> The broad, long street lay like a river.  
> (*Maturaikkāñci* 359)

Does not “passageway of the world” confer a new distinction upon Pukār?

The mingled cries of many birds sounded like the front of an army commanded by victorious kings:

> The calls of waterfowl, land birds, and every kind of winged creature
> spread as a great confusion of many groups of voices,
> like the battlefront of kings victorious in war.  
> *(117–119)*

The uproar of victory may be understood today from the noise made by candidates and their supporters after an election victory.

### Impermanence of the Body

The Cāraṇa ascetics offer Kauṇti moral instruction. No one can prevent evil karma from yielding evil. As a planted seed inevitably becomes a crop and gives its fruit, good karma must likewise produce good results. As a lamp goes out in an open space swept by strong wind, life leaves the body at the appointed time:

> Powerful karma does not depart when told to depart;
> like the sown seed, it comes forth and reaches its fruit.
> When the hour arrives, none can prevent it.
> Like a great flame in a vast place beaten by fierce wind,
> lives do not remain forever joined to bodies.  
> *(10:171–174)*

Whether desired or not, what must happen will happen. Compare *Nālaṭiyār*:

> Even those who suffer cannot remove what must befall;
> and what must be obtained will likewise come.  
> (*Past Karma*, 4)

### The Form of the Lord of Vēṅkaṭam

The Brahmin from Māṅkāṭu tells Kōvalaṉ that on the summit of Mount Vēṅkaṭam, between the Sun visible on one side and the Moon on the other, Viṣṇu stands like a dark cloud wearing lightning as a garment of new gold and the rainbow as an ornament. In one lotus hand he holds the discus, in the other the white conch; a necklace shines on his chest, and a flower-coloured garment wraps his body:

> On the summit of the lofty mountain Vēṅkaṭam,
> where swollen waterfalls descend,
> the spreading-rayed Sun and the Moon shine,
> rising on either side of the middle space.
> There, like a cloud of lovely hue
> clothed in lightning and adorned with the radiant bow,
> the red-eyed Tall One stands,
> bearing in his graceful lotus hands
> the hostile-destroying discus and milk-white conch,
> wearing a splendid necklace upon his chest
> and shining in a gold-flowered garment.  
> *(11:41–51)*

A similar image appears in *Paripāṭal*:

> Because the season had arrived, like the signs
> of the two different orbs adorning the double sky,
> his hands bore the discus and the curved conch.  
> *(13:7–9)*

The “two different orbs” are Sun and Moon.

The other twin epic, *Maṇimēkalai*, also depicts Sun and Moon together. The woman called Pukār seems to wear a white earring on one side and a gold earring on the other: the Moon shines in the east and the Sun in the west:

> The flower-creeper Pukār, surpassing the world of poets …  
> *(5:109)*
>
> The mature Moon at the eastern side
> and the descending ray at the western side
> were like a silver-white earring and an earring of gold.  
> *(5:119–121)*

An English poem too describes the Moon and Sun visible at once in the evening—Lord Tennyson’s “The Lotos-Eaters.” The warriors of the Greek king Ulysses, returning to Ithaca after conquering Troy in Asia Minor, halt on an island:

> They sat them down upon the yellow sand,
> between the sun and moon upon the shore.  
> *(5:1–2)*

These three comparisons from other works have been introduced through the method of literary comparison to enhance our appreciation of Iḷaṅkō’s poem.

### Transformation of the Landscape

When the Sun’s fierce heat joined summer, the Mullai and Kuṟiñci landscapes lost their nature and became arid Pālai. A fitting simile is offered: the land changed like a country made to suffer when its king and his ministers abandon just governance:

> As a vast land loses royal order
> when the victorious king goes astray
> together with the officers of the crown,
> so, when the lord of summer joined the hot-rayed king
> and both perverted their proper goodness,
> Mullai and Kuṟiñci departed from their order,
> lost their lovely nature, inflicted trembling distress,
> and assumed the form called Pālai.  
> *(11:60–66)*

“Officers of the crown” means ministers and other royal functionaries. Summer is personified as an aged lord; it corresponds to the ministers, while the hot-rayed Sun is the king.

### The Canto of Resting in the Outlying Settlement

Kōcikaṉ sees a withered *mādhavi* creeper and asks whether it too suffers, like the maiden Mādhavi afflicted by Kōvalaṉ’s departure:

> “When Kōvalaṉ left, the great-flower, long-eyed Mādhavi
> endured cruel sorrow; like her,
> stripped of your flowers by this mighty summer,
> you too seem to grieve, O *mādhavi* creeper.”  
> *(13:48–51)*

The woman Mādhavi is compared with the creeper of the same name. It is a fitting simile and a play on words.

Kōcikaṉ tells Kōvalaṉ that because of his departure, his father, mother, and relatives lie disordered like a serpent deprived of its precious gem and a body deprived of life:

> The lord of the two treasures and the lady of the great house
> became like a serpent that had lost its rare gem;
> the close circle of kin, like a body that had lost dear life,
> fell into an ocean of sorrow.  
> *(13:57–60)*

Kōvalaṉ is compared both to the serpent’s jewel and to life. The renowned ancient city itself became distraught like Ayodhyā when Rāma departed:

> Like Ayodhyā deprived of mighty Rāma,
> the renowned ancient city fell into great bewilderment.  
> *(13:65–66)*

Here Kōvalaṉ is compared with Rāma.

### The Canto of the Vision

When Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ camped upon a sandbank beside the great river, mountain people brought many kinds of tribute and waited to present them. Their waiting is compared to the defeated kings who, bearing tribute in submission, waited for an opportunity to see the Cēra:

> Like enemy kings who, finding no opportunity anywhere
> to see the royal lord, stood bearing tribute.  
> *(25:35–36)*

The phrase suggests that they could not obtain an audience even after going to Vañji to pay tribute; learning that the king had gone to view the mountain wealth, they came there too, yet still could not see him quickly or easily. This testifies to the Cēra’s greatness. Even today, do people not wait a long time in queues to see certain eminent persons who think themselves very great?

### The Canto of Taking the Auspicious Sword and Umbrella

When Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ left Vañji to fight his enemies, he resembled Indra leaving his own city to war against the demons:

> Like the lord of heaven leaving his own city
> to advance against the Dānavas,
> he left Vañji.  
> *(26:78–79)*

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ sprang upon his enemies like a lion rising for prey and leaping into a great herd of elephants:

> Like a lion risen in the hunt for prey,
> delighting at the sight of a vast herd of great elephants
> and springing upon it …  
> *(26:188–190)*

### The Moon as the King’s Face

The anklets on Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s feet are said to rub against the crowns of kings he has conquered and enslaved. Yet when he bestows grace upon his own subjects, his face is cool and blossoming. The Moon appeared over the ancient city like that gracious face:

> The flower-rayed Moon appeared for the world to worship,
> like the auspicious face of ankleted Kuṭṭuvaṉ—
> whose feet rub the outer surfaces of conquered crowns—
> when he protects and favours his subjects;
> it showed itself to the famed ancient city and moved on.  
> *(28:37–40)*

It is common to compare a woman’s face to the Moon. Here, in an innovative reversal, the Moon is compared to the Cēra’s face, on account of coolness.

A *Puṟanāṉūṟu* poem by Auvaiyār may be compared. A great elephant fights enemies fiercely, yet behaves gently with village children who wash its white tusks at the waterside. In the same way Atiyamāṉ is terrible to enemies but sweet to poets such as Auvaiyār:

> Like the great elephant that enters the bathing place
> while village children wash its white tusks,
> you are sweet to me, great lord;
> but like its unapproachable musth,
> you are harsh to those who oppose you.  
> *(94)*

How graceful the thought!

### The Canto of Benediction—Prose-and-Song Interlude

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ already intended to go north and bring a stone from the Himalaya for the image. When he was told that northern kings had insulted the southern rulers, his anger flared. This is compared to striking with a short stick a jewelled wheel already rolling by itself, making it run even faster: “like driving a rolling jewelled wheel with a goad” (Canto 29, prose-and-song interlude).

At the end of the work, under *Concluding Statement on the Text*, there is a skilful simile. Just as a long and massive mountain may be made visible in a small mirror, an immense history is compressed into the book called *Cilappatikāram*:

> Like one who reveals a long, great mountain
> within the reflected image of a mirror,
> the meaning of the narrative that extends into *Maṇimēkalai*
> has been displayed: *Cilappatikāram* is complete.  
> *(15–18)*

## Metaphors

### The Returning King

In the evening sky the king who is the Moon appears, drives away the enemy who is dusk, spreads his milk-white rays, and rules the subjects who are stars:

> In the evening sky the white crescent appeared,
> drove away the miserable petty rebellion of dusk,
> spread its milk-white rays without departing from its nature,
> and, in the silver radiance, ruled the stars.  
> *(4:23–26)*

Because evening is called a hostile petty rebellion, the white crescent must be understood as a victorious king. As kings rule people from the earth, the crescent rules the stars from the sky. “The rebellion of evening” is a localized metaphor.

### The Pond as a Woman

The pond-woman possesses the swan as her gait, the fragrance of the water-lily as perfume, the lotus as her red mouth, fine dark sand as hair, bees as bards singing modes, and blue lilies as eyes. Around her resounds the drum formed by bird calls:

> The clear-water pond, with the swan’s soft gait,
> wore the honey-filled fragrance breathed by water-lilies;
> the lotus was her red mouth, cool dark sand her hair.
> Bees with minstrels’ mouths sang the *Nērtiṟam* mode;
> lovely blue lilies opened as her flower-eyes,
> while bird-mouth drums resounded around her …  
> *(4:72–77)*

### Ploughing

The Brahmin Māṭalaṉ ploughs with the plough that is his Vedic tongue and sows the seed of lofty moral counsel in the field that is Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s ear:

> The Brahmin ploughed with his Vedic tongue
> and sowed the lofty meaning in the field of the king’s ear.  
> *(28:187–188)*

### The Maiden Earth

The earth is a woman whose garment is the sea, whose breasts are mountains, whose necklace is the great river, and whose hair is cloud. The Sun rises, removes the dark covering over her, and spreads its rays:

> The maiden earth, whose garment was wave-filled water,
> whose breasts were mountains, whose necklace the great river,
> whose hair was cloud, and whose expanse held broad eyes,
> cast off the covering-sheet of enveloping darkness.
> From the summit of the great eastern mountain
> the Sun appeared and spread blossoming rays
> of radiant light that made the world shine.  
> *(5:1–6)*

### The Unfailing Creeper of the Pāṇḍya Line

The Vaikai is personified as a woman. Flowers swept down from many trees form her garland and girdle; the banks are her hips, sand mounds her breasts, *murukku* petals her red mouth, jasmine her teeth, fish her eyes, and dark fine sand her hair:

> *Kuṭacam*, bamboo, luxuriant *pakaṉṟai*,
> *piṭavam*, *mayilai*, and other mingled trees
> gave flowers that joined everywhere
> as garland and girdle upon her curving banks.
> The broad raised spaces enclosed by them were her hips;
> flower-rich islands heaped with sand rose on either side
> as radiant young breasts facing one another.
> Fallen *murukku* petals were her red mouth;
> cascading jasmine, her ornamented smile;
> long black fish, moving and leaping, her eyes;
> shining dark sand never free of fragrant flowers, her hair.
> Praised upon poets’ tongues, she was the flower-creeper
> of lofty, world-sustaining, unfailing course—
> the Vaikai, the unfailing creeper of the royal line.  
> *(13:157–170)*

The named plants are species of tree or creeper; *vālukam* means a mound of sand; the bright petal is that of the *murukku*. The flower-creeper is the woman-river Vaikai, praised by poets because her water flows without failing. Compare Kampaṉ’s description of the Godāvari as embracing a “cool, righteous course.” The Vaikai belongs to the Pāṇḍya line.

### Disease and Medicine

While warriors remained in the battlefield camp, they suffered by imagining the seductive sidelong glances of their wives. Where are those glances situated? Beneath the bow of the eyebrow, upon the Moon that is the face within the cloud that is hair, stand the eyes—red-lined because they have defeated Kāma’s flower arrows. Now the warriors return victorious from camp. Their wives send their sidelong glances as messengers, awakening love. The eyes that, when remembered during separation, caused the disease of longing now become medicine for that disease and delight their husbands:

> The maidens with flower-like, collyrium-dark eyes,
> their uncontrollable, rounded, striped breasts burning with heat,
> their soft hair spread like cloud scented by *akil*—
> beneath the curved dark bow upon the moon within that cloud,
> their rich sidelong glance, red-streaked after driving away
> the flower arrows of the fish-bannered god,
> became both messenger and medicine.  
> *(28:15–21)*

Cloud is hair, Moon is face, bow is eyebrow, and the sidelong eye is messenger.

The idea that eyes which caused disease in separation become medicine in union recalls a *Tirukkuṟaḷ*:

> In this collyrium-dark eye of hers are two glances:
> one is the glance that causes disease;
> the other is the medicine for that disease.  
> *(1091)*

Some people develop a headache at four in the evening; it disappears after tea. What is the medicine? Tea. What caused the headache? The very same tea—when absent, disease; when obtained, medicine. Wine is perhaps the same; and woman too!

Compare a passage from *Kuṇa Nāṟpatu*:

> It cannot be cured by medicine, nor removed by gems;
> even severe ascetic effort can hardly drive it away.
> Like the clear liquor of honey-buzzing wine,
> which itself becomes the medicine for the illness it caused,
> the fullness-destroying sorrow brought by you
> can be cured by nothing but your grace.

The “clear wine” is liquor; “you” is the heroine. The hero addresses her. Her glance is like pinching the child and then rocking the cradle: its attraction awakens his erotic illness, while its sign of love becomes ambrosial medicine and consoles him.

Did *Kuṇa Nāṟpatu* receive this idea as a gift from the quoted *Tirukkuṟaḷ*? When Iḷaṅkō makes the eye both disease and medicine, is it his own imagination, or another gift from the *Kuṟaḷ*? *Kāṉal Vari* contains a related fancy: only a woman’s breasts can cure the disease caused by her eyes:

> The incurable, abiding sickness caused
> by the straight carp-eyes in the radiant full-moon face
> seems curable only by the soft breasts
> mottled with spreading beauty.  
> *(7:8:3–4)*

“The breasts cure” means that an embrace cures.

In this way Iḷaṅkō polishes and enriches his epic through similes and metaphors.

Simile and metaphor are arts of the people, used even in the speech of those without formal education, and are indispensable ornaments of literature. Some works gain great value from them; poets who use many apt similes may receive the title “Poet of Similes.” But writers who calculate that they must insert a fixed number and force them into the work cannot attain greatness. Similes must flow naturally through the current of thought and give delight. Iḷaṅkō adorns the epic by using them naturally, where needed and in the measure required.


---

# 17. The Place of *Kāṉal Vari* in the Epic

> **Source-completeness notice:** The supplied Project Madurai e-text breaks off during this chapter after the second quoted *Kāviri* song and states “continued in part 2.” The attached Part 2 begins with Chapter 20. The remainder of Chapter 17 is therefore not present in the supplied source. The translation below covers every available paragraph and verse of this chapter.

## The Turning Point

The canto called *Kāṉal Vari* is a turning point in *Cilappatikāram*. Māṭalaṉ the Brahmin tells Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ himself that it was the *Kāṉal Vari* song that finally pressed down the crowned heads of the northern kings Kanaka and Vijaya by making them carry the black stone intended for Kaṇṇaki’s image. Had this song not existed, there would have been no occasion for either the Madurai Kāṇḍam or the Vañji Kāṇḍam.

*Kāṉal* means a grove by the seashore. *Vari* means a particular kind of song. The songs sung by Kōvalaṉ and Mādhavi while seated in the seashore grove are therefore called *Kāṉal Vari*.

The Indra festival was celebrated at Pukār. As usual, Mādhavi gave an elaborate performance of dance and song, and many people watched with delight. Kōvalaṉ could not tolerate it. Once Mādhavi had become exclusively his, he resented her dancing for the pleasure of everyone. This is a trait found in some men: a man may desire any woman he likes, but his own wife must not so much as raise her eyes toward another man. The parents of one prospective bridegroom went to see a girl. He is said to have asked, “Has some other prospective bridegroom already come and looked at her? If so, I do not want her.” Kōvalaṉ seems to have been that bridegroom’s elder brother in spirit. Yet Mādhavi, though born in the courtesan community, was faithful.

Realizing that Kōvalaṉ had become displeased with her, Mādhavi skilfully cooled his anger. Do women need to be taught how? The full-moon season arrived, and the townspeople went to bathe and celebrate by the sea. Mādhavi and Kōvalaṉ too went to the shore. Their very manner of travel naturally foreshadowed separation: Kōvalaṉ rode a horse-like mule, while Mādhavi travelled in a carriage.

In the coastal grove, beneath the shade of a *puṉṉai* tree, they enclosed a space on fresh sand with curtains so that it resembled a private room. A couch was placed inside, and Mādhavi and Kōvalaṉ sat upon it.

Mādhavi took the yāḻ from Vayantamālai’s hand. It had no defect in its resonator, stem, pegs, or strings. Through the eight methods beginning with *paṇṇal* and *parivaṭṭaṇai*, she sounded and examined it. As her fingers moved over the strings, she listened closely to determine whether the eight musical operations, including expansion and shaping, were properly aligned. She did not command Kōvalaṉ to play. Instead, as though merely asking him to begin and establish the rhythm for her, she placed the yāḻ in his hand.

Kōvalaṉ naturally began to perform *Kāṉal Vari*. But the songs appeared to contain signs suggesting that he had a particular woman in mind. He may have inserted such a suggestion only in mischief or play. The substance of the songs is as follows.

## 1. River Songs—“Live Long, Kāviri!”

He first sang: “O Kāviri! Even if the Cōḻa unites with the Ganges, you do not quarrel with him; that is chastity—live long! Even if the Cōḻa joins Kumari, you do not quarrel; that is chastity—live long! Your movement with fresh flood and great sound is the Cōḻa’s prosperity—live long, Kāviri!” The source provides the first two of the three songs corresponding to these ideas:

> Though the lord of the white umbrella garlanded by the Moon,
> upholding the straight sceptre upon his crowned head,
> unites himself with the Ganges,
> do not quarrel—live long, Kāviri!
>
> O carp-eyed one, though he unites with the Ganges,
> your refusal to quarrel has taught me
> that this is the great chastity of noble women.
> Live long, Kāviri!  
> *(Song 2)*

> Though the lord of the enduring white umbrella,
> upholding the unbending straight sceptre,
> unites himself with the virgin Kumari,
> do not quarrel—live long, Kāviri!
>
> O carp-eyed one, though he unites with Kumari,
> your refusal to quarrel has taught me
> that this is the enduring great chastity of women.
> Live long, Kāviri!  
> *(Song 3)*

*The supplied text ends here.*

# 19. The Condition of Kōvalaṉ

> **Source text not present in the supplied attachment.**
>

Part 1 ends during Chapter 17, and the embedded Part 2 begins at Chapter 20. No translation has been invented or reconstructed.

# 20. Kaṇṇaki’s State of Chastity

## The Auspicious Blessing Song

Kaṇṇaki is the character who appears from the beginning of *Cilappatikāram* to its end. She was the daughter of Māṉāykaṉ of Pukār, a man whose generous hand poured wealth like rain. Kaṇṇaki had the form of a golden creeper, the fortune of Lakṣmī dwelling upon a lotus, a celebrated and auspicious beauty, chastity like Arundhatī, exalted qualities worthy of women’s worship, and love for everyone. She was twelve at marriage:

> In Pukār, the city where long-enduring fame and pleasure dwell,
> she was the tender shoot of Māṉāykaṉ’s line,
> whose generous hand equalled the great rain-cloud;
> like the banner of a giving sky, she was twelve years old.
>
> Women worshipped and praised her, saying,
> “She possesses the fortune of the lotus-dwelling goddess,
> a form worthy of fame, and the virtue
> of the faultless northern star.”
> Rich in great qualities and love,
> she bore the abiding name Kaṇṇaki.  
> *(Auspicious Blessing Song, 23–29)*

Storytellers addressing children begin, “Once there was a town. In it lived a father and mother. They had a daughter.” Iḷaṅkō follows the same method: there was a town called Pukār; a man named Māṉāykaṉ lived there; he had a daughter named Kaṇṇaki; when she completed twelve years, he arranged her marriage.

Stories often identify clan names. Iḷaṅkō does so indirectly. Kaṇṇaki’s father is Māṉāykaṉ and Kōvalaṉ’s father Mā Cāttuvāṉ. Both *nāykaṉ* and *cāttuvāṉ* can denote a merchant; with *mā*, each means a great merchant. These may therefore look more like community titles than personal names. Apart from them, no principal figure of *Cilappatikāram* seems to be called by a caste title; “the Brahmin of Māṅkāṭu” is a descriptive phrase of another kind.

Suppose, by way of example, that their personal names had been Muttaiyaṉ Māṉāykaṉ and Vaṭivēlaṉ Mā Cāttuvāṉ. A famous elder of the Chettiar community may be referred to simply as “Chettiar,” without his given name; in a particular setting the title points to that one person. The same occurs with “Mudaliar.” Perhaps Māṉāykaṉ and Mā Cāttuvāṉ function similarly.

One may object that a single clan should have only one title. Yet even among relatives there may be several: Mudaliar and Chettiar; Gounder, Nayakar, and Padaiyachi; and numerous titles among the Mukkulathor. Kaṇṇaki’s and Kōvalaṉ’s related families could likewise have used Māṉāykaṉ and Mā Cāttuvāṉ. If *nāykaṉ* and *cāttuvāṉ* were enough, why add *mā*? It may correspond to present expressions such as “the great farm,” “the great estate,” “the great house,” or “the great wealthy man.”

My uncertainty arises because *mā* occurs in both names. Is it natural to both personal names, or was it artificially added to show that the fathers were equals? In street-play versions, moreover, the names are reversed and altered: Kōvalaṉ’s father is Māṇākkaṉ, resembling Māṉāykaṉ, and Kaṇṇaki’s father Māccōṭṭāṉ, resembling Mā Cāttuvāṉ. Iḷaṅkō names the fathers but not the mothers, which is a deficiency. Boatmen’s songs are said to call Kōvalaṉ’s mother Vaṇṇamālai.

In 1960 I published a novel called *Malar Maṇam*. I named only the fathers of the hero and heroine. A Ramanathapuram scholar who translated it into English told me that I should have named the mothers too. But when even the fathers’ names in the epic are uncertain, how could Iḷaṅkō have known and supplied the mothers’ names?

These observations are not final conclusions. The difficulty largely arises only when the street-play names are compared with the epic and when both names are found to carry *mā* and nearly the same meaning. I leave the matter to scholars.

Kaṇṇaki did not rebuke Kōvalaṉ for abandoning her. She did not return to her parents’ home. When he called her, she did not quarrel but departed with him at once. For this she is praised as chaste like the northern star Arundhatī.

Iḷaṅkō’s phrase “Kaṇṇaki, whose name abides” is apt. *Maṉṉutal* means to remain:

> In this impermanent world, those who sought permanence
> established their fame and then passed away.  
> (*Puṟanāṉūṟu* 165:1–2)

Kaṇṇaki’s name remains today. It will remain as long as *Cilappatikāram*—indeed, as long as Tamil. Since a temple was built for her, its endurance is assured. Iḷaṅkō knew at first hand both her history and the building of the shrine by his elder brother; thus at the very beginning he draws the auspicious opening mark by calling her “Kaṇṇaki, whose name abides.” As Arundhatī remains forever in people’s minds, so will Kaṇṇaki.

## The Canto of Establishing Domestic Virtue

### Praise of Kaṇṇaki’s Beauty

The first canto narrates the marriage. The second describes their household life. Kaṇṇaki and Kōvalaṉ excelled in love and embraced so closely that their garlands mingled, like Sun and Moon joined together. Forgetting themselves in pleasure, Kōvalaṉ gazed upon Kaṇṇaki’s auspicious face with inexhaustible love and praised her beauty:

> The flower-eyed woman and her loving lord …  
> *(11)*
>
> seeing each other, they attained delight and excelled in love …  
> *(25)*
>
> like the sight of the two lights seated together …  
> *(31)*
>
> their garlands mingled, leaving them helpless …  
> *(35)*
>
> with unending love he looked upon her auspicious face,
> and Kōvalaṉ uttered praise beyond all measure.  
> *(36–37)*

The epic first praises the Moon, then the Sun; it first introduces Kaṇṇaki and then Kōvalaṉ. There is a natural correspondence: the Moon’s gentle light precedes the Sun’s fierce light just as Kaṇṇaki precedes Kōvalaṉ. The second canto likewise says “the flower-eyed woman and her loving lord,” and compares them to two luminaries impossibly united.

### Kōvalaṉ’s Love

Some researchers write that Kōvalaṉ had no genuine inward love for Kaṇṇaki. What then is the implication of Iḷaṅkō’s repeated expressions—“loving lord,” “excelled in love,” “looked upon her face with unending love,” “Kaṇṇaki trembled before her lover,” “he entered the forest with his beloved,” “for her lover in the heat of the fierce rays,” and “he embraced at once the carp-eyed, long-eyed beloved”? The intended meaning is plainly that Kōvalaṉ possessed deep love for Kaṇṇaki.

But how did “unending love” end? Here the poet leaves readers to imagine Mādhavi’s power of enchantment, and thus suggests impermanence. Still, the phrase is not false. Kōvalaṉ eventually separated from Mādhavi and returned to Kaṇṇaki with renewed love. People say that a quarrel between husband and wife does not last. Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki reunited; “unending love” is therefore appropriate.

### A Garland of Praise

Kōvalaṉ says in effect: “My precious Kaṇṇaki, you are Lakṣmī. Because the Moon was born from the sea together with Lakṣmī, Śiva has given the crescent from his crown to become your forehead. Kāma’s sugarcane bow has been split into two eyebrows. The narrow middle of Indra’s thunderbolt has become your waist. Murukaṉ’s spear has been divided into your two eyes.”

Poets conventionally compare a woman’s forehead to a crescent, brows to a bow, waist to the narrow middle of an object such as a drum, and eyes to spears. Iḷaṅkō renews these familiar similes by changing the angle: the gods themselves donate their attributes to her. Such transformation of convention displays poetic mastery.

Bhārathidāsaṉ used a comparable method while still a young student. When his teacher, the Puducherry scholar P. A. Periyasāmi Piḷḷai, died, Bhārathidāsaṉ lamented that the teacher had left his power to illuminate their small intelligence to the Sun, his affection to their mothers, his swift poetic power to the ever-sounding sea, his fame to the Himalaya, his patience to the earth, and his sweet speech to the cuckoo. Rather than merely comparing teacher and objects, he imagines the teacher distributing his qualities among them. This resembles Iḷaṅkō’s method.

Kōvalaṉ continues: the peacock, ashamed before Kaṇṇaki’s colour and grace, has fled to the forest; the swan, embarrassed by her gait, hides among flowers. The poor parrot, though humbled by speech blending flute, yāḻ, and ambrosia, remains in her hand to learn sweetness from her.

He asks why her companions burden her with ornaments when the auspicious marriage cord itself is enough. A few flowers would suffice; why a great garland—do the women have some bargain with it? The painted design upon her breasts is enough; why add pearls—do they have some private arrangement with the necklace? Why make her slender waist suffer beneath so many ornaments? Compare the *Tirukkuṟaḷ*:

> What need has she of alien ornaments,
> who possesses a doe-like innocent glance and modesty?  
> *(1089)*

His joking suspicion of a “bargain” between her companions and the garlands gives freshness to the praise.

> O stainless gold! O pearl of the right-spiralling conch!
> O flawless fragrance! O sugarcane! O honey!  
> *(2:73–74)*

These images evoke the five senses. Gold delights sight. A smooth conch-pearl gives pleasure to touch. Fragrance delights the nose. Sugarcane delights the tongue. Honey, associated with “honeyed speech,” delights hearing through words. Kaṇṇaki thus gives joy to all five senses. Compare:

> All five senses—seeing, hearing, tasting,
> breathing fragrance, and knowing by touch—
> are contained in the bright-bangled woman.  
> *(Tirukkuṟaḷ 1101)*

The praise continues:

> Rarely attainable maiden! Medicine for my precious life!
> great, modest daughter of a great merchant house!
> Shall I call you a gem not born in a mountain?
> Ambrosia not born in the wave-filled sea?
> Music not born from the yāḻ?
> O woman with long, dark, falling hair—what shall I call you?
> Thus he praised her in many imperishable expressions.  
> *(75–81)*

Iḷaṅkō calls this “imperishable discourse.” It is indeed a passage that can be quoted by people in every age.

Kaṇṇaki lived with her husband for some years, pleasing her mother-in-law and distinguished in hospitality. Thus the second canto shows her receiving both Kōvalaṉ’s praise and her mother-in-law’s support.

One researcher has claimed that Kōvalaṉ never had sexual relations with Kaṇṇaki. Where, other than the bedchamber, could he have praised her in this fashion? Was she dissolving tamarind for a curry while he delivered these lines? They lived together as husband and wife for several years.

## The Canto Celebrating the Evening

After those years Kōvalaṉ went to Mādhavi. Kaṇṇaki no longer wore anklets, girdle, breast-paint, earrings, collyrium, forehead mark, or oil in her hair. Her face lost the sweat of union and the smile meant for Kōvalaṉ. She wore nothing beyond the auspicious marriage cord and became grief embodied:

> From her lovely small red feet the jewelled anklets went;
> from the soft-clothed waist the girdle departed.
> She drew no vermilion design upon the courtyard of her breasts
> and delighted in no ornament beyond the auspicious marriage emblem.
> Casting off heavy earrings, she let her ears hang bare;
> small beads of sweat left her moon-bright face;
> her long red-carp eyes forgot collyrium;
> her coral-radiant brow lost its mark;
> Kōvalaṉ lost the gleam of her white smile;
> her dark, dense hair forgot its dressing of oil.
> Such was helpless-hearted Kaṇṇaki.  
> *(47–57)*

She lived almost like a widow. Only the marriage cord announced that her husband lived. “Kōvalaṉ lost her white radiant smile” implies that she smiled only before him. Sweat appears on the face during union; now the absence of union removes it. This was regarded as the conduct of a chaste woman separated from her husband. One cannot expect the same today.

## The Canto of Seeing the Country

### Kaṇṇaki’s Delicacy

After travelling only one *kātam* from Pukār toward Madurai, Kaṇṇaki asked how far Madurai remained. Kōvalaṉ smiled and said it was near—“five or six *kātams*”—though the full distance was about thirty:

> Her creeper-like waist bent; her feet ached;
> her fragrant, abundant hair stirred as she breathed in short gasps.
> Her little teeth shone through childish speech:
> “Which of these is ancient Madurai?”
> He laughed and said, “Beyond our broad country,
> O fragrant-haired one, it is near—only five or six *kātams*.”  
> *(38–43)*

Extremely delicate, she had barely covered one of thirty *kātams* before waist and feet hurt and she panted. With a helpless false smile and childlike words, she looked at each settlement: “Is this Madurai? If not, how far?” Kōvalaṉ laughed with pity and disguised “thirty” as “six times five” or as “five or six,” lest the true distance overwhelm her. The scene contains a hint to be explained later. Her delicacy becomes still clearer if we imagine her walking with a hand upon Kōvalaṉ’s shoulder.

A literary parallel occurs in Kampaṉ’s *Rāmāyaṇam*. Before Hanumān goes in search of Sītā, Rāma gives identifying memories. One is Sītā’s innocent question when they left the city for the forest: before even passing the outer gate, she asked where the forest was and how far away:

> On the day when the long-crowned king’s son,
> bearing his father’s command, abandoned full wealth
> and began the road, before they had left
> the commanded city and its gate,
> she asked, “Where is the forest?”—tell her that too.  
> (*Recognition Episode*, 61)

Kaṇṇaki, like Sītā, was delicate. Such questions are natural to womanly innocence and childhood.

### Kaṇṇaki’s Trembling

While Kauṇti, Kaṇṇaki, and Kōvalaṉ rested in a grove, a wicked man and a courtesan arrived. Seeing the beautiful pair, they asked who they were. Kauṇti said, “My children.” The mockers asked whether brother and sister could be husband and wife. Hearing the vile words, Kaṇṇaki tightly covered the inner openings of her ears and trembled before Kōvalaṉ:

> Hearing the evil speech, she buried the openings of her ears;
> Kaṇṇaki trembled before her beloved.  

People cover their ears at shameful words. The poem does not merely say that she covered the ears, but that she “buried the inner ear,” closing it as completely as an object buried in earth, lest even a fragment enter.

Why did Kōvalaṉ not tremble? Social convention treats a woman’s relation with a man other than her husband as more humiliating than a man’s relation with another woman. Kauṇti too understood the insult as directed against Kaṇṇaki, not Kōvalaṉ, and cursed the pair:

> “They seem to have mocked my flower-garlanded maiden;
> become old jackals in the thorn-filled forest!”—
> such was the ascetic curse uttered by Kauṇti.  
> *(231–233)*

The circumstance reveals the gendered social inferiority imposed upon women.

### Kaṇṇaki’s Compassion

When the two became jackals and howled, Kaṇṇaki and Kōvalaṉ asked that they be restored. Women often show greater pity in such circumstances, saying “Alas, poor thing!” at news of suffering. Even when both tremble at the howl, the poet names Kaṇṇaki first:

> The fragrant-flowered maiden and the young lord trembled.  
> *(236)*

Her trembling should be read here not as fear but compassion.

## Kaṇṇaki’s Theology

### The Canto that Reveals the Nature of the Dream

Tēvanti, Kaṇṇaki’s Brahmin friend, tells the abandoned and dream-troubled woman: “Your husband does not hate you. In a former birth you failed to perform the vow required for protecting a husband. This is its result. Bathe in the pools called Sōmakuṇṭam and Sūriyakuṇṭam near Kāviri’s confluence, then worship at Kāma’s temple. You will live with your husband without separation in this life and join him in bliss in the next. Separated women act so; let us do the same.” Kaṇṇaki replies that such bathing and worship are not their custom:

> To the ornamented woman who said, “Let us bathe,”
> the fine-bangled woman replied, “It is not worthy.”  
> *(63–64)*

Many will recall the *Tirukkuṟaḷ*:

> She worships no god but rises worshipping her husband;
> when she says, “Rain,” the rain will fall.  
> *(55)*

Modern rationalists reject the usual meaning and explain it anew: such a wife is as useful as a rain that would fall upon command—if such rain existed. That reading makes the woman a metaphorical rain. Such a woman would be welcome; no one denies it. But Vaḷḷuvar did not compose the couplet with this modern interpretation. He intended the traditional meaning.

Tiruttakkatēvar also describes wives worshipping husbands in *Cīvakacintāmaṇi*: they die if he commands death, cease ornament when he departs, never utter Kāma’s name, and live worshipping the husband; like Lakṣmī upon a honeyed flower, they relieve the distress of the man they join.

Vaḷḷuvar certainly knew that no woman, however virtuous, can literally command rain. The revolutionary point is: do not cling to a god while neglecting the household; attend to the family. “Rain when she says rain” is hyperbole. Elsewhere he says that the earth-woman laughs at the idle poor (1040) and that the god himself ties up his garment and runs ahead to help the person determined to raise his family (1023). The earth does not literally laugh; a god does not literally tuck up clothing. These are literary fancies. The husband-worship couplet is similar.

Yet can rationalists accept without question that Vaḷḷuvar tells the wife to worship the husband but nowhere tells the husband to worship the wife? I too am a kind of rationalist. Whoever we are, we should speak and write impartially.

The *Cilappatikāram* passage shows Kaṇṇaki’s theology: she honoured her husband as her deity.

Iḷaṅkō is skilled at arranging an apt dramatic coincidence. At the very moment Kaṇṇaki tells Tēvanti that she does not worship another god, Kōvalaṉ—having left Mādhavi—is entering the doorway. A maid announces him:

> After the fine-bangled woman replied to the jewelled woman,
> “Such bathing is not worthy,”
> a slender-waisted maid at the long-guarded doorway said,
> “Our Kōvalaṉ has come.”  
> *(63–66)*

During the same conversation Tēvanti had divined, “You are not rejected by your husband.” Like the proverbial coincidence of a crow alighting and a palmyra fruit falling, Kōvalaṉ returned moments after her prediction. The literary art deserves enjoyment.

## The Hunter’s Song

### Kaṇṇaki’s Modesty

On the road beyond Uṟaiyūr, Kauṇti, Kaṇṇaki, and Kōvalaṉ rested at a shrine of Aiyai. Cāliṉi, priestess of the hunter community, became possessed and praised Kaṇṇaki:

> “Is she not the noble woman of Koṅku,
> the lady of the western mountain,
> the maiden of southern Tamilakam,
> the shoot born from accomplished penance,
> the peerless great gem risen for the world,
> the splendid jewel?”—
> thus she spoke in divine possession.  
> *(47–50)*

Through Cāliṉi’s prophecy Iḷaṅkō hints that Kaṇṇaki will soon become a deity. A possessed oracle was an apt mouth for knowledge of the future.

Kaṇṇaki became deeply embarrassed. Saying that the wise old woman had spoken in bewilderment about one so insignificant, she withdrew behind the broad back of Kōvalaṉ and allowed a new little smile to bud:

> “The wise old woman has spoken in delusion,” she said;
> shrinking behind the broad back of the husband hard to regain,
> she stood as a new smile began to bud.  
> *(51–53)*

Some people never allow others to speak and talk only of their own greatness; when others praise them, they swell with pleasure. People of high character do not proclaim themselves and, when praised, modestly deny that they deserve it. Kaṇṇaki belongs to this second class. As the saying goes, “the eight-span body became one span,” she made herself small behind her husband’s back. He is “the husband hard to regain” because she had now recovered the man long separated from her. *Viruntu* means newness and *mūral* a smile. Because she had not smiled for years, this smile was “new.” The old proverb said, “When a woman smiles, it is over; when tobacco is spread, it is over.” Accordingly Iḷaṅkō does not make the smile fully flower; it merely buds. Modesty, innocence, fear, and reserve were perhaps prescribed for women with Kaṇṇaki in mind.

## The Splendid Deity

When Kauṇti entrusts Kaṇṇaki to Mātari, she introduces her with praise: the earth has never known the touch of this young woman’s coloured feet. Yet she has endured the fierce Sun, tongue parched, walking over stony ground and accepting trembling hardship for her beloved. Ignoring her own pain, the flower-creeper labours for her husband’s good. She is a divine woman who has undertaken the indispensable duty of chastity. “We have seen no splendid deity other than her”:

> The earth-maiden has never known
> the coloured little feet of this young creeper who came with me.
> In the fierce heat of the harsh rays,
> for her beloved she endured trembling distress,
> withered until her tongue was dry,
> and, not regarding her own suffering,
> this noble flower-creeper laboured for him.
> Other than this deity who has assumed
> the indispensable burden of chastity
> required of loving women,
> we have seen no deity of such splendour.  
> *(137–144)*

“The earth has not known her feet” means she had never walked outside the house; when she travelled, she used a vehicle. The phrase shows both delicacy and wealth.

The *Tirukkuṟaḷ* says:

> She who guards herself, protects the man who took her,
> preserves worthy speech, and never falters—is a wife.  
> *(56)*

Kaṇṇaki protected Kōvalaṉ but did not protect herself; she sacrificed self-interest for him. Vaḷḷuvar calls a wife the “companion of life”; Iḷaṅkō here calls her the “sweet companion.” The word “indispensable” in “the indispensable duty of chastity” corresponds to the meaning of the Sanskrit-derived “important.” Tamil purists use *iṉṟiyamaiyāmai*, indispensability; Iḷaṅkō teaches the usage here.

Elsewhere he calls her “the young woman of sprouting white teeth and mature wisdom” (15:202)—a woman who obtained great understanding at a young age.

## Kaṇṇaki’s Final Domestic Life

At Mātari’s house in Madurai, fine rice and many vegetables were given to Kaṇṇaki for cooking. She cut the vegetables on the curved cutting-blade. Her delicate fingers reddened; sweat fell from her face; smoke from the newly lit stove reddened her eyes. Mātari’s daughter Aiyai first kindled the fire with straw and remained nearby to help. Aiyai assisted, but Kaṇṇaki herself cooked according to the skill she possessed:

> As she cut and prepared many fresh vegetables
> with the curved-mouthed cutting tool,
> her soft fingers reddened, her auspicious face sweated,
> and her red eyes grew redder in the smoke-blackened kitchen.
> With Aiyai, who had kindled the straw fire,
> she cooked for her beloved with the culinary skill she knew.  
> *(29–34)*

Her fingers reddened because they were unaccustomed to cutting. Perhaps the English named okra “lady’s finger” after seeing such delicate fingers! *Koṭuvāy kuyam* is the curved cutting-board blade; *maṭaimai* is culinary art. Cooking must not be dismissed as simple: treatises existed for it. *Maṇimēkalai* refers to “the matter of the cookery treatise” (2:22).

Both her natal and marital homes were rich and would have employed servants; had she practised cooking earlier, fingers and eyes would not redden so quickly. Large logs do not catch at once; small twigs or straw are lit first. Appar’s hymn on Tiruvāṉaikkā says:

> When one dies, no one comes to help;
> with small firewood they kindle the flame and depart.

Though a funeral pyre uses large logs, the body is larger still, and small wood begins the fire. Here Aiyai appropriately uses straw, abundant in a cowherd household.

The phrase “cooked for her beloved” is meaningful. She did not cook for herself but for Kōvalaṉ, wishing to prepare his food with her own hand.

When it was ready, Kaṇṇaki seated him on an artfully woven palmyra mat supplied by Mātari’s house. She brought water in a clay vessel and cleansed his feet, sprinkled and smoothed the earth before him, spread the inner leaf of a young plantain, served the meal, and said, “My lord, eat the ambrosia”:

> After the prosperous man sat upon the crafted seat,
> beautifully woven by a skilful woman
> from the fair white fibre of palmyra grass,
> with her fragrant-flower hand she received
> the water from her beloved’s feet in a fired-clay bowl.
> As though removing the earth-maiden’s faintness,
> she sprinkled water and smoothed the ground with her hand,
> spread the inner leaf of an unfruited plantain,
> and said, “My lord, eat the ambrosia here.”  
> *(35–43)*

The woven mat shows that women already practised small household crafts. Kaṇṇaki’s washing of her husband’s feet reflects, to some extent, female subordination; it was the fashion of the time. The *cuṭumaṇ maṇṭai* is a kiln-fired clay bowl. Its mention suggests that she had been accustomed at home to gold or silver vessels, while Mātari’s household used clay.

The grammar allows two interpretations: she may have taken water from the vessel and rubbed his feet, or placed his feet above the vessel so that the poured water fell into it. In one house, after I ate at a table, the hosts placed a broad brass plate before me, poured water over my hands above it, and carried away the collected water. From that experience I favour the second possibility.

Comparable foot-washing occurs in Kampaṉ and *Periyapurāṇam*. In the *Kiṭkintā Kāṇṭam*, the ascetic woman Cuyampirapā, hearing that Hanumān’s party sought Sītā for Rāma, rejoices that her penance has borne fruit, washes their feet, and feeds them ambrosial food:

> Hearing them, she rejoiced: “My faultless penance
> has this very moment shown me liberation.”
> She washed their feet, lovingly fed them
> sweet food tasting like ambrosia,
> and spoke words that cooled their hearts.  
> *(56)*

In *Periyapurāṇam*, Appūti Nāyaṉār brings Appar home, washes his feet, and sprinkles that water upon himself and his family:

> He caused the sage to enter the house;
> with flower-adorned water he washed the feet before him,
> sprinkled it upon them all,
> and their hearts became full.  
> *(1807)*

Cuyampirapā and Appūti performed the act for honoured guests; Kaṇṇaki did it for the husband she revered.

The line comparing her sprinkling to “removing the earth-maiden’s faintness” is an example of the figure in which the poet attributes an imagined intention to an action. Water is still sprinkled beneath a meal leaf. It cleans the ground, deters ants from crossing the wet boundary, and keeps the leaf from suddenly sliding into a neighbour’s. Since water is sprinkled on a fainting person’s face, the poet imagines Kaṇṇaki reviving the Earth. Perhaps the Earth had fainted because she knew Kōvalaṉ would be killed within hours.

The “young plantain” is one that has not yet borne a bunch. The inner leaf is clean, broad, golden, and untouched by bird droppings; in common speech it is the *talai-vāḻai ilai*, the prime plantain leaf.

People judge a feast by the grandeur of its leaf. A comic story says that a man boasted of a rich household’s enormous leaf: the dishes at the far edge were beyond reach, so a ladle was provided to pull them closer. Another replied that at a zamindar’s feast the leaf was still larger: even the ladle could not reach, so he had to stand up and walk to fetch the dishes. The story measures hospitality by leaf-size. Mātari’s people therefore supplied a generous leaf for Kōvalaṉ.

Kaṇṇaki addresses him as *Aṭikaḷ*, “revered lord.” In dramas influenced by Sanskrit, a wife called her husband *prabho* or *prāṇa-nātha*; later Tamil drama used *attāṉ*. Kaṇṇaki uses the loftier Tamil word. This meal is her final act of household life.

A parallel in *Periyapurāṇam* describes Appūti sending his eldest son, named Tirunāvukkaracu, to cut a golden young plantain leaf for Appar’s meal. Though bitten by a snake, the boy brings it before collapsing. The mention of a young plantain leaf invites comparison with *Cilampu*.

## Kaṇṇaki’s Unchanging Heart

After Kōvalaṉ ate, Kaṇṇaki folded and gave him betel. He lamented his offences against his parents and wife: “I gave you no good, only suffering. Yet when I said, ‘Come, let us go to Madurai,’ you forgot my fault and came.” Kaṇṇaki replied:

“I did not grieve because I lacked personal pleasure during your absence. I grieved because, without you, I could not give to the righteous, honour Brahmins, receive ascetics, and welcome guests according to ancient excellence. I never displayed resentment before your parents. Seeing that I suffered because their son had left, they comforted me with loving words. To please them I smiled falsely. They understood the illness and pain hidden in that smile and grieved inwardly. My sorrow is that your conduct created such a condition. Since my life is one of unwavering attachment to you, I rose and came when you called.”

> I lost the privilege of giving to the righteous,
> honouring Brahmins, meeting ascetics,
> and receiving guests according to ancient excellence.
> The great lady who bore you and the renowned head of the house,
> the lord of vast wealth and enduring distinction,
> cast aside the anger that did not remain before them;
> with hearts rich in love they mingled words of grace
> and praised me. In the false smile with which I answered,
> the concealed illness and suffering seemed suddenly revealed,
> and their inner hearts grieved.
> You practised conduct unworthy of honour.
> Yet because mine is a life of an unchanging heart,
> when you called, I rose and came.  
> *(71–83)*

Kaṇṇaki does not lament the loss of her own pleasure, but the loss of opportunities to benefit others.

### A Note on Ordinary Life

In an ordinary family much else might have happened. Kōvalaṉ’s parents might have blamed Kaṇṇaki as an unlucky woman who brought misfortune upon their son and told her to return to her mother. Her own parents might have said, “We unknowingly married her to the companion of a courtesan; enough of her ruin,” and taken her home. Kaṇṇaki herself might have gone; it was the same town.

Instead all act with generosity. Kōvalaṉ’s parents treat her like a daughter. She conceals pain before them with a false smile. Yet “the beauty of the inner mind appears in the face,” and “as crystal shows what is placed beside it, the face shows what hardens the heart” (*Tirukkuṟaḷ* 706). They understand and grieve inwardly while maintaining an outward appearance of normality so that she will not notice. The phrase “their inner hearts grieved” is important.

Was her smile the very laughter Vaḷḷuvar recommends—“Laugh when adversity comes”? Iḷaṅkō’s account may differ from ordinary life. Perhaps everyone openly lamented; perhaps her parents invited her home and her in-laws advised her to go; perhaps she firmly refused. Who now knows what happened?

Still, Iḷaṅkō’s version is appropriate because he is not a historian but a literary author. A historian should record events without doubt or imagination. A literary author may use the event as a base and imagine what ought to happen and what society should follow. Here the implied teaching is that when a son abandons his wife, his parents must not scold or torment their daughter-in-law. They should say, “Do not fear. Your husband will return. We are here and will protect you to the end.” The daughter-in-law should remain composed. This is the better conduct.

## “Lamp of the Wide World”

Before leaving for the market with the anklet, Kōvalaṉ praises her:

> Leaving the chief kin of the household,
> the young attendants, the company of servants,
> and the circle of companions,
> you took as your great support modesty, innocence,
> the fame praised by the good, and cherished chastity;
> you came with me and removed my sorrow here.
> O gold! O creeper! O flower-adorned woman!
> image of modesty! lamp of the wide world!
> shoot of chastity! treasure of beauty!
> Taking one anklet from your little feet,
> I shall go, exchange it, and return;
> remain without bewilderment.  
> *(84–93)*

In Pukār she left four supports: principal relatives such as parents and parents-in-law; personal attendants; female servants of several functions; and her circle of friends. She brought four others: modesty, innocent reserve, the praise of the good, and protecting chastity.

Kōvalaṉ thinks his sorrow has ended, though it is about to increase. He loses himself calling her gold and creeper. “Lamp of the wide world” hints that she, formerly the lamp of a household, will become a divinity lighting the world. Literature often calls women lamps of the house:

> The radiant-browed woman who was the lamp of the house.  
> (*Puṟanāṉūṟu* 314)

> Women are the illumination of the house.  
> (*Nāṉmaṇikkaṭikai* 105)

*Maṟṟi varuvaṉ* means “I will sell or exchange it and return,” but also hints that he will return transformed—as a corpse. “Do not be bewildered” contains the hint that she will soon be overwhelmed by bewilderment.

## Kaṇṇaki’s Heroic Wrath

Four cantos—*The Garland of Sorrow*, *The Song of Circling the City*, *The Canto of the Lawsuit*, and *The Garland of the Vow*—display Kaṇṇaki’s heroic emotion and action. She hears of Kōvalaṉ’s death; walks through the streets proclaiming her vow; defeats the Pāṇḍya king in argument so completely that king and queen die; and twists off her left breast and burns the city.

People say a story may have neither head nor foot. Not every detail of *Cilappatikāram* can be accepted literally. A city does not burn because a breast is torn off and thrown. Perhaps sympathizers set fire to the quarter of the goldsmith whose action caused Kōvalaṉ’s death. Nothing can be stated with certainty. The injustice was grave, but burning an entire city is disproportionate. The episode may be an imaginative construction making Kaṇṇaki a goddess of heroic wrath.

## A Deficiency in Kaṇṇaki

After considering her greatness, one cannot ignore a possible deficiency. She lacked the power to keep Kōvalaṉ from going to Mādhavi, or at least from remaining there so long.

Some people dislike breakfast at home and go to a hotel to graze upon idli, vadai, puri, potato, pongal, sambar, chutney, kadappa, and adai curry. Kōvalaṉ’s conduct was similar. Perhaps Kaṇṇaki looked like a woman dressed in the austere form of Māriyammaṉ! Kōvalaṉ, seeking glamour, surrendered his heart to Mādhavi’s dancing, singing, swaying, shaking, and glitter. Alas, Kaṇṇaki’s life was ruined.

Some add another criticism. When Kōvalaṉ returned, she should have acted offended and made herself difficult. Instead she apparently thought he had nothing left to give Mādhavi and immediately offered the anklets; when he proposed Madurai, she secretly left with him. This, critics say, reveals lack of worldly skill.

Yet her immediate departure has a reasonable explanation. A scene from *Maṇimēkalai* helps by partial analogy.

### An Evening Scene of Swans

A male and female swan played in a lotus pond. At evening, a lotus closed around the female and hid her. The male tore open the petals, rescued her, and climbed with her onto the high frond of a tall coconut tree:

> When the lotus enclosed the female
> with whom the male swan had played till weariness,
> he tore the flower covering apart,
> took his mate, and climbed the high frond
> of a lofty, great coconut palm.  
> *(123–126)*

They left because pond and lotus had become threatening. They did not settle on a low shrub but sought distance from the water, choosing the tallest kind of tree, then the upward-reaching high frond. The poem does not say they “sat”; it says they “climbed,” preserving upward movement.

Kaṇṇaki acted like those swans. She wanted to take her husband far from Mādhavi, the threat to her household. If they remained in Pukār, the old spirit might climb the drumstick tree again—that is, Kōvalaṉ might return to Mādhavi. A proverb says, “One who has tasted food and one who has tasted woman will not remain quiet.” Her fear was natural. She also left secretly because others might prevent the journey.

Therefore her refusal to quarrel or resist cannot simply be called a defect. She had once let the fortress fall; would she be deceived again? She could not foresee what happened in Madurai. That was accidental, and she was not responsible for nature’s command.

It remains regrettable that she could not stop Kōvalaṉ at the beginning. Perhaps she tried greatly and he remained obstinate and unreformed. Who can say otherwise?


---

# 21. Kauṇti Aṭikaḷ’s Sense of Duty

## A Companion

“Do not travel a long road without a companion,” says Ativīrarāma Pāṇḍiyaṉ’s *Veṟṟivēṟkai* (78). Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki secretly left Pukār in darkness and had to travel roughly thirty *kātams*—about three hundred miles—to Madurai. Their rare and invaluable companion was Kauṇti Aṭikaḷ.

## The Title *Aṭikaḷ*

Kauṇti was a Jain woman ascetic who lived at a place called Kauṇti’s monastery, one *kāvatam* west of Pukār. It was customary to call exalted men or women *Aṭikaḷ*. Though Kōvalaṉ had behaved badly, Kaṇṇaki regarded him as exalted and addressed him, “Aṭikaḷ, eat the ambrosia” (*Canto of the Killing Ground*, 43).

## Historical Fact or Literary Invention?

Was Kauṇti’s companionship a historical event that could truly have occurred, or a character invented by Iḷaṅkō to make the narrative move conveniently? The couple had barely crossed Pukār and the Kāviri and travelled one *kāvatam* when they met a guide who remained with them all the way to Madurai. Some may suspect that it looks too neatly arranged. One scholar has said that Iḷaṅkō made the companion a woman for Kaṇṇaki’s sake and an ascetic for Kōvalaṉ’s. Did he fear that, if the female Kauṇti were not a renunciant, Kōvalaṉ—who had acquired a “taste for women”—might try his hand with her too?

Yet the event is believable. Long-distance travellers naturally converse and join company with someone they meet on the road. The couple passed the place where Kauṇti stayed. She asked who they were, where they were going, and why. Kōvalaṉ told her. At first she objected: their aim was unsound, and the delicate woman could not cross the harsh forest road. Kōvalaṉ would not yield. Kauṇti then said, “Go, if you must. You say you are travelling to Madurai. I too have long wished to go there, hear the works of the great, and worship the Arhat. I shall come.” Thus all three proceeded together. We may reasonably believe that this occurred rather than being wholly imagined.

## Fitness of the Journey

A husband and wife alone on a very long road are not secure; a honeymoon is not the situation in question. To speak plainly, when a woman must withdraw along the road to relieve herself, Tamil custom does not consider a man the appropriate attendant; another woman should accompany her. Kauṇti met this need. Likewise, when a husband must go away briefly, he should leave his wife with a woman. *Cilampu* says that Kōvalaṉ left Kaṇṇaki with Kauṇti once to drink water and once to attend to his morning needs. Kauṇti was old and dignified enough for Kōvalaṉ to bow at her feet. The journey of the three was therefore appropriate, useful, and safe.

## Kauṇti’s Anger

While they rested in a grove, a degraded man and a courtesan mocked Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki. When Kauṇti said, “They are my children,” the pair asked whether brother and sister could become husband and wife. Enraged, Kauṇti cursed them into jackals. At the couple’s request, she later limited the curse and allowed their restoration.

Even if we do not literally believe in transformation into jackals, we may accept that Kauṇti became angry and fiercely rebuked them.

Some critics argue that such anger is incompatible with renunciation and that Kauṇti was not a true ascetic. Yet her title *Aṭikaḷ* is attached precisely because she was one. A solution may be found by reflecting on the *Tirukkuṟaḷ* chapter “The Greatness of Renunciants”:

> The anger of those who have climbed and stand
> upon the mountain called virtue
> is difficult to withstand even for a moment.  
> *(29)*

## Kauṇti’s Refutation

A Brahmin from Māṅkāṭu explained beliefs of his own tradition. Kauṇti replied by stating the Jain position and rejecting his claims. This shows that she possessed skill in argument.

## Kauṇti’s Persuasive Speech

> The world will swiftly listen and act
> if it obtains people capable of speaking
> in orderly and pleasing fashion.  
> (*Tirukkuṟaḷ* 648)

In the *Canto of Seeing the City*, Kauṇti speaks persuasively and consoles Kōvalaṉ. Bowing to her, he says: “I departed from right conduct, lost my wealth, and brought Kaṇṇaki in suffering to a strange city. Let her remain under the protection of your sacred feet while I enter Madurai, find members of my merchant community, and arrange our future. Will this burden you?”

Kauṇti answers: “Kōvalaṉ, all this is the fruit of karma. The great have made the mouth a drum and the tongue its beating-stick and proclaimed endless moral instruction. The learned perform good acts; those who slip from life’s path suffer the pain of separation, union, and Kāma. All is karma. Why grieve over what has passed? Many have suffered like you. Rāma searched the forest in anguish after losing Sītā. Naḷa gambled away his kingdom, entered the forest with his wife, and abandoned her there at night. Both suffered separation. You still have your wife beside you. Compared with Rāma and Naḷa, you are greatly fortunate. Do not grieve.” Thus she skilfully turned the comparison toward consolation.

Her persuasion appears again when Mātari, returning from an offering of milk to the local goddess, worships Kauṇti in the settlement outside Madurai. Kauṇti wants to send Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki into the city before dark but worries who will take them and where they will stay. She recognizes that Mātari, a cowherd woman whose harmless occupation is protecting cattle, is fit to receive persons seeking refuge.

She says: “Mātari, these two belong to the merchant community. Their names are Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki. Kōvalaṉ’s father Mā Cāttuvāṉ is immensely wealthy in Pukār and renowned throughout the country. The merchants of Madurai know him. If they learn that this is his son, they will rejoice as though finding a rare treasure, welcome the couple, and install them in a fine mansion. Until they reach the merchant quarter, I give them into your protection, woman of the pastoral community.”

> Mātari, listen. If the merchants of his clan
> hear the name of this maiden’s husband’s father,
> they will welcome them like people who have found rare treasure,
> receive him with the broad dark-eyed woman,
> and install them in a fragrant house.
> Until they enter the home of those possessors of great wealth,
> I have given them as refuge to you, woman of the herding clan.  
> *(125–130)*

Kauṇti’s choice is excellent. Her manner of naming Mā Cāttuvāṉ contains subtleties. Instead of pointing to Kōvalaṉ and saying “his father,” she points through Kaṇṇaki: “this woman’s husband’s father.” This indirectly affirms Kaṇṇaki’s primacy as heroine. Yet saying simply “this woman’s father-in-law” might diminish Kōvalaṉ, so the longer phrasing also introduces them explicitly as husband and wife.

In some families a child gains honour from the father and must be introduced as “so-and-so’s son.” In others a father gains honour from the child. Kōvalaṉ’s case is pitiable: he is identified both as Mā Cāttuvāṉ’s son and Kaṇṇaki’s husband. The couplets on a father making his son foremost in an assembly (67) and a son causing people to ask what penance his father performed to beget him (70) have little work here.

Kauṇti also creates prestige: “Do not think lightly of them. Once Madurai’s merchants know that Mā Cāttuvāṉ’s son and daughter-in-law have arrived, they will snatch them away in eagerness. I entrust such high-born people to you, a pastoral woman without considering high and low; this is a great opportunity for you.”

She further wins Mātari’s heart: “Bathe Kaṇṇaki, mark her forehead, adorn her with flowers and clean clothing, and become her companions, guard, and mother. Her feet have never touched earth, yet for her husband she has crossed the road in anguish. We have seen no deity other than this woman of chastity. Do you not know that in a land where such women live, rain does not fail, prosperity does not diminish, and the king’s victory and just sceptre do not falter?”

> Other than this deity who has undertaken
> the burden of chastity, we have seen no splendid god.
> In a country where chaste women live,
> the sky does not lie, prosperity does not fail,
> and the victory of the long-land’s kings does not decay.  
> *(143–147)*

Science cannot accept that rain and prosperity depend on chaste women. Kauṇti may be using hyperbole to win Mātari, like Vaḷḷuvar’s saying that rain falls when the husband-worshipping wife commands it. The epic’s introductory prose also makes the surprising claim.

## The Merit of Protecting Refugees

Kauṇti gives Mātari an example. In a grove near Kāvirippūmpaṭṭiṉam, Cāraṇa ascetics taught lay Jains. A celestial being appeared with one hand still resembling a black-fingered monkey’s. Asked for his history, an ascetic explained:

The wife of Cāyalaṉ, bearer of the title Eṭṭi, fed every visitor. One day she served an ascetic. A starving monkey ate his leftovers and drank the washing water, then gazed longingly at him. Moved by compassion, he entrusted it to her as though it were a child. She protected it well and, after its death, made gifts in its name. The monkey was reborn as the son of King Uttara Kauttaṉ in Vāraṇavāci of the Central Country. The prince made many gifts, died at thirty-two, and became the celestial being now present. Through the merit of protecting the monkey, Cāyalaṉ and his wife attained liberation.

“Understand from this the greatness of guarding one who seeks refuge. Now take Kaṇṇaki and Kōvalaṉ quickly to your house,” Kauṇti concludes.

Her association with them was not a fleeting “railway friendship.” Her responsible effort to protect companions met on the road deserves the highest praise.

## Kauṇti’s Religious Position

Kauṇti’s deity is the Arhat; she belongs to Jainism. One may say that Iḷaṅkō uses her to convey Jain teachings. Several internal signs support this.

When she learns their destination, she says that she has long wished to go to fault-free Madurai in the good southern Tamil land, hear the moral teaching of great Cāraṇas who have abandoned violent doctrine, and praise the Omniscient Arhat:

> Because I have long held a united desire
> to go to fault-free Madurai in the good southern Tamil country,
> hear the moral teaching of stainless learned ones
> who have abandoned doctrines of violence,
> and praise the Omniscient One,
> I too shall come.  
> *(Canto of Seeing the Country, 56–60)*

She carries a begging bowl, sling at the shoulder, and peacock-feather whisk:

> Kauṇti Aiyai took the faultless begging bowl,
> the sling upon her shoulder, and the peacock-feather whisk.  
> *(98–99)*

These are customary Jain ascetic implements.

When Cāraṇa ascetics teach in a grove, all three fall at their feet. The ascetic says that worshipping the Arhat ends rebirth and recites many names: Omniscient, Righteous, Beyond the Boundary of Knowledge, Self-controlled, Jina, Perfected One, Blessed One, First of Dharma, Leader, Dharma, Reality, Pure One, Ancient One, Scholar, Victor over Passion, Deity, Lord of the Auspicious Path, Supreme, Virtuous, Light Beyond, Knower of Truth, Holy One, Cāraṇa, Cause, Siddha, Great One, Noble One, Radiant Light, Lord, Teacher, Natural Virtue, Our King, Faultless Fame, Great King of Virtues, Śaṅkara, Īśa, Self-born, Four-faced, Source of the Scriptures, Arhat, Sage of Grace, Master of Music, Eight-qualitied One, Beginningless Ancient Reality, Celestial One, and First of the Veda.

Hearing this, Kauṇti raises joined hands above her head and vows: her ears will hear no speech but the Arhat’s; her tongue utter no names but his; her eyes see no feet but his; she will fall before no other; her hands join for no other; her head bear no other feet; her mind receive no other sacred utterance. Her Jain devotion is clear. She later repeats the Cāraṇa teaching to others.

When the Brahmin of Māṅkāṭu tells them that bathing in the pools Puṇṇiya Caravaṇam, Pavakāraṇi, and Iṭṭacitti grants various benefits, Kauṇti refutes him: one may know all needed truth from the Arhat’s ethical texts without entering Puṇṇiya Caravaṇam; one need not immerse in Pavakāraṇi to know former births; and the benefits of Iṭṭacitti can be gained by righteous living and compassionate protection of living beings.

Even when entrusting Kaṇṇaki to Mātari, she uses a story connected with Jain Cāraṇas.

## A Simple Difficulty

A minor contradiction is easily resolved. Kauṇti declares that body, senses, mind, and speech belong in worship only to the Arhat, yet calls Kaṇṇaki “the splendid deity” and says she has seen no other. The Arhat is an unseen deity; Kaṇṇaki is a visibly present one. The situation also matters: Kauṇti must exalt the woman she entrusts if Mātari is to receive her wholeheartedly. After many days of travel at perhaps one *kāvatam* a day, Kauṇti knew Kaṇṇaki’s character. Moreover, Iḷaṅkō, writing after Kaṇṇaki had been enshrined, places through Kauṇti’s mouth a hint of the divinity to come.

In ordinary speech, a fervent Vaiṣṇava or Śaiva may still say of a noble person, “He is a god,” or “That mother is a goddess.” This usage too should be remembered.

Finally, when Kauṇti learned of Kōvalaṉ’s death, she fasted until she died. Some still act so. She chose it as a renunciant; people today need not imitate her.


---

# 22. Mādhavi’s Resolve

After Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki, Mādhavi holds the next place in *Cilappatikāram*. Drama gives its principal characters supporting characters, and Mādhavi is formally such a figure. Yet Kōvalaṉ appears to spend more time with her than with Kaṇṇaki. In the first part of the epic, Kaṇṇaki almost becomes secondary while Mādhavi occupies her place. If the goldsmith is Kōvalaṉ’s villain, Mādhavi is Kaṇṇaki’s antagonist; speaking frankly, one may even call her an antagonist who damaged Kōvalaṉ’s good life.

Through dance, music, and beauty she attracted him. She moved through the epic as a queen of art, performed before the king, won the *talaikkōl* distinction, and received a green-gold garland valued at 1,008 *kalañcus*.

Yet like a red lotus rising from mud, Mādhavi was born to Cittirāpati, a courtesan of a degraded profession, but chose Kōvalaṉ alone as husband to the end. After his death she refused her mother’s demand that she enter hereditary courtesanship.

Cittirāpati had placed the dance staff in her daughter’s hand at five, trained her for seven years, and made her debut before the king at twelve. Mādhavi buried her mother’s dream and laboured to establish in society the higher principle that the degrading profession should end.

With unrivalled dance, song, and bodily brilliance, she gave Kōvalaṉ quarrel and union and delighted him. But when her public performance at the Indra festival entranced everyone, resentment appeared in him like fire hidden beneath ash.

At the seashore he sang *vari* songs playfully, suggesting that he desired another woman. Mādhavi answered in the same playful mode, suggesting desire for another man. Kōvalaṉ mistook play for fact, calculated crudely that “a courtesan is only a courtesan,” and abandoned her.

That very day she sent a letter through Vayantamālai asking him to return. He refused even to receive it and closed the Mādhavi chapter as though it could never reopen. That night on the moonlit terrace she suffered unspeakably. The yāḻ and the music she attempted seemed to oppose her and became confused.

She sent another letter through the Brahmin Kōcikaṉ, asking him to find Kōvalaṉ on the forest road and bring him back. Kōvalaṉ had become fruit beyond reach.

Mādhavi had lived like a respectable wife with unfailing love for him. She later made their uncarved-sculpture daughter Maṇimēkalai renounce while still a tender fruit, and renounced herself, giving dignity to genuine asceticism.

## Mādhavi’s Defects

Mādhavi did not accept Kōvalaṉ because she was overcome by his beauty or virtue. She obtained him through a transaction. The green-gem garland worth 1,008 *kalañcus* was publicly offered in the market with the condition that its purchaser might attain her. The poor, innocent, lust-driven Kōvalaṉ paid the price, bought the garland, and only then reached Mādhavi.

He entered her house still carrying the purchased garland and is said to have remained there. This cannot mean that he never again visited the family business or the house where Kaṇṇaki lived. He must have returned repeatedly for wealth, ignored the household’s advice, and carried more property back. When he finally returned empty-handed, Kaṇṇaki apparently assumed that poverty had driven him home and said, “There are anklets; take them.”

This implies that she had already handed over and lost one ornament after another. Kōvalaṉ sees her faded body and laments:

> Seeing your withered body and its suffering,
> I—sporting with the deceitful woman
> whose nature joined falsehood—
> lost the mountain of vast wealth supplied by my lineage;
> poverty now gives me shame.  
> *(68–71)*

If such wealth was lost, the garland bought for 1,008 *kalañcus* was surely lost with it.

Could all the wealth have disappeared through charity alone? It was more likely consumed by their ostentatious life. At Maṇimēkalai’s naming ceremony, Mādhavi and Kōvalaṉ are said to have poured a rain of red gold in gifts. Whose household gold did Mādhavi rain down? Some argue that the singular “deceitful woman” in Kōvalaṉ’s confession does not refer to Mādhavi. Whom else could it mean?

Could Mādhavi have been unaware that Kōvalaṉ’s abandonment of Kaṇṇaki was wrong and caused pain to his wife, parents, and others? If she truly believed he should return to his wife, would she have sent Vayantamālai after he left, said, “Though he has not come this evening, we shall see him in the morning,” and later sent Kōcikaṉ?

Thus she bears some responsibility for Kōvalaṉ’s neglect of trade, separation from Kaṇṇaki, and extravagant life.

Yet impartiality requires another angle. If a man destroys himself through drink, is it the liquor’s fault? If a woman eats poison and dies, is it the poison’s fault? The drinker and poison-eater are responsible. Likewise, if Kōvalaṉ was ruined through Mādhavi, the primary fault is Kōvalaṉ’s. Still, as liquor and poison are harmful, Mādhavi may be called unsuitable—from the standpoint of Kaṇṇaki, the family, and even Kōvalaṉ himself.

## Mādhavi’s Merits

We cannot leave her condemned without recognizing her good qualities. The fullness she later attained concealed the deficiency of her earlier life.

Paṭṭiṉattār sings of a wife who lifts her sleeping husband’s hand aside, leaves the house for another man, returns before dawn, and resumes sleep; women born in respectable families may thus pass from hand to hand, though such cases are rare. Conversely, some women born in good families draw a man from his wife yet live with him alone as a faithful spouse.

Mādhavi’s position was different. She was the daughter of Cittirāpati’s courtesan line, whose women did not “sell” the body permanently but rented it for a time. Nevertheless, once Kōvalaṉ came to her, she lived with him as a faithful woman and bore his child, even though she did not send him back to his wife. When a playful song turned serious and caused his departure, she did not follow hereditary custom and seek another man. She repeatedly tried to restore their household. She grieved continuously.
