On hearing of his death, she became a severe renunciant and made her young daughter do the same. This is her great distinction.

## Was Mādhavi Born in the Courtesan Community?

Some deny it; others affirm it.

Those who deny it cite the line:

> The broad-shouldered maiden, undiminished in birth,
> joined to conduct not inferior to the excellence
> of the celestial women of unsurpassed greatness.  
> *(3:4–6)*

They argue that “undiminished in birth” cannot describe a woman of a degraded courtesan lineage. They add that she did not explicitly demand money from Kōvalaṉ; she remained with him; bore his child and lived as a family woman; received royal honour; twice sent letters after his departure; and shaved her head and renounced after his death. They distinguish the hereditary court-artists who danced at royal festivals from ordinary prostitutes.

Those who affirm her courtesan birth reply that “undiminished in birth” belongs to the myth of Urvaśī. While Urvaśī danced before Agastya in Indra’s court, she and Indra’s son Jayanta looked at each other with desire, lost rhythm, and were cursed to be born on earth. Mādhavi is linked to that celestial lineage; the praise concerns the mythic origin of the artistic clan, not social respectability.

The public sale of the garland is strong evidence. The king fixes a daily price of 1,008 *kalañcus*. A companion stands in the market announcing that the purchaser of the green garland may obtain the creeper-like woman. Such procedure belongs to the courtesan institution, however refined the artistic status.

Ancient Tamil usage calls public women “flower-price women,” those who receive payment daily for union. *Cilappatikāram* itself uses the expression (5:51).

Māṭalaṉ later tells Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ that Mādhavi said to Cittirāpati:

> Hearing it, the maiden Mādhavi told her good mother,
> “I shall follow the good path.
> Let Maṇimēkalai never assume the courtesan’s form
> that inflicts vast suffering.”  
> *(27:103–106)*

“I shall follow the good path” means that she will not follow the hereditary profession, and she forbids it for Maṇimēkalai.

A parallel in *Maṇimēkalai* concerns the merchant Cātuvaṉ, a second version of Kōvalaṉ. He abandons his wife Ātirai, joins a courtesan, eats her food, gives away vast wealth, and loses everything through dice and gambling. When he becomes poor, the courtesan drives him away and displays another paying man:

> Cātuvaṉ, husband of Ātirai, became unworthy,
> left his ornamented wife, and went away.
> A courtesan fed him from her hand;
> through dice and gambling he gave away great wealth.
> When the wealth was ruined and his kin suffered,
> the courtesan who had cherished him displayed another’s favour
> and shook him from her hand as a man without money.  
> *(16:3–10)*

The nature of the institution is clear.

Several *Cilappatikāram* passages should be recalled: Kōvalaṉ says he lost his family’s mountain of wealth “sporting with the deceitful woman”; he wandered “with loud-voiced minstrels and city courtesans”; he calls himself one who departed from the path and brought a flower-bodied woman to suffering; and he laments his life among empty talkers and fresh courtesans.

In a respectable household, a husband may jokingly say, “I saw a beautiful woman and shall love her,” but the wife will rarely answer, even in play, “I saw a handsome man and shall love him.” This one-sided morality still exists. Yet in *Kāṉal Vari*, Mādhavi answers Kōvalaṉ’s suggestion of another woman with a suggestion of another man. The author argues that a conventional family woman would not sing so; a woman raised in courtesan culture possessed a different freedom.

When Kōcikaṉ delivers Mādhavi’s letter, Kōvalaṉ reads it and thinks, “She is without evil” (13:94). This cannot mean she is physically well, for she sends the letter in distress. Nor can it simply mean she has never committed any fault, since he believed her extravagance and supposed love for another had caused his departure. The likely meaning is: though born into a community where women pass from hand to hand, she did not choose another after he left; she remained faithful and sent for him. In that sense she was “without evil.” He then says, “The fault is mine” (13:95), admitting that he wrongly suspected her. His readiness to absolve the woman who helped bury his marriage also reveals the old limitation of his woman-seeking mind.

Further evidence appears in *Maṇimēkalai*. When Cittirāpati asks Queen Irācamātēvi to release Maṇimēkalai, the queen says:

> “Drink, falsehood, lust, killing,
> and theft of the heart—these the wise renounce.
> Fearing as pollution your leaderless life
> that takes these as its chief principles,
> the flower-creeper came here;
> she will not return with you to your house.”  
> *(24:77–81)*

U. Vē. Cāminātaiyar glosses “leaderless life” as the lowest profession of prostitution.

A forest deity disguised as Vayantamālai also tells Kōvalaṉ that Mādhavi lamented the courtesan’s life:

> Whether exalted, learned in texts,
> or capable of discerning every category,
> people reject it as a disease and cast it away:
> the courtesan’s life has reached the lowest state.  
> *(11:180–183)*

Though spoken by a deceptive deity, the statement is placed in the epic by Iḷaṅkō and identifies the social class.

The word *tēvaṭiyār* originally meant a servant of the deity, one involved in temple worship. Over time it came to denote women engaged in courtesanship. In a temple known to me, such a woman once sang daily and danced with an instrument before the decorated deity during festivals. The street where they lived was called Tēvaṭiyār Street. The practice has now ended. Most families formerly connected with the profession have reformed into respectable, chaste households. Mādhavi’s transformation was similar.

I once wrote a story, *A Treasure Hard to Find*. A courtesan named Cittirātēvi forces her daughter Kōtai toward the profession. Kōtai resolves to accept the first man as husband; the wealthy Cētunātaṉ deceives her after she becomes pregnant. Their child Kalā is born. When the mother again presses Kōtai into the profession, Kōtai commits suicide. The grandmother raises Kalā and tries to initiate her, but Kalā refuses, marries, and lives chastely. Mādhavi embodies both Kōtai’s and Kalā’s reform. Her personal conduct may be blameless in the end, but the maternal lineage is nevertheless a courtesan lineage.

Her severe renunciation probably arose from guilt as well as grief. She may have reflected: because of me their household broke; they crossed a difficult road to Madurai; Kōvalaṉ, the Pāṇḍya king and queen, Kaṇṇaki, many goldsmiths, Kōvalaṉ’s mother, and Kaṇṇaki’s mother died; Madurai burned. Feeling herself a cause, she renounced and prevented her daughter from entering the hereditary occupation.

Renunciation was also a weapon protecting a widowed woman of courtesan birth from the vulture-eyes of low men. Her firmness deserves admiration.

## Mādhavi’s Religion

Even among relatives, one family may be Śaiva and another Vaiṣṇava. Nammāḻvār, a Vēḷāḷa, was Vaiṣṇava; Appar, also a Vēḷāḷa, was Śaiva. Religious difference across communities is therefore unsurprising. In the age when Jainism and Buddhism flourished, relatives and members of the same or different social groups followed either faith.

In *Cilappatikāram*, Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki travel with the Jain nun Kauṇti and worship Jain Cāraṇas. In *Maṇimēkalai*, Mādhavi and Maṇimēkalai explicitly become Buddhist renunciants and receive moral teaching from the Buddhist master Aṟavaṇa Aṭikaḷ. Mādhavi’s chosen religion was therefore Buddhism.


---

# 23. The Role of the Brahmin Characters

## Brahmins

Four Brahmin characters appear in *Cilappatikāram*: one woman, Tēvanti, and three men—the Brahmin of Māṅkāṭu, Kōcikaṉ, and Māṭalaṉ.

A speaker who noticed this arrangement once criticised Iḷaṅkō for giving a great deal of room in his work to Aryanism—that is, to Brahmins. This criticism calls for an answer.

Members of the Brahmin community become hurt and angry when anyone calls them *pārppāṉ* or *pārppanar*. Those opposed to Brahminism say *pārppāṉ*; servants of Aryanism say “Brahmin”; self-styled moderates alternate between the two according to convenience. These people, caught between both positions, are pragmatists who know how to survive.

In the classical corpus, including the *Tolkāppiyam*, the words *pārppanar*, *pārppār*, *pārppāṉ*, and *pārppani* occur, scattered across more than seventy-five places. Not all the people so designated were Aryan Brahmins. Tamil Brahmins—Tamil *antaṇar*—existed in Tamilakam from ancient times. Shall we examine firm evidence for this?

In the chapter on the combination of the shortened *u* in the *Eḻuttatikāram* of the *Tolkāppiyam*, the following occurs within sūtra 77, beginning “uyirum puḷḷiyum”:

> “In the two domains of the higher and the non-rational classes,
> compounds of quality reveal all five genders.” (77:4–5)

The Brahmin commentator Nacciṉārkkiṉiyar explains it thus:

> “Consider such forms as *karum-pārppāṉ*, *karum-pārppani*, *karum-pārppār*, *karuṅ-kutirai*, and *karuṅ-kutiraikaḷ*. They respectively mean: a Brahmin man who is dark, a Brahmin woman who is dark, Brahmin people who are dark, a horse that is dark, and horses that are dark. Thus a compounded qualitative noun conveys all five genders.”

Those qualified here by the word “dark” are the *antaṇar* of Tamil Nadu. Those who conduct worship in Śaiva temples under the title *kurukkaḷ* are called Ādi-Śaiva Brahmins.

There is internal evidence within *Cilappatikāram* itself, in the “Canto of the Exposition,” that Tamil Brahmins existed:

> “Behold the firm and mighty Cēra with the long spear,
> who granted a heavenly dwelling to the Tamil Vedic Brahmin.” (23:63–64)

The expression “Tamil Vedic Brahmin” deserves notice here.

Among the four Śaiva preceptors, it is generally accepted that Cuntarar belonged to the Ādi-Śaiva Brahmins who performed temple worship. Nāvukkaracar was a Vēḷāḷa. The other two, Campantar and Māṇikkavācakar, were Tamil *antaṇar*. However many Vedic rites may have been attached to Campantar in the *Periyapurāṇam*, he was a Tamil *antaṇar*; Māṇikkavācakar was likewise. Do Brahmins descended from the Aryan line bear names such as Campantar, Ñāṉacampantar, Māṇikkavācakar, or Tiruvātavūrar? To my knowledge, they do not, though perhaps a very few may be found somewhere. Members of the Ādi-Śaiva Tamil *antaṇar* community called *kurukkaḷ* do sometimes bear the name Campantaṉ. Brahmins will not even turn toward the name Tirunāvukkaracar. Appūti Aṭikaḷ, who belonged to the *kurukkaḷ* lineage, named everything in his household—his possessions, his children, his water-shed, and all other rational and non-rational things—Tirunāvukkaracar.

It also appears that in ancient times Brahmins acted as companions and helpers to Tamils.

In the *Kaṟpiyal* section of the *Poruḷatikāram* of the *Tolkāppiyam*, the forms of speech proper to a Brahmin companion are given:

> “To speak of the state of love and of the state of the chariot;
> to reveal the hero’s unspoken intention;
> to interpret omens connected with cattle;
> to urge departure and to restrain departure—
> these and similar acts belong to the Brahmin.” (36)

The same section lists the intermediaries who may go from the hero to end the heroine’s estrangement:

> “The companion, the mother, the Brahmin, the confidant,
> the bard, the woman singer, the younger men, the guests,
> the actors, the *viṟali* dancers, the wise, and witnesses—
> these are said to be the properly constituted mediators.” (52)

One may recall here the traditional account in the *Periyapurāṇam* that an *antaṇar*—said to have been Śiva himself—went twice as messenger from Cuntarar to end Paravai Nācciyār’s estrangement.

A sūtra in the *Akattiṇaiyiyal* section of the *Nampi Akapporuḷ*, together with its old commentary, may also be compared:

> “To set forth the impermanence of youth, body, and wealth;
> to restrain departure and to consent to departure;
> all these and similar acts belong to the Vedic men.” (100)

The commentary says that the sūtra gathers together all the functions of the Brahmin companion among the two kinds of confidants. Everything beginning with youth belongs to him; “and similar acts” includes seeking access and the like.

In *Cilappatikāram*, the Brahmin woman Tēvanti was Kaṇṇaki’s friend. The *antaṇar* Kōcikaṉ knew Mādhavi’s household and therefore went to see her when she grieved after separation from Kōvalaṉ. Through him Mādhavi sent a letter to Kōvalaṉ. One must therefore not condemn all the Brahmins appearing in the epic as Aryan Brahmins.

Some practices followed by Aryan Brahmins and Tamil Brahmins may have resembled one another. One group may have adopted certain practices from the other. Both groups are entitled to the distinguished name *maṟaiyavar*, “people of sacred learning.” Let us now consider, one by one, those who occur in the epic.

## 1. Tēvanti

Although brief references to Tēvanti occur under other headings, we shall examine her somewhat more fully here. Tēvanti was Kaṇṇaki’s Brahmin friend.

A Brahmin woman named Mālati was nursing her co-wife’s child when the milk caught in its throat and the child died. Mālati lay in supplication at the temple of Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ, begging that the child be restored to life. At that moment a demoness called Iṭākiṉi swallowed the corpse. To remove Mālati’s grief, Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ appeared in the form of the child. Mālati returned the child to the co-wife. He grew into a young man, married Tēvanti, lived with her for eight years, and then vanished. The widowed Tēvanti thereafter remained attached to the temple of Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ. This is her story.

Some researchers astrologically divine that Tēvanti and her husband, Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ, had no bodily union. Only if one can believe that a deity came in human form and married Tēvanti can one also believe that the two had no bodily relation. This is simply a form of epic imagination.

One evening Tēvanti came and blessed Kaṇṇaki, saying, “May you regain your husband.” As though foretelling what was to happen later, Kaṇṇaki told Tēvanti of the evil dream she had seen. Tēvanti replied that it was the fruit of an old deed and advised her to bathe in the two pools called Sōmakuṇṭam and Sūriyakuṇṭam and worship at the temple of the god of love, by which she might regain her husband. Kaṇṇaki rejected the advice, saying that immersion in sacred pools and worship of that deity were not fitting for her.

Later, on learning that Kōvalaṉ had been killed, Tēvanti went to Madurai. From there she travelled with Aiyai to Kaṇṇaki’s temple in the Cēra country and lamented. Possessed by a deity, she uttered many revelations. She told the deified Kaṇṇaki that Kaṇṇaki’s mother and Kōvalaṉ’s mother had died, unable to bear their children’s deaths, and that both fathers and Mādhavi had embraced renunciation. She explained Maṇimēkalai’s renunciation to the Cēra king. Before Kaṇṇaki, Tēvanti alternated songs with the nurse and the intimate maid. Once possessed, she caused water to be sprinkled upon three little girls and revealed their previous births: one had been Kaṇṇaki’s mother, another Kōvalaṉ’s mother, and the third Mātari. After death, all three had been reborn as those girls and had come there. Later Kaṇṇaki possessed Tēvanti and made her speak of Iḷaṅkō’s renunciation.

### A Woman Priest

At the end, this Tēvanti was appointed priestess of Kaṇṇaki’s temple:

> “He ordered that the precincts of the Pattini shrine be duly arranged,
> and that the adornment of the daily festival be performed;
> he commanded that flowers, incense, and fragrant offerings
> be conducted by Tēvanti.” (30:151–154)

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ endowed land for the temple’s expenses, arranged for the daily festival worship, and appointed Tēvanti priestess to perform the regular rites—anointing with fragrant paste, offering flowers, and raising sweet-smelling incense.

The appointment was an intelligent and fitting act. Kaṇṇaki’s shrine was a temple of a female deity; Tēvanti had been Kaṇṇaki’s friend; she had long been accustomed to temple life at Pācaṇṭa Cāttaṉ’s shrine; as a widow she had no household responsibility or other occupation; she was a Brahmin woman; and on several occasions she had entered states of divine possession. In a country where even temples of goddesses are served by male priests, making Tēvanti a priestess was a new and revolutionary act.

## 2. The Brahmin of Māṅkāṭu

The epic does not disclose the Māṅkāṭu Brahmin’s personal name. Surely he must have had one. Perhaps he is a Vedic man who needs no name. He came from Māṅkāṭu in the Kuṭaku hill region of the Cēra country and, like Kōvūr Kiḻār or Ālattūr Kiḻār, is identified by his village. He is also identified by community, as in a name such as “the young gold-merchant of Uṟaiyūr.” This shows that people of the time could be known by their village, by their community, or by both. He was a Vaiṣṇava *antaṇar* with a strong desire to travel and see different places.

After crossing Uṟaiyūr, Kauṇti, Kōvalaṉ, and Kaṇṇaki rested in a young grove. The Māṅkāṭu Brahmin arrived there, praising and blessing the Pāṇḍya king and his country. Kōvalaṉ asked him to state his village and identity. The Brahmin replied: “I come from Māṅkāṭu in the Kuṭaku hills. My eyes urged me to set out and travel so that they might behold Araṅkaṉ reclining in divine sleep at Śrīraṅgam and Vēṅkaṭattāṉ enthroned at Tiruvēṅkaṭam.” When Kōvalaṉ asked the road to Madurai, he continued:

“It is pitiable that you have begun this journey in the fierce heat of late summer. If you cross the forest and mountain by this road, you will see three paths branching like Śiva’s trident. If you take the right-hand road, you must cross the Pāṇḍya king’s Little Hill. The middle road is easy, but a bewildering deity dwells along it, and you must escape its delusion.

“If you take the left road, you will reach the dark grove-hill of Viṣṇu. There is a cavern there. To enter it, think upon Viṣṇu’s feet and circumambulate the hill three times. A female deity will appear and question you; if you answer, she will open the entrance. After passing through several gateways, you will reach one with double doors. Another female deity will ask questions. If you answer suitably, she will show you three pools: Puṇṇiya Caravaṇam, Pavakāraṇi, and Iṭṭacitti. By bathing in Puṇṇiya Caravaṇam one learns the fivefold grammar; by bathing in Pavakāraṇi one perceives former births; by bathing in Iṭṭacitti one attains whatever one has desired.” He went on at length in this manner.

On hearing him, the Jain-affiliated Kauṇti rejected the Vaiṣṇava claims. “None of what you describe is necessary,” she said. “The fivefold grammar of which you speak can be learned from the scripture of our Arukaṉ. Former birth may be inferred from the present one. If one lives truthfully, one may attain the desired end without bathing in Iṭṭacitti.” With this reply she dismissed him.

It seems that Iḷaṅkō used the Māṅkāṭu Brahmin to advertise Vaiṣṇavism and then used Kauṇti Aṭikaḷ to brush it aside and promote Jainism.

## 3. Kōcikaṉ

Kōcikaṉ was an *antaṇar*. Because he belonged to the Kōcika lineage, he is also called Kaucikaṉ. His role in *Cilappatikāram* is small: he functions as a postman. Carrying the letter Mādhavi had entrusted to him for Kōvalaṉ, he travelled through the forest searching for him.

One morning Kōvalaṉ had gone beside a water source to perform his bodily necessities. Wearied by travel and unaware that Kōvalaṉ was nearby, Kaucikaṉ saw a withered *mādhavi* creeper and spoke to it in pity. Hearing him pronounce the word “Mādhavi,” Kōvalaṉ approached and asked for news. Kaucikaṉ said:

“Kōvalaṉ, after you left the city, your parents suffered grief beyond words. They sent people in many directions to find you, but without result. Hearing this from Vayantamālai, Mādhavi became overwhelmed with sorrow and lay confined to her bed. When I went to see her, she wrote this letter and asked me to find you and place it in your hands.” He then gave the letter to Kōvalaṉ. That is Kaucikaṉ’s role; he appears as a helper of Mādhavi’s household.

The poetic charm of the passage lies in this: *mādhavi* is also a name for the flowering creeper called *kurukkatti*, and that floral name had been given to the woman Mādhavi. Women are named after flowers—Centāmarai, Tillai, Tuḷaci, Alli, Kumutam, Ceṅkaḻunīr or Ceṅkēṇi, Marukkoḻuntu, and the Sanskrit-derived names Ampujam, Paṅkajam, Carōjā, Vaṉajā, Kamalam, and Mallikā. Some are called Mālati, Caṇpakam, or Kāñcanā, three names associated with flowers. Others bear Puṣpā, the Sanskrit word for flower. Today some women bear the pure Tamil name Malar. On the same basis the woman Mādhavi received the other name of the *kurukkatti* creeper.

Seeing the withered creeper, Kaucikaṉ spoke in sorrow, his heart withering like that of Vaṭalūr Rāmaliṅka Vaḷḷalār whenever he saw a withered crop. On hearing the word “Mādhavi,” Kōvalaṉ made contact with him.

But did Kaucikaṉ truly address the withered *kurukkatti* by the word *Mādhavi*? The matter is doubtful. May we not say that Iḷaṅkō himself created the double meaning and played upon it to polish the epic and heighten its flavour?

## 4. Māṭalaṉ

Māṭalaṉ enters *Cilappatikāram* midway through the work—after the first book, the Pukār Kāṇḍam, within the second book, the Madurai Kāṇḍam—and continues to act until the end of the third, the Vañji Kāṇḍam.

He was foremost among learned *antaṇar*, a native of Talaicceṅkāṭu near Pukār. He had travelled south, bathed in the Kumari river, circumambulated Mount Potiyil, visited Madurai, and finally come to rest in the grove-school where Kauṇti stayed with Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki. Kōvalaṉ saluted him and asked about his journey.

There Māṭalaṉ praised Kōvalaṉ by recounting incidents that established him as “a warrior of compassion,” “a wealth that never departs,” and “a noble protector of the destitute.” The credit for making Kōvalaṉ appear as the epic’s hero belongs to Māṭalaṉ.

“Kōvalaṉ, so far as I know, you have done only good in this birth; yet your coming here with Kaṇṇaki to suffer in this fashion must be the fruit of an evil deed from an earlier birth,” he said, consoling him. Since Māṭalaṉ came from Talaicceṅkāṭu near Pukār, he knew Kōvalaṉ’s good acts well. He must also have known that Kōvalaṉ had left his parents and Kaṇṇaki, become attached to Mādhavi, and lost his wealth. But he did not wish to recall these matters and break Kōvalaṉ’s spirit. Instead, he said, “You have done good,” and offered comfort. Māṭalaṉ was a wise man who acted according to circumstance and knew how to speak. He repeatedly used the doctrine of karma to support what he said. If belief in karma serves one valuable purpose, what can it be other than this capacity to create peace by offering consolation?

Māṭalaṉ and Kauṇti advised Kōvalaṉ that it was no longer proper to remain in Madurai’s outskirts. If the couple entered the city and joined members of their merchant community, they would be warmly received as the son and daughter-in-law of Mācāttuvāṉ. Māṭalaṉ thus also served as a noble guide who pointed toward a means of survival.

He did more than console and advise. He feared blame and possessed a strong sense of responsibility. Māṭalaṉ informed the mothers of Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki that their children had died. Both women, their hearts broken, gave up their lives. Grieving that they had died only because he had brought the news, Māṭalaṉ accepted responsibility and feared the stain of blame. To cleanse it, he travelled north, bathed in the Gaṅgā, and found peace.

After bathing in the Gaṅgā he met Cēraṉ Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, who had defeated the northern kings and was encamped on the river’s southern bank. Having saluted him, Māṭalaṉ narrated the events in Madurai and other news:

“Mādhavi’s song in the *Kāṉal Vari* crushed the crowned heads of Kanaka and Vijaya. Kōvalaṉ separated from Mādhavi, travelled to Madurai with Kaṇṇaki, was falsely accused of theft, and died. Kaṇṇaki came to the border of your Cēra country and gave up her life. On learning this, Mātari, who had given her refuge, entered fire and died. I went to Pukār and reported these events. Hearing them, Mācāttuvāṉ became a Buddhist renunciant; Mānāykaṉ entered an Ājīvaka establishment and took up renunciation; the mothers of Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki died of broken hearts; Mādhavi and Maṇimēkalai renounced the world. In the Pāṇḍya country Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ and Kōpperuntēvi died. Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ’s younger brother Veṟṟivēl Ceḻiyaṉ sacrificed a thousand goldsmiths and now rules Madurai. The Cōḻa king in Pukār remains well.”

After hearing him, Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ presented Māṭalaṉ with fifty *tulām* of gold, equal to the king’s own bodily weight.

When Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ became enraged on hearing that the Pāṇḍya and Cōḻa kings had belittled his northern victory, Māṭalaṉ calmed him. He taught the impermanence of wealth, youth, and the body, and urged him to cease making war and instead perform a righteous sacrifice.

Māṭalaṉ sprinkled upon three little girls the water given by the divinely possessed Tēvanti. Their former births were revealed: two had been Kōvalaṉ’s mother and Kaṇṇaki’s mother; the third had been Mātari. Māṭalaṉ explained their identities to the Cēra king, after which the king entered the sacrificial hall with him.

When we consider Māṭalaṉ’s many functions, we are reminded of a passage from Shakespeare’s *As You Like It*:

> “All the world’s a stage,
> And all the men and women merely players;
> They have their exits and their entrances;
> And one man in his time plays many parts.”

The whole world is a theatre. Men and women are merely actors. They leave the stage in death and enter it in birth. One person plays many roles during a lifetime.

In keeping with this idea, Māṭalaṉ performs many parts in *Cilappatikāram*. Iḷaṅkō needed such a figure to connect the narrative’s actions and hold its structure together. He created and employed Māṭalaṉ appropriately for that purpose.

At the beginning of this chapter I noted the criticism that Iḷaṅkō had given excessive room in his work to Aryanism and Brahmins. Whether these Brahmin characters were historical participants in Kaṇṇaki’s story or were shaped by Iḷaṅkō in this form, everything becomes intelligible once we remember that Brahmins were suitable for the kinds of narrative functions assigned to them in the epic.

There is also a Brahmin-related story woven into the work that is not directly connected to its central plot—the story of Parācaraṉ, Vārttikaṉ, Takkiṇāmūrtti, and Kārttikai. Parācaraṉ, a Brahmin from the Cōḻa country, approached the Cēra king and received a great quantity of wealth as a gift. He passed this wealth to a little boy named Takkiṇāmūrtti, son of the Brahmin Vārttikaṉ in the Pāṇḍya country. Seeing the child’s exuberant display, people falsely accused his father of having stolen the riches and given them to his son. Vārttikaṉ was imprisoned.

His wife Kārttikai lamented and pleaded. When the Pāṇḍya king later discovered the truth, he not only released Vārttikaṉ but freed the other prisoners as well. This Brahmin story appears in the “Canto of the Exposition.”

It is narrated to Kaṇṇaki by the city-goddess Madurāpati in order to illustrate the greatness of the Pāṇḍya rulers.


---

# 24. Mātari’s Excellence

Iḷaṅkō introduces Mātari midway through the story, in the “Canto of Refuge.” She was a woman of the cowherd community, a devotee of Tirumāl—Kṛṣṇa—and also a worshipper of a minor deity. She possessed the privilege of supplying dairy produce to the royal palace. Her daughter was named Aiyai.

When Kauṇti, Kōvalaṉ, and Kaṇṇaki were staying on the outskirts of Madurai, Mātari passed that way after offering milk to the minor deity Iyakki. On seeing her, Kauṇti reasoned: “She appears to belong to the cowherd community. There is no cruel or deceitful occupation in the life of cowherds, who protect cattle and give their produce to others. She therefore will do no harm. She is an elderly woman of experience and seems upright and compassionate. It will be safe to entrust Kaṇṇaki to her.” The poem says:

> “After offering milk to fair-eyed Iyakki
> in the ancient city’s outer quarter,
> the virtuous woman returned—
> Mātari, the senior woman of the cowherds.
> Seeing Kauṇti Aṭikaḷ, she bowed at her feet.
> ‘The life of cowherds, who guard the cows
> and distribute their yield, holds no cruelty.
> This aged woman is without evil,
> upright and compassionate.
> There is no fault in lodging the maiden
> with Mātari,’ Kauṇti thought.” (116–124)

People offer to a deity whatever blessing or wealth is most abundant in their own lives. A cowherd woman lacks no milk. Since Mātari possessed the good fortune of milk in abundance, she offered milk. Being cultured, she touched Kauṇti’s feet and worshipped her.

Iḷaṅkō calls her *mutumakaḷ*, “senior woman,” in two places: “the senior woman of the cowherds” and “the faultless senior woman.” In the first, the word means a woman of consequence—an important person—in the cowherd community. In the second, it means an elderly woman innocent of wrongdoing, to whom a person may safely be entrusted.

The moment Mātari bowed at her feet, Kauṇti concluded that she was upright and compassionate. Could Kaṇṇaki be placed in her house? Cowherds know no treachery: their work is not deceitful; they protect their cattle from sickness and distress and sustain them with good fodder. There could therefore be no error in entrusting Kaṇṇaki to them. One should not count against them the fact that some milk-sellers mix water into milk. Not everyone does it. Mixing another substance might cause illness; water causes none. However much water is added, milk remains white—such is milk’s great blessing! Through Kauṇti’s reflections, Iḷaṅkō thus introduces Mātari’s excellence.

## Mātari’s Wealth

The phrase “senior woman of the cowherds” itself suggests that Mātari possessed considerable wealth and influence. When Kōvalaṉ later left her house for Madurai’s market street, the “Canto of the Place of Killing” describes him as “leaving the house of the cowherds of many cattle” (*pallāṉ kōvalar illam nīṅki*, line 98). *Pal āṉ* means “many cows”: Mātari’s household owned numerous cattle.

In ancient times the person who owned many cattle was regarded as wealthy. Indeed, the Tamil word *māṭu*, “cattle,” also means wealth. Many literary examples could be given, but one *Tirukkuṟaḷ* is sufficient:

> “Learning is the imperishable and distinguished wealth of a person;
> all other possessions are not wealth.” (400)

Here *māṭu* means wealth. In Kannada, too, *dhana* may mean cattle and wealth. The Latin word *pecunia* likewise has an etymological connection with livestock and property. The abundance of cattle was therefore once the measure of prosperity. The expression “cowherds of many cattle” reveals the wealth of Mātari’s household. Mātari also led Kaṇṇaki to her house surrounded by cowherd men and women; this too indicates her standing.

Kauṇti entrusted Kaṇṇaki, not Kōvalaṉ, to Mātari. There is no need to speak separately of granting refuge to a man: Kōvalaṉ was included in the refuge extended to Kaṇṇaki.

## Mātari’s Hospitality

Having accepted Kaṇṇaki as one under her protection, Mātari did not lodge her in the main household among her own people. She placed the couple in a small, well-guarded house roofed with timber, freshly renewed with red-earth plaster. She had Kaṇṇaki bathed in fresh water and told her: “My daughter Aiyai will remain as your companion and help you. Since Kauṇti has brought you to a good place, your husband too need have no anxiety.”

Because Kōvalaṉ observed the vows of a Jain layman, Mātari instructed the women, including “the young woman related through the husband,” to provide without delay good vessels in which the meal could be prepared during daylight:

> “Since the lord observes the vows of a Jain householder,
> with the young woman related through the husband,
> quickly provide good vessels suited
> for cooking the meal that is prepared by day.” (18–21)

The phrase *nāttūṇ naṅkai* means the husband’s sister—here Aiyai. It is a worldly custom to address a newly arrived man as an elder brother to a woman of the household. Kōvalaṉ therefore becomes Aiyai’s brother; Kaṇṇaki becomes her sister-in-law; and to Kaṇṇaki, Aiyai becomes the husband’s sister. The creation of such relationships is a civilised practice.

“Aṭikaḷ,” “the lord,” refers to Kōvalaṉ. *Cāvaka nōṉpikaḷ* are laypeople who observe religious vows while living in the household. Kōvalaṉ is referred to in the respectful plural, just as one might say “Kōvalaṉ, they.” Kaṇṇaki is not included in this phrase because she herself is the person about to cook.

The “food prepared by day” is literally the food made in daylight. What connection exists between a Jain lay-observer and cooking during the day? Kōvalaṉ was a Jain. Jains do not light lamps at night, lest moths fall into the flame and die; to maintain the vow against killing, they eat before darkness descends.

There is a village called Nallāṟṟūr about ten miles west of Puducherry. People in that village once ate before lamps were lit, while there was still daylight. People of Cuddalore used to tease that a guest who reached Nallāṟṟūr after dark would find it difficult to obtain a meal. This is not a present custom; it must belong to an earlier period. Was it not in the Cuddalore region that Nāvukkaracar once joined Jainism? Nallāṟṟūr falls within the Cuddalore electoral area. This practice may therefore have existed there when Jainism flourished. The village is also distinguished by the tomb of Sivappirakāca Aṭikaḷ, the “treasury of imagination” who composed many works.

My suggestion that the meal was cooked by day because Kōvalaṉ was a Jain is only an inference. Those who do not accept it may leave it aside. The distinction between *nāḷ aṅkāṭi*, “day bazaar,” and *allaṅkāṭi*, “night bazaar,” confirms that *nāḷ* may signify daytime.

The command to provide “good vessels” is also noteworthy. However many utensils women possess, they are rarely satisfied. Though their houses may contain aluminium, brass, copper, stainless steel, and silver vessels of many kinds, the sight of earthen pots and jars arranged by a potter may make them exclaim, “Ah! What beautiful things!” Mātari’s house may likewise have contained many vessels. “Good vessels” means new, superior utensils not previously used by the household.

“Give them without delay” shows, on one hand, that the guests had arrived in great hunger and, on the other, that the food had to be cooked and eaten before nightfall. Rice, vegetables, milk, ghee, curd, and other ingredients were then supplied:

> “Together with the cooking vessels they gave her:
> the clustered, mature jackfruit of the noble tree
> that bears without visible blossom;
> striped cucumber, green pomegranate,
> mango fruit and sweet plantain;
> fine *cāli* rice and the products of their milk—
> saying, ‘Bangle-armed lady, receive these.’” (23–28)

Thus they gave cooking vessels, superior *cāli* rice, milk, and milk products such as ghee and curd, together with a mature jackfruit, cucumber, green pomegranate, mangoes, and plantains. A meal containing jackfruit, plantain, mango, and rice is called “the three fruits and rice.” All three fruits were provided to Kaṇṇaki.

*Kōḷip pākaḷ* denotes jackfruit. The “fruit” in “clustered mature fruit” refers to the stage at which the jackfruit has matured but not fully ripened. Before its bulbs become sweet, the fruit is chopped repeatedly with a heavy knife and cooked as a vegetable; in this stage it is called *kottukkāy*.

A fine thought lies hidden in the expression “the jackfruit of the noble tree that bears without flowering.” N. Mu. Vēṅkaṭacāmi Nāṭṭār explains:

> “The clustered and mature fruit of the jack tree, which bears without flowering, is said to possess a noble nature because, like noble people who give without being asked, it fruits without first displaying flowers. *Kōḷi* is a tree that fruits without flowering; *pākaḷ* is the jack tree.”

A verse in the *Ciṟupañcamūlam* expresses a related idea:

> “There are trees that fruit without flowering;
> those who know goodness and master books
> are old in wisdom without ageing.” (22)

Just as a tree appears to fruit without flowering, those who understand goodness and are learned in books are respected as elders even when young in age. The *Tivākara Nikaṇṭu* also defines the term:

> “*Kōḷi* is a cool tree that fruits without flowering.” (4:80)

Fig, sacred fig, banyan, pineapple, and jack are commonly said to fruit without flowers. What does this mean? When a person who rarely visits finally appears, people say, “Your coming is like the flowering of the fig.” They assume that fig and similar trees bear fruit without blossom. In truth, fig, sacred fig, pineapple, banyan, and jack all have flowers. Many blossoms gather together, are enclosed by outer tissue, and take a rounded form; pollination occurs within, and the cluster develops into a fruit. Each apparent fruit must therefore be understood as an aggregation of many flowers. Among such *kōḷi* forms, jackfruit is foremost in both size and taste. The *Perumpāṇāṟṟuppaṭai* says:

> “Among the *kōḷi* trees with rich and tender branches,
> the jackfruit is proclaimed supreme in fruit.” (407–408)

This is also known through ordinary experience. Hence Iḷaṅkō’s phrase, “the jackfruit of the noble tree that bears without flowering.”

## A Sight Too Full for the Eyes

As Kaṇṇaki served the meal and Kōvalaṉ ate, the intimacy between them became a sight too wonderful for Mātari and her daughter Aiyai to contain with their eyes. They spoke joyfully to one another: “Could this Kōvalaṉ be Kṛṣṇa, flower-dark in colour, whom Yaśodā bore in the northern cowherd settlement? Could this Kaṇṇaki be Nappiṉṉai, the radiant woman of our own community who, beside the river Yamunā, removed Kṛṣṇa’s distress and married him? How can we adequately wonder at and delight in this sight?”

> “Is this noble man eating the good nectar
> the fresh-flower-coloured one whom Yaśodā bore
> in the cowherd settlement?
> And is this many-bangled woman the lamp of our ancient clan,
> who beside the Yamunā relieved the sorrow
> of the pure jewel-coloured one?
> Aiyai and her mother, filled with wonder, said:
> ‘Their sight here is too much for our eyes to hold.’” (46–53)

At certain moments, people seem to interpret circumstances through the forms of their own community and the deity they worship. In the *Vēṭṭuva Vari*, Kaṇṇaki is metaphorically praised in forms suited to hunters. Here the cowherd women Mātari and Aiyai see Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki in the forms of Kṛṣṇa and Nappiṉṉai, figures belonging to their own community.

A comparable scene occurs in Cēkkiḻār’s *Periyapurāṇam*. When Paravai Nācciyār first sees Cuntarar in Tiruvārūr, she wonders who he can be:

> “Is he Murukaṉ appearing before me?
> Or Love himself in surpassing radiance?
> Or a celestial warrior with garlanded chest?
> Or one who has received the true grace
> of the Lord with lightning-red matted hair?
> Who is this man who has transformed my mind?” (290)

Paravai wonders whether he is Murukaṉ, the god of love, a celestial, or a devotee of Śiva. That verse reflects a Śaiva religious setting. In the same way, Mātari and Aiyai imagine according to their community and faith. “The noble man” is Kōvalaṉ; the “many-bangled woman” is Kaṇṇaki; the “fresh-flower-coloured” and “pure jewel-coloured” one is Kṛṣṇa; the “lamp” is Nappiṉṉai. Perhaps women naturally enjoy watching such scenes!

## The Story of Nappiṉṉai

A cowherd king named Kumpakaṉ lived near Mithilā. His daughter was Nappiṉṉai. Seven demons came as wild bulls and caused trouble. Kumpakaṉ announced that he would give his daughter in marriage to whoever subdued and killed the bulls. Kṛṣṇa did so and married Nappiṉṉai. This occurred on the bank of the Yamunā. Mātari and Aiyai were overwhelmed with delight because Kaṇṇaki seemed to them like this Nappiṉṉai.

## Difference of Community

Kaṇṇaki must have been utterly exhausted when Mātari brought her home under protection. In such a condition, should the work of cooking have been left to her? Should not Mātari and Aiyai have prepared and served the meal? Why was Kaṇṇaki herself made to cook?

What explanation can be offered except that the cowherd community was regarded as low and the merchant community as high, and that members of a high community would not eat food prepared by those deemed lower? Even when cowherds achieved rare and great things, people of the time refused to attribute the achievement to them and instead claimed that someone else had done it. Those called high-caste possessed the arrogance of asking, “Are we to accept that a cowherd did this?” Consider several examples.

Tirumūlar, who belonged to the cowherd community, composed the *Tirumantiram* of three thousand remarkable verses. But the truth was obscured. The story says that a cowherd named Mūlaṉ fell dead while tending cattle; the cows cried. A yogi travelling from Kailāsa took pity, projected his life into Mūlaṉ’s body, hid his own body, and led the cattle home. When he returned, Śiva had destroyed the yogi’s original body because he was destined to remain there and serve. Living thereafter in the cowherd Mūlaṉ’s body, the yogi composed the three thousand verses. Is this true? No; it is imagination. The history was changed so that the greatness of a cowherd’s intellectual power would not be acknowledged.

Another cowherd boy who tended cattle defeated Avvaiyār in wit by asking whether she wanted “roasted fruit” or “unroasted fruit.” Unwilling to assign this distinction to the cowherd community, high-caste tellers transformed the story and claimed that Murukaṉ had appeared in the form of the boy.

Kālidāsa, the Sanskrit poet, is one of the world’s great poets. He belonged to a pastoral community and had tended goats and cattle. Yet a story was constructed that Kālī wrote something upon his tongue and thereby made him a great poet. Others produced the Himalayan-sized lie that Kālidāsa was a Brahmin who happened to herd animals. Brahmins do not tend goats. Perhaps those high-caste storytellers babbled in memory of the historical claim that Aryans entered India through the Khyber Pass driving sheep and cattle!

Now an atomic bomb is about to fall here. Kṛṣṇa himself, said to have grown up in the northern cowherd settlement, belonged to the cowherd community. The story says that the child born to Vasudēva and Dēvakī was carried to the house of Nandagōpa and Yaśodā in the cowherd settlement. Vasudēva left the male child Kṛṣṇa there and carried away the female child lying beside Yaśodā. Would Yaśodā or the women who attended the birth not search for the missing girl? Would they not wonder how a male child had appeared when a girl had been born? To “adjust” the inconsistency, the story says that the goddess Māyā had come as the girl. This must therefore be imagination.

Kṛṣṇa lived wholly within a pastoral setting and married Nappiṉṉai of the cowherd community. Historians describe Kṛṣṇa and Balarāma as kings of the Yādava—that is, pastoral—community. A direct piece of evidence is Iḷaṅkō’s phrase in *Cilappatikāram*, “whom Yaśodā bore in the cowherd settlement.” Those who cannot accept this may reject it. The thought is offered for scholarly examination, not as a final and absolute conclusion.

## The Cowherd Women’s Kuravai

After Kōvalaṉ left for the market street, many evil omens appeared in the cowherd settlement. Mātari described them to Aiyai and then arranged for the cowherd women to perform a *kuravai* dance. Tirumāl is praised throughout this canto.

## Mātari’s Vaiṣṇavism

Mātari followed Vaiṣṇava belief. After the *kuravai*, she took flowers, fragrant incense, sandal paste, and a garland and went to worship Tirumāl in his temple on the bank of the Vaigai:

> “The senior cowherd woman, graceful from the dance,
> took flowers, incense, prepared sandal paste, and garlands;
> she went down to the bathing-place
> to worship the feet of great Tirumāl
> beside the long-flowing Vaigai.” (*Tuṉpa Mālai*, 1–5)

Although Mātari worshipped the minor deity Iyakki, she clearly held Vaiṣṇava convictions on a larger scale. Is not Tirumāl the god of the pastoral *mullai* landscape? Does not the *Akattiṇaiyiyal* of the *Tolkāppiyam* say, “the forest world inhabited by Māyōṉ” (sūtra 5)?

Iḷaṅkō’s statement that Mātari was reborn in her next life as the daughter of a Vaiṣṇava family creates a delightful sense of wonder.

When news arrived that Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki had died, Kōvalaṉ’s mother and Kaṇṇaki’s mother gave up their lives. Mātari, having lost the person entrusted to her protection, entered fire and died. Of these three, Kōvalaṉ’s mother and Kaṇṇaki’s mother were reborn as twin girls in the womb of the wife of a merchant named Araṭṭaṉ Ceṭṭi in Vañji. Mātari was reborn in a family serving Tirumāl, who reclines in divine sleep upon the serpent couch at Tiruvaṉantapuram.

The first two, being members of the merchant community in their former births, were reborn as daughters of a merchant. Mātari, having been born among cowherds who served Tirumāl, having worshipped him, and having performed the Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai*, was reborn in a Vaiṣṇava family.

Thus Mātari shines as an excellent and radiant character in *Cilappatikāram*.


---

# 25. The Goldsmith’s Falsehood

The goldsmith who caused Kōvalaṉ’s death held the rank of royal goldsmith. He wore the garment and carried the staff appropriate to that office. He became the messenger of Yama to Kōvalaṉ and came accompanied by a hundred goldsmiths.

When Kōvalaṉ asked whether he could appraise the anklet, the liar bowed and replied modestly: “Though I am not skilled in valuing ornaments for the feet, I make crowns for kings.”

On seeing the anklet’s rich workmanship and value, he decided to use it as a substitute for the queen’s anklet he himself had stolen. He asked Kōvalaṉ to remain near his house and went to see the king.

## The Skill of Thieves

The Pāṇḍya king was on his way to the queen, intent on ending her estrangement through love, when the goldsmith told him that the thief who had stolen her anklet was waiting near his house. The king ordered: “Kill him and bring the anklet.”

The goldsmith returned with the guards and pointed out Kōvalaṉ. Seeing Kōvalaṉ’s appearance, some said that he could not possibly be a thief. The goldsmith opposed them. Thieves, he said, were accomplished in using eight weapons—spell, deity, drug, omen, stratagem, place, time, and instrument—and could deceive people through each in different ways.

He added an example. One night, while a prince slept, a thief stole the necklace from his chest. The prince immediately drew his sword. The thief snatched away the sword’s sheath. Whenever the prince thrust at him, the thief manoeuvred so that the blade entered the empty sheath; in the end he vanished. “Such men cannot be trusted,” the goldsmith said.

One of those present then recalled that on a previous night he had encountered a thief. When he raised his sword to kill him, the thief seized it and ran away. “They cannot be trusted,” he agreed. As they spoke, an ignorant and drunken guard struck Kōvalaṉ down with his sword.

After Kaṇṇaki pleaded her case and Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ died, his younger brother Veṟṟivēl Ceḻiyaṉ ascended the throne and is said to have sacrificed a thousand goldsmiths.

Through the wicked deed of one goldsmith, Kōvalaṉ, Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ, Kōpperuntēvi, Kaṇṇaki, Kaṇṇaki’s mother, Kōvalaṉ’s mother, Kauṇti, and Mātari all died. Did sacrificing a thousand goldsmiths bring any of them back?

Every profession has concealed practices. People commonly suspect that concealment may be somewhat greater in goldsmithing. Yet killing a thousand goldsmiths in a manner aimed at uprooting the entire community was also tyranny. One person’s act can bring disgrace upon a whole community.

When Mahatma Gandhi was killed by the Brahmin Nathuram Vinayak Godse, people for a time called other Brahmins “Godse.” That comparison is relevant here—but such collective condemnation was no more just.

This part of the story reveals that there was a science or manual of theft, that training in stealing existed, and that some people became accomplished practitioners. Even today it is said that young people are trained in theft in certain places.

Theft might gradually diminish if thieves received the death penalty. The Pāṇḍya king declared, “Killing a thief is no tyranny; it is the victory of the white spear,” and under the law of his time ordered the death of Kōvalaṉ, whom he believed to be a thief. Yet even while such a law existed, there were manuals and schools of theft and criminals like the goldsmith. The thought makes one’s head spin. In street theatre this goldsmith is called Vañcip Pattan.

Iḷaṅkō describes one thief as:

> “A thief who escaped through his learning.” (16:199)

That is, by means of his education and training in the science of theft, he moved away from the place and vanished. Even without studying a manual of theft, certain “scholars” who append all twenty-six letters from A to Z after their names engage in theft, bribery, and embezzlement. What then need be said of those who have received formal training in stealing?

## The Single Anklet

Although I condemn theft, forgive me for introducing an argument that might assist it. Iḷaṅkō ought to have made the goldsmith present one further piece of evidence to the Pāṇḍya king, but he omitted it.

When people sell a matched pair of ornaments, they generally sell both at the same time. If one sells only one of a pair of earrings, the earring being sold loses value, and the single earring left at home also loses value. If a new earring is later purchased to match it, the two will rarely look exactly alike; the replacement will be inferior. A person selling only one earring may therefore be suspected of having stolen it somewhere.

The same applies to an anklet. The goldsmith might have strengthened his accusation by saying: “The very fact that this man has brought only one anklet is sufficient proof that he stole it somewhere.” If this crafty argument that occurred to me had also occurred to Iḷaṅkō, the episode would have been even more effective. Let us proceed.

Every caste and profession has been mocked through degrading phrases, proverbs, and stories. I belong to the Kaikkōḷar community, and sayings exist that disparage us too. Just as some members of our community steal weaving thread, some goldsmiths steal gold. Members of other trades also try whatever dishonest hand they can manage.

Let this chapter therefore end with an appeal that workers of every profession conduct themselves with generosity and honesty.


---

# 26. The Excellence of Pukār and the Cōḻas

At the very beginning of the work—in its tenth line—Iḷaṅkō praises the city:

> “Let us praise flower-filled Pukār! Let us praise flower-filled Pukār!”

Pukār is the shortened alternative name of Kāvirippūmpaṭṭiṉam. Today, saying “I am going to the city” may mean going to Chennai. In that period, saying “I am going to the city” may similarly have meant going to Pukār.

## Iḷaṅkō’s Disappointment

Iḷaṅkō expresses the confidence that Potiyil, the Himalaya, and Pukār would remain forever without decline. Pukār, he says, possessed a distinctive excellence and such abundant opportunities and comforts that its ancient inhabitants never needed to migrate. Since wise people of complete learning and experience inhabited all three places, they would speak only of their stable endurance, never of their destruction:

> “Whether Potiyil or the Himalaya,
> or Pukār of incomparable excellence,
> enriched by ancient clans who know no migration—
> the exalted, who have completely mastered truth
> through learning and understanding,
> say only that these stand without trembling;
> they never speak of their decline.” (1:14–19)

Contrary to Iḷaṅkō’s faith, however, Pukār was swallowed by the sea. The land on which the city stood may have projected farther into the sea than the land on either side. When a cyclone caused the sea to surge, mountain-like waves may have risen and engulfed Pukār. Its fate would have resembled the sea’s swallowing of Dhanushkodi in 1952.

It would have been better had Iḷaṅkō written only:

> “Whether Potiyil or the Himalaya,
> or Pukār of incomparable excellence...”

The great disappointment comes from his additional claim that Pukār was inhabited by ancient clans who did not know migration.

There is reason to believe that Pukār’s old inhabitants did migrate. Nāṭṭukkōṭṭai Chettiars live in Pudukkottai and Ramanathapuram districts, and their region is often called Chettinad. Some Chettiar houses there are built with the ground floor raised upon a very high plinth.

In 1962, another Chettiar visited our house with the distinguished Tamil professor Dr S. Meyyappan of Annamalai University. He too became my friend, though I no longer remember his name. I asked him why houses in his region were built with their first floor raised on such a high foundation. He explained:

“Our people came from Kāvirippūmpaṭṭiṉam, Pukār. The sea destroyed Pukār, and even in the part that survived, household possessions were soaked and ruined. Disliking a coastal region haunted by fear of water, they moved to the present elevated country where there was no such danger. Still fearing that floods might come, they built their houses on high foundations approached by many steps.”

I do not know what other Chettiars think of this explanation. Whatever the truth, Iḷaṅkō’s line about “Pukār where ancient clans knew no migration” has become a source of disappointment. Yet Iḷaṅkō cannot be held responsible for nature’s divine play.

Pukār is said to have possessed pleasure and fame comparable to both the heavenly world and the Nāga country:

> “Pukār, where enduring fame and expansive delight flourish,
> like the great city of the Nāgas and the Nāga land itself.” (1:21–22)

The city was crowded with merchants of such wealth that kings desired it. It possessed the resources to receive the whole world as guests and provide whatever they needed. Goods from many countries accumulated by ship, pack animal, and other means, making the city resemble a gathering of many lands.

In Pukār, filled with heaps of wealth piled in countless crores, trade continued after evening in the merchant streets. Lamps shone before sellers of colours, sandal paste, flowers, crafted goods, sweet cakes, fish, and other wares. The lighthouse, the lamps on the fishermen’s boats, the lights used by foreigners, and the lamps carried by guards all poured out radiance.

Pukār contained many shrines and precincts: the Karpaka shrine, the White-Elephant shrine, the Nāga shrine, the Sun shrine, the Spear shrine, the Vajra shrine, the Nigaṇṭha shrine, the Moon shrine, and others.

The city was divided into two great quarters. The eastern half along the shore was Maruvūrppākkam; the western half was Paṭṭiṉappākkam.

Maruvūrppākkam contained ornament mansions and warehouses; dwellings of *mlecchas* and other foreigners; streets of those who sold cosmetic paste, lime powder, sandal paste, flowers, garlands, and eaglewood; streets of artisans who fashioned objects from many metals and kinds of wood; tailors; leather workers; flower-stringers; a street of bards who sang with many instruments; and streets of people engaged in small and disreputable occupations. These details, with the relevant verse, have also been discussed under the chapter “Commerce.”

Paṭṭiṉappākkam contained streets occupied by the king, ministers, soldiers, Brahmins, merchants, court eulogists, Māgadha bards, physicians, astrologers, pearl-stringers, conch-cutters, timekeepers, military commanders, actors, courtesans, comic performers, and festival-drum players. The royal palace district lay in this quarter.

When one considers these two distinct quarters, one is tempted to think that division exists everywhere and in everything.

Between them lay the day-market, just as a battlefield lies between the camps of two hostile kings. There stood the shrine and sacrificial altar of the spirit who had aided the Cōḻa king Mucukunta. Sacrifices, dances, and songs were performed at the altar. Other institutions and events in Pukār included the following.

## The Altar of Human Sacrifice

From the time of Mucukunta Cōḻa, warriors are said to have offered their own lives. The warriors of Maruvūrppākkam and those of Paṭṭiṉappākkam competed with one another, blessing the king with victory and cutting off their own heads as sacrifice. We may infer that suicide troops existed even then.

A still more severe form of human sacrifice appears in the *Kaliṅkattupparaṇi*. Warriors cut their heads off at the base of the neck and place them in the hands of the goddess Koṟṟavai. The severed head praises her, while the headless trunk stands worshipping her:

> “They sever the long head at the base of the neck;
> they place the severed head in the goddess’s hand;
> the offered head praises victorious Koṟṟavai;
> the diminished body stands with hands joined in worship.”
> (*The Singing of the Temple*, 15)

The human sacrifice in the *Kaliṅkattupparaṇi* seems even more terrible than that in *Cilappatikāram*.

A pavilion in one part of Pukār contained the pearl canopy, debate hall, and ornamental gateway that Karikāla had obtained from defeated enemies.

**The Veḷḷiṭai Hall:** A place where merchants’ bundles were stored. A person who stole one was made to carry it around the city.

**The Ilañci Hall:** The sick were cured by bathing there.

**The Neṭuṅkal Hall:** Those driven mad by poison or medicine, those who had swallowed poison, and those possessed by spirits were cured by circumambulating this hall. Its stone emitted light.

**The Spirit Square:** A spirit there seized and devoured false ascetics, unchaste people, evil ministers, those who desired another’s spouse, and false witnesses.

## The Hall of the Image

This hall contained an image that shed tears silently whenever the king’s sceptre bent from justice or judges abandoned impartiality.

During the Indra festival, sacred fires were maintained in the temples of many gods, and festivities were held for the Thirty-Three and other deities. Jain and Buddhist establishments conducted special events. Musicians of many traditions performed. Royal functionaries joined together to install Indra ceremonially and conduct the festival.

During the celebrations, pleasure-seekers such as Kōvalaṉ roamed the streets in delight. Numerous public women moved among them. Their breasts brushed against the chests of men in the crowd, and the painted cosmetic designs upon the women’s breasts stained the men. Fearing that their wives would suspect and scold them if they returned home with such marks, the men went home accompanied by guests. After all, guests are included among those who can mediate and end a wife’s estrangement.

Tirunīlakaṇṭa Nāyaṉār suffered a similar “accident.” Perhaps he did not go home with a guest. His wife suspected him and declared, “Do not touch me.” From then onward he would not even turn to look at bodies draped in women’s saris. Paṭṭiṉattār kept him in mind when he sang, “I am not able, through a woman’s vow, to renounce youth.”

Iḷaṅkō created this scene for literary flavour. Deliberate bodily brushing in a crowd might occur, but matters would hardly become so serious that every man required a guest as protection.

The excellence of the Cōḻa lineage is as worthy of praise as that of Pukār. When the goddess Madurāpati addressed Kaṇṇaki, she recalled the Cōḻa king Cibi, who cut flesh from his own body for a dove, and Manu Nīti Cōḻa, who drove his chariot over his son to render justice to a cow whose calf had been killed.

Kaṇṇaki herself narrated the celebrated histories of chaste women of the Cōḻa country.

The epic records several Cōḻa victories: because he had no enemies in the south or west, Karikāla marched northward, won victories, struck the Himalaya with his goad, and engraved the tiger emblem there.

It also magnifies other glories of the dynasty—Mucukunta Cōḻa’s aid to Indra and Toṭittōṭ Cempiyaṉ’s destruction of the suspended fort, among many others.

A Tamil maxim says that women’s chastity can be protected and flourish only when a king rules justly with an upright sceptre. Kaṇṇaki, by the firmness of her chastity, is said to have assigned the fame she attained to the Cōḻa king:

> “Unless mighty kings administer justice,
> chastity cannot flourish among women of great renown—
> so declared the ancient, gracious Tamil wisdom.
> Because she was the Pattini whom the world worships and praises,
> she bestowed that glory upon the kings
> whose heads are adorned with the *ātti* garland.” (28:207–211)

The *Maṇimēkalai* expresses the same principle:

> “The austerity of ascetics and the chastity of women
> cannot exist when the ruler’s protection does not.” (23:208–209)

Thus the excellence of Pukār and the greatness of the Cōḻa lineage receive their proper place in *Cilappatikāram*.


---

# 27. The Excellence of Madurai and the Pāṇḍya

> “Lofty Madurai, rich in mansions,
> and the immense glory of the Pāṇḍya,
> distinguished in celebrated song.”
> (*Prologue*, 19–20)

The proem of *Cilappatikāram* thus celebrates both Madurai and the Pāṇḍya. Madurai’s distinctions belong to its king, and the king’s distinctions belong to Madurai. Let us examine some of both.

In the “Canto of Lodging in the Outskirts,” Kōvalaṉ tells Kauṇti that Kaṇṇaki’s feet cannot endure walking by day upon the stony road heated by the fierce sun. They may instead travel at night, he says, because under Pāṇḍya rule there is no cause for fear. The powerful bear kills no traveller and digs up no anthill; the striped tiger does not attack herds of deer; crocodiles, snakes, spirits, and thunder harm no living being. The fame of the land protected by the just sceptre of the southern king has spread everywhere:

> “The strong-seizing bear digs up no cruel anthill;
> the sword-striped tiger assails no herd of deer;
> serpent, spirit, prey-seeking crocodile, and thunder
> cause no affliction to those who pass.
> Great indeed is the fame spread everywhere:
> ‘This is the land guarded by the just sceptre
> of the southern king.’” (13:5–10)

It is a literary convention to imagine that where righteous people, ascetics, and sages dwell, wolf and lamb drink at one ford and tiger and deer sleep in one place. Many works contain such descriptions. Iḷaṅkō speaks in that tradition through Kōvalaṉ, but he has a particular purpose in placing the statement here; that will become clear later.

Kōvalaṉ mixed simply with the travelling bards he met and sang with them. He asked how far Madurai remained. They replied:

“The wind of Madurai, steeped in many fragrant pastes, flowers, and incenses, is already blowing upon us. Though poets praise the breeze from Potiyil, it does not equal Madurai’s wind because it is not mingled with such perfumes. Since the Madurai breeze has begun to touch us, the prosperous ancient city of the Pāṇḍya cannot be far. Even a solitary traveller will meet no one who obstructs or troubles him; the road is so secure that you may proceed by night.”

> “Not like the Potiyil breeze exalted
> by the red tongues of poets,
> behold—the Madurai breeze has come here.
> His ancient city, rich in sacred prosperity,
> is no great distance away;
> even if you go alone, none will bar your way.” (13:130–134)

Madurai’s wind is said to surpass even the breeze from Potiyil, and a person may travel alone in Pāṇḍya territory without fear. What a delightful description!

Yet in the latter half of the scientifically advanced and highly civilised twentieth century—1992—not only can one not travel alone; sometimes one cannot travel safely even in a group. When will the human herd reform?

As the three travellers approached Madurai, the drums of temples, the recitation of the Vedas by *antaṇar*, the war-drums of soldiers, elephants’ trumpeting, horses’ neighing, and wrestlers’ drums roared like the sea, sounding as though the city had come forth to welcome them:

> “As the tumultuous Kūṭal roared like the dark sea,
> its swelling sound came forth to meet them.” (13:149–150)

The phrase “came forth to meet them” may simply mean “sounded before them,” but it is more attractive to understand it as a ceremonial welcome. When honoured persons enter a town they may be received with drums and music; the passage resembles that. This is not literal fact but poetic attribution.

Could it also, playfully, mean that the sound was “contrary” to them—an ominous reception—because none of the three would live in Madurai and all would die? That is only an amusing secondary interpretation.

They next reached the Vaigai, whose water was hidden from sight. As though the good woman Vaigai knew the death awaiting Kaṇṇaki and did not wish to look upon the travellers in grief, she covered her entire body with a garment. The “garment” was the mass of fragrant flowers floating upon the current. She also restrained her tears:

> “The unfailing banner-woman of the ancient line,
> the Vaigai, as though she knew what would befall the lady,
> covered herself in a garment of holy fragrant flowers
> and concealed and restrained the long water filling her eyes.” (13:170–173)

Iḷaṅkō gives a double meaning to “the long water filling her eyes.” The word *kaṇ* can mean both eye and place. The Vaigai restrained the waters filling its channel and flowing from afar, keeping them within its banks so that they did not overflow, destroy anything, or frighten the travellers. The line combines punning and poetic attribution: a natural river covered in flowers and held within its banks is represented as deliberately behaving so for their sake.

The three crossed the Vaigai on a wooden raft, reached its southern bank, circled Madurai, and lodged in an outer settlement.

The blue lilies, water-lilies, and lotuses along their road seemed to know beyond doubt the suffering awaiting husband and wife. They grieved to sorrowful music, shed tears, and trembled when the wind touched them:

> “The dark long blue lilies, water-lilies, and lotuses,
> as though they knew without doubt
> the lonely grief awaiting the woman and her husband,
> heard the musical bees lament in compassion;
> they shed tears and trembled as the wind struck.” (13:184–188)

To put it bluntly, the bees beat their breasts for wages and sang funeral music on behalf of the flowers. The flowers paid the bees in honey. “Tears” also carries the sense of intoxicating liquid—honey—and “trembling when the foot touched” also means swaying when the wind (*kāl*) blew. This too is poetic attribution.

The long cloth banners flying above the walls stretched toward the travellers as though extending hands to stop them and warn, “Do not come to Madurai”:

> “The long flags upon the hard-won fortress,
> waving across their path, showed hands
> as though saying, ‘Do not come.’” (13:189–190)

Literature often represents banners moving in the wind as either barring someone or welcoming them. In Villiputtūrār’s *Bhārata*, the flags on Kṛṣṇa’s walls seem to stop Duryodhana, who has come seeking military aid:

> “Though you have come here, our cloud-hued lord of beauty
> will not become an ally in war for any but the Pāṇḍavas.
> ‘Turn back and go,’ the splendid banners
> upon every crest of the vast wall seemed to bar him with their hands.”
> (*Uttiyōka Paruvam*, 2:6)

That is a poem of refusal. By contrast, in the “Vision of Mithilā” in Kampaṉ’s *Rāmāyaṇa*, Mithilā’s banners welcome Rāma, Viṣṇu incarnate, who has come to marry Sītā:

> “Thinking, ‘The red goddess left the spotless lotus
> and, through our austerity, came to dwell here,’
> the fragrant city stretched out the hands
> of its rich jewelled banners toward the lotus-red-eyed lord,
> as though calling, ‘Come swiftly!’” (1)

*Ollai vā* means “come quickly.” The violent fluttering of the banners appears like an urgent summons.

Leaving Kaṇṇaki with Kauṇti, Kōvalaṉ toured ancient Madurai. He saw streets of perfume-sellers; courtesans’ streets; a bazaar of many goods; streets selling the nine gems; cloth shops; grain markets; quarters of different communities; three-way and four-way junctions; temples; bazaars; sacrificial halls; branching lanes; and streets shaded like canopied avenues by flags and standards.

In Madurai, drums, conches, and horns sounded each morning at the temples of Śiva, Tirumāl, Baladēva, and Murukaṉ, at the dwellings of the righteous, and at the king’s palace.

The market streets were crowded with carts, elephant-goads, fly-whisks, leather shields, armour, spears, objects of copper and bronze, weapons, garlands, elephant tusks, tools, and varieties of sandal paste.

Gold shops sold four kinds of gold: Cātarūpam, Kiḷicciṟai, Āṭakam, and Cāmpūnatam.

Textile shops stacked hundreds of each kind of garment made from cotton, silk, fine animal hair, and other fibres.

In the grain-market street, brokers moved with balances and measuring vessels. Bundles of pepper, areca nut, and other goods filled the place, and sixteen kinds of grain were sold.

The fortress walls carried self-acting mechanical weapons: devices that shot arrows; monkey mechanisms; stone-slings; machines holding boiling oil and molten copper; baskets of stones; hooks; devices that twisted necks or bit skulls; machines that hurled climbers from the walls; impaling stakes; arrow-bundling mechanisms; needle traps; kingfisher devices that pecked out eyes; boar, striking-bamboo, elephant, snake, eagle, tiger, cart, and ram mechanisms. These were designed to attack enemies automatically.

## The Lunar Dynasty

In the evening sky, the young crescent appears, defeats the petty chiefs of darkness called evening, spreads white rays, and rules the kingdom of the stars. How can it do this? Because, though young, it is the ancestor of the Pāṇḍya dynasty, whose kings defeat hostile rulers even in youth. The Pāṇḍyas were said to belong to the lunar line:

> “Though young, they destroy hostile kings—
> because it is the first of that battle-great southern line,
> the white crescent appears in the evening sky,
> drives away the petty chiefs of sorrowful darkness,
> spreads its milk-white rays without swerving from order,
> and, as a silver lamp, rules the kingdom of stars.” (4:21–26)

The idea that Pāṇḍyas defeated enemies while still young also occurs in the *Puṟanāṉūṟu*. Kārikkaṇṇaṉār of Kāvirippūmpaṭṭiṉam praised the Pāṇḍya Peruvaluti who died at Veḷḷiyampalam as a bull of the five Pāṇḍyas, able to destroy enemies as even a small thunderbolt can destroy a great race of serpents:

> “Though young, like the rare thunderbolt
> that strikes and destroys a branching serpent clan,
> you are the battle-great bull of the five Pāṇḍyas,
> intolerant of warriors who oppose you.” (58:6–8)

The image of a small thunderbolt suggests victory over enemies even in childhood.

Iṭaikkuṉṟūr Kiḻār similarly praised Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ, victor of the battle at Talaiyālaṅkāṉam. The king was a boy who still wore children’s tinkling anklets and the five-weapon amulet that protected infants. When enemies arrived, the child removed the anklets, put on the warrior’s anklets, cast away the protective amulet, stopped drinking milk, ate solid rice, went to war, and won:

> “He removed the tinkling anklets and fastened bright warrior’s anklets...
> today he gave up milk and ate solid food...
> the new warriors who came against him in anger...
> he struck them down so that they fell upon the earth;
> yet he neither rejoiced nor swelled with pride.” (77)

Against the background of such traditions, Iḷaṅkō says that “though young, the southern kings drive away hostile rulers.”

The moon bears the name *uṭupati*, “lord of the stars,” in works including Kampaṉ’s *Rāmāyaṇa*. The expression must be read within ancient cosmology, not through modern science. Because the moon appears larger than the stars at night, it was called their lord. Mythology also treats the twenty-seven stars from Aśvinī to Rēvati as women and the moon as their husband. Iḷaṅkō’s phrase “king of the stars” carries this sense.

## The Tall One

The “Canto of Burning” says that the Pāṇḍya conquered lands, wielded a just sceptre, eliminated cruel practices, established lasting victory and fame, and protected the world:

> “The Tall One of celebrated distinction,
> who gained the earth and wielded the just sceptre,
> cut down cruel practices, secured victory,
> enlarged enduring fame, and guarded the fourfold world.” (22:57–60)

“The Tall One” denotes a Pāṇḍya king and is especially associated with the Pāṇḍya who stood while the sea washed his feet. The *Puṟanāṉūṟu* calls him “the Tall One of the festival of the three seas” (9:10). The *Maturaikkāñci* likewise speaks of:

> “The Tall One, garlanded upon the chest,
> whose great benefaction gave land,” (60–61)

and:

> “Like the Tall One of earth-giving fortune,
> of fame made splendid by united assemblies.” (62–63)

The element “long” in the name Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ may derive from the title Neṭiyōṉ, “the Tall One.” In the four lines from *Cilappatikāram* quoted above, Iḷaṅkō repeatedly portrays the Pāṇḍya from several angles.

## Madurāpati’s Praise

The city-goddess Madurāpati praises the Pāṇḍya to Kaṇṇaki after the city has been burned. Under his rule, she says, no sound had ever been heard except the recitation of sacred learning; the bell of inquiry had never rung. Apart from enemy kings who refused to bow at his feet, no citizen had ever accused him of tyranny:

> “Except the sound of the tongue of sacred learning,
> he had never heard the sound of the bell’s tongue.
> Apart from kings who would not bow at his feet,
> he was no ruler whom his subjects reviled.” (23:31–34)

She continues that the Pāṇḍya line bore no stain concerning women. It descended from the Golden-Handed Pāṇḍya, who cut off his own hand so that no reproach might arise concerning the wife of a Brahmin named Kīrantai. It also descended from the king who not only released the Brahmin Vārttikaṉ, wrongly imprisoned by royal officers, but freed all the other prisoners as well.

Madurāpati therefore declares that Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ, whom Kaṇṇaki accuses, was “born in a distinguished lineage joined to righteous conduct” (23:40).

The Pāṇḍyas were said to have made the northern golden mountain—Mount Mēru—their boundary. Iḷaṅkō indicates this by the phrase “he whose boundary was the golden peak” (23:12).

The deified Kaṇṇaki herself says:

> “The southern king was without evil;
> he became an honoured guest
> in the temple of the king of the gods.” (29:10)

Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ will always possess the distinction of straightening, with the life that left his body, the cruel sceptre that had bent.

## The Pāṇḍya’s Blame

I remember hearing in childhood, in street theatre or a stage play, Kaṇṇaki singing to the king during their debate: “O vengeful Pāṇḍya who repays blame with blame!”

Yet this chapter has so far described his excellence at length. Iḷaṅkō’s stylus even makes the palace gatekeeper bless him as “the Pāṇḍya untouched by blame.” Some researchers join Iḷaṅkō in great efforts to rescue the king from reproach. They cite the compassionate statement placed in the mouth of the rival king Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ:

> “The king straightened with his departing life
> the sceptre that cruel karma had bent,
> and made it once more a just sceptre.” (25:98–99)

But why did the sceptre bend, and why did it then need straightening? Only because it had bent did it require correction. To save the king from blame, the offence is assigned to karma: “cruel karma bent it.” If so, a murderer too kills only through karma—why then impose the death penalty upon him?

The queen had become estranged and withdrawn from the king. Eager to end her estrangement, he hurried toward her apartments. The goldsmith intercepted him and announced that the thief who stole the anklet had been caught. Without investigation, without consulting an assembly or delivering a considered judgment, the king ordered: “Kill him and bring the anklet.” Can the world, then or now, accept that the fault belonged to karma? Would it not mock Tamilakam for having possessed so degraded a king? Perhaps the experiential saying “love has no eyes” arose precisely because this catastrophe resulted from sexual estrangement and the rush toward reconciliation.

The king’s own death is sufficient evidence that he was guilty beyond measure. He must have reflected deeply: “Because Kōpperuntēvi withdrew from me, I hurried to resolve our quarrel; on that path I believed the goldsmith and committed this enormous wrong.” His heartbeat then stopped. Today this would be called a heart attack.

There is still a way for human society to forgive him. Had he been a tyrant, he could have comforted himself that an unfortunate error had occurred, sentenced the goldsmith to death, begged Kaṇṇaki’s forgiveness, offered her assistance, and closed the case. Instead, before news of his failure could spread to other kings and peoples, he gave up his life. Iḷaṅkō makes Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ say:

> “The king of kings grieved and said:
> ‘Before the words that he had departed from justice
> could reach the ears of rulers like ourselves,
> he caused the news that his life had left its abode
> to reach us first.’” (25:94–97)

Before others heard that the Pāṇḍya’s sceptre had failed, they first heard that he was dead. There is therefore no path left but to forgive his blameworthy act.

## Kōpperuntēvi’s Share of the Fault

When the king died, Kōpperuntēvi too gave up her life, proving that, like Kaṇṇaki, she possessed a fierce form of marital chastity. If a person’s mother dies, another may console them by saying, “I will be like your mother and help you.” But if a woman’s husband dies, one cannot say, “I will be your husband; do not grieve.” That cultural truth is expressed in the line:

> “For those who have lost a husband, there is none who can be shown in his place.” (20:80)

The queen’s death was caused not only by the loss of her husband; she shared in his blame, a point most people fail to consider. Instead of simply accepting that her husband had enjoyed the dances and songs of the women of Kūṭal, she imagined a hidden meaning, became estranged, left the public assembly, and went to the inner palace. The king therefore hurried after her to end the quarrel. On the way, the goldsmith accomplished his design. The queen must have understood her own role in this; death followed.

On hearing the goldsmith, the king could have thought: “The queen’s estrangement can be resolved later; first let us investigate the theft.” He might have consulted his ministers and rendered justice. There may, however, have been another reason why he gave priority to the queen—one that Iḷaṅkō perhaps forgot to mention. Her anger may partly have arisen because the king had still not recovered her missing anklet. He may therefore have placed first importance on finding it. Like the crow alighting at the exact moment the palmyra fruit falls, the goldsmith appeared then and said that the thief had been caught. The king may have ordered his men, in accordance with royal law, to kill the supposed thief and recover the anklet.

The death penalty is imposed for murder. The king and queen—who killed Kōvalaṉ or caused him to be killed—imposed that penalty upon themselves. By their own deaths they gave renewed lustre to the fame of the Pāṇḍyas.


---
