# 28. The Excellence of Vañji and the Cēras

The Cēras who ruled from Vañji possessed many distinctions. The Cēra king Māntarañcēral Irumpōṟai is said to have granted the Tamil Vedic Brahmin Kōtamaṉ life in the upper world through a sacrifice.

A Brahmin named Parācaraṉ, who came from the Cōḻa country, received abundant wealth from a Cēra king and blessed him thus:

> “Long live the mighty one whose victory has ripened to fullness!
> Long live the guardian who felled the sea’s *kaṭampu* tree!
> Long live the king who engraved his curved bow upon the mountain cleft!
> Long live the Poraiyaṉ of the cool, flower-filled Porunai!
> Long live King Māntarañ Cēral!” (23:80–84)

When Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ learned that Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ had died because his sceptre departed from justice, he said that kingship was a difficult profession. If rain fails, or if subjects die unjustly, the world says that the king’s sceptre has failed. The thought produces great fear. To be born into a royal line obliged to protect the people justly while fearing tyranny is therefore a source of suffering rather than an honour worthy of worship:

> “If the wealth of rain is withheld, there is vast fear;
> if life is lost through error, there is greater fear.
> Guarding the people and fearing the tyrant’s sceptre,
> birth in the noble line that protects humankind
> brings suffering; it is not a thing to be worshipped.” (25:100–104)

Comparable passages include the *Puṟanāṉūṟu*:

> “Though the rain may fail and the reservoirs diminish,
> this wide world blames its guardians.” (35)

The *Periyapurāṇam* says of Manu:

> “He did not think, ‘He is the one son of my own line.’
> Knowing it his duty to follow the path of justice,
> King Manu drove the wheel of his chariot
> over the tender body of his own son.
> Is rare kingship difficult—or is it easy?”
> (*Tiruvārūr*, 44)

The *Tirukkuṟaḷ* likewise says:

> “In the land of the king who wields the sceptre according to nature,
> rain and harvest gather together.” (545)

and:

> “If a king acts in violation of justice,
> the sky will violate its order and refuse to rain.” (559)

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ was a great warrior who completed what he resolved. Unable to endure the northern kings’ contemptuous words about the southern rulers, he swore a fierce oath: if he went north and failed to return with a stone from the Himalaya for making an image of Kaṇṇaki, he would be a sword incapable of making enemies tremble in battle and a tyrant whose sceptre made his own people tremble:

> “Unless I bring a stone upon the crowned heads
> of the kings of the northern quarter
> and fashion the deity,
> if my sword returns empty,
> may I become one who, in the fierce splendour of war
> with close-set warrior’s anklets,
> cannot make hostile kings tremble,
> but whose sceptre makes the people
> of the prosperous land tremble instead.” (26:13–18)

He vowed to bring the stone borne upon the crowns of northern kings. The expression “to fashion the god” is delightful. Instead of merely saying, “If I return empty-handed,” he says, “If my sword returns empty,” which is even more vivid.

In the “Canto of the Water Consecration,” Māṭalaṉ praised Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ: “At Nēri Gate, in a single day, you destroyed and defeated the nine collateral Cōḻa kings who came to fight your kinsman Kiḷḷi.” Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s mother Naṟcōṇai was the daughter of the Cōḻa Maṇakkiḷḷi; Maṇakkiḷḷi’s son Kiḷḷi was therefore Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s maternal cousin. This victory is also recorded elsewhere in the epic:

> “He defeated in fixed battle at Nēri Gate
> the nine kings wearing abundant *ātti* garlands.” (28:116–117)

The fifth decad’s prologue in the *Patiṟṟuppattu* also records it:

> “He encamped outside the gate
> and, in insatiable war, caused nine claimants
> of the Cōḻa line to fall,
> cutting them down in established battle.” (Prologue 5:18–20)

The Cēra also defeated the minor ruler Paḻaiyaṉ of Mōkūr by cutting down his long neem, the community’s guardian tree:

> “With the strength of his uplifted sword,
> he cut at the root the long-branched neem
> guarded by Paḻaiyaṉ.” (27:124–125)

The *Patiṟṟuppattu* similarly says:

> “He caused the entire trunk, down to its full root,
> of the dark-branched neem guarded by Paḻaiyaṉ
> to be cut apart.” (Prologue 5:13–14)

His other achievements included victory at Iṭumpil and driving the sea backward.

Several further distinctions belong to Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ:

- He fulfilled his mother’s wish by taking her to bathe in the Gaṅgā.
- He praised the Pāṇḍya king’s honourable death.
- Though Kanaka and Vijaya were defeated captives, he treated them with dignity, housed them in a separate palace, and provided every necessary comfort.
- When he released Kanaka and Vijaya, he freed other prisoners as well.
- He remitted taxes.
- He possessed the honour that could not endure northern kings insulting southern rulers.
- He carved an image of Kaṇṇaki, built her a temple, and established her worship.

Because no event comparable to those in Pukār and Madurai occurs in Vañji, the city’s own excellence does not receive equal space in the epic.

## Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s Birth

There is disagreement about Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s parentage. The following passage in the prose interlude of the “Canto of Blessing” shows that he was the son born to Neṭuñcēralātaṉ through Naṟcōṇai, daughter of the Cōḻa Maṇakkiḷḷi:

> “Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, who fought the people of Koṅku at Ceṅkaḷam
> and reached the bank of the great Gaṅgā,
> was the son whom the radiant daughter of the sun-like Cōḻa
> bore to Cēralātaṉ, who ruled the world
> by placing one language from Kumari to the northern Himalaya.”

Here “the Cōḻa” is Maṇakkiḷḷi and “the Cōḻa’s daughter” is Naṟcōṇai. The statement was written by Iḷaṅkō, their second son.

But the fifth-decad prologue of the classical *Patiṟṟuppattu* reads:

> “Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, the son whom the Cōḻa Maṇakkiḷḷi bore
> to Neṭuñcēralātaṉ, lord of the Kuṭavar,
> desiring a stone for the divine Pattini...
> drove the sea backward.”

The relationship expressed here is wrong. It makes one man the son born from another man’s womb. Instead of “the son whom the Cōḻa Maṇakkiḷḷi bore,” it should have read: “Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, the son born from the womb of the daughter whom Cōḻa Maṇakkiḷḷi begot.” Scribes copying the palm-leaf manuscript may accidentally have omitted the two words “daughter’s womb” between “begot” and “son.”

## Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s Religion

Several internal passages in *Cilappatikāram* suggest that Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ was a Śaiva:

> “He bowed his head, which bowed to none,
> circumambulating and adorning it with the victorious Vañji garland
> before the red feet of the exalted one
> whose vast dark head bears the moon’s ray
> and whose form contains the world.” (26:54–57)

> “O celestial child who appeared in Vañji,
> radiant through the grace of the lord of red matted hair!” (26:98–99)

> “The king who appeared through the grace
> of the bull-riding lord
> and made the great earth shine.” (30:141–142)

> “As some stood praising with the sacred remnant
> of him who reclines in divine sleep in the Āṭaka hall,
> because he had placed upon his jewelled crown
> the radiant red feet of the god of red matted hair
> who conceals the clear water,
> he received that remnant
> and bore it upon his ornamented jewelled shoulder.” (26:62–67)

“The exalted one whose head bears the moon,” “the celestial lord of red matted hair,” “the bull-riding lord,” and “the god of red matted hair” all denote Śiva. These passages show Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ as a Śaiva. Yet he was no enemy of other religions; Śaivism merely held first place for him.

## Love of Language

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ possessed great attachment to Tamil, his mother tongue. Iḷaṅkō makes him transfer the martial valour and power of Tamils onto the language itself:

> “Kanaka and Vijaya, whose tongues knew no restraint,
> did not understand the power of difficult, noble Tamil.” (26:159–161)

Iḷaṅkō also sings, “Let us behold the power of southern Tamil” (26:185), and “the power of southern Tamil belonging to the king with close-set warrior’s anklets” (27:5). The younger brother possessed the same love of the mother tongue as his elder brother. Alas, Tamil!


---

# 29. Notable and Delightful Details

Let us now examine, canto by canto, a selection of the notable and delightful details found in *Cilappatikāram*.

## The Song of Auspicious Blessing

### The Wedding Invitation

Half a century ago, most families did not print invitations when a wedding took place. As a boy, I saw people go from house to house, offer betel leaf and areca nut, and invite others orally. Even relatives living in other towns were visited in person and invited with betel and areca.

Today, through an abundance of money, invitations are printed at great expense. There may be one invitation from both families, another in the name of the women, and so on. Some even announce the wedding in newspapers and ask readers to treat the advertisement itself as an invitation. The amount now spent on an invitation for a wealthy wedding might once have paid for my grandfather’s entire marriage.

In ancient Pukār, the families of Kaṇṇaki and Kōvalaṉ were both extremely wealthy. They therefore seated women upon elephants and sent them through every street of Pukār to announce the marriage. This was an affair of great houses. Even men today may fear an elephant; it is striking that women then rode upon one to make the announcement:

> “They seated ornamented women upon the backs of elephants
> and gave the marriage to the great city.” (1:43–44)

The expression “gave the marriage to the city” is particularly beautiful and delightful.

Today, invitations printed by the bride’s family generally put the bride’s name first and the groom’s second; the groom’s family reverses the order. Iḷaṅkō, composing the equivalent of a wedding invitation in *Cilappatikāram*, places Kaṇṇaki before Kōvalaṉ. Was Iḷaṅkō a member of the bride’s family? Of course not.

The title *Cilappatikāram*—“The Account of the Anklet”—arises from Kaṇṇaki’s anklet. The heroine occupies a more indispensable place in the epic than the hero. Perhaps this is why Kaṇṇaki receives first position.

Or perhaps her name precedes her husband’s by the same principle found in names such as Umāpati, Umāmahēśvaraṉ, Ambikāpati, Mīṉāṭcicuntaraṉ, Vaḷḷināyakaṉ, Vaḷḷimaṇāḷaṉ, Citti Vināyakar, Lakṣmīkāntaṉ, Lakṣmīnārāyaṇaṉ, Rādhākṛṣṇaṉ, Sītārāmaṉ, and Jānakīrāmaṉ, where a woman’s name introduces the man’s. If Iḷaṅkō appeared before us now, we could ask him the exact reason. Perhaps it was simply the convention of his time. Or, as a Cēra from a region later associated with matrilineal inheritance, did he give women first place?

## The Canto Celebrating the Evening

### Jasmine Grown in the House

A flower-spread bed is described as:

> “A couch of many flowers where jasmine grown in the house
> and the flowering *mallikai* had opened.” (4:27–28)

In the “Canto of Lodging in the Outskirts,” a garland of *mādhavi*, *mallikai*, and jasmine is likewise called:

> “A spreading garland of *mādhavi*, *mallikai*,
> and jasmine grown in the house.” (13:120–121)

Chaste women are said to have cultivated jasmine in their homes. The expressions “jasmine grown in the house” and “jasmine grown in the dwelling” indicate this. The same idea appears in the *Kuṟuntokai*:

> “Her many-dark tresses smell
> of the jasmine at the house-front.” (19:4–5)

in the *Naṟṟiṇai*:

> “In the middle of the house, jasmine buds open in season.” (115:6)

in the *Akanāṉūṟu*:

> “Pointing to the great branch of the household jasmine.” (23:12)

and in the *Peruṅkatai*:

> “Jasmine rising from the house mingled with *mallikai*
> and spread a great fragrance.” (1:33:73–74)

Many other works contain the same association. Drawing on this tradition, Oṭṭakkūttar even says in the *Takkayākapparani* that Umā herself cultivated jasmine. Its hundred thousand branches climbed into the sky and brushed the moon; the deer-mark upon the moon, fearing that the plant belonged to the goddess, did not graze upon it:

> “The jasmine that the sharp-horned goddess never released
> rose into the sky with a hundred thousand branches;
> its cool shoots touched the moon’s horn,
> and the fawn there did not graze them,
> thinking them forbidden.” (75)

Because jasmine was grown in the houses of chaste women, the *Āciriyanikaṇṭu* gives “chastity” as one of its names (137). Many other literary works likewise connect jasmine and chastity.

### The Whiteness of Fame

It is a literary convention to describe fame as white. In the lines:

> “The white crescent appeared in the evening sky
> as a silver lamp ruling the kingdom of stars.” (4:23–26)

commentators also interpret *viḷakkam*, “radiance,” as fame. Fame is represented as silver-white.

Parimēlaḻakar and others give the meaning “fame” to *viḷakkam* in the *Tirukkuṟaḷ*:

> “If the grievous disease called enmity departs,
> it will yield unfailing and imperishable radiance.” (853)

Kampaṉ’s *Rāmāyaṇa* says that white banners spread like fame and like the white waves of a celestial river:

> “The great army’s banners rolled in the wind like fame
> and spread without bound like the white waves
> of the river of the sky.” (5.9.7)

The moon’s white light also seemed as though Rāma’s fame had entered and wandered there:

> “It resembled the splendour
> of that man’s fame entering and roaming.” (5.1.65)

Kampaṉ uses the idea elsewhere too. Sivappirakācar writes in the *Pirapuliṅkalīlai* that white Mount Kailāsa appeared like the accumulated fame of Śiva:

> “The prosperous hill of Kailāsa,
> where the generous lord’s white fame had gathered.” (2.4)

### Kōvalaṉ’s Absence

Because Kōvalaṉ had left Kaṇṇaki, her feet lost their anklets; her waist lost its girdle; her breasts lost the writing of red cosmetic paste; her ears lost their heavy earrings; all her limbs lost ornaments other than the auspicious marriage-thread; her face lost its fine perspiration; her eyes lost collyrium; her forehead lost its mark; her white, radiant smile lost Kōvalaṉ; and her dark hair forgot its oil:

> “Her lovely little red feet were without their ornamental anklets;
> the girdle left her soft-clothed waist;
> she drew no red paste upon the courtyard of her breasts;
> she rejoiced in no ornament except the auspicious one;
> abandoning her heavy earrings, her ears hung bare;
> fine perspiration fled her moon-bright face;
> her long red-fish eyes forgot their collyrium;
> her coral-radiant forehead lost its mark;
> her white shining smile lost Kōvalaṉ;
> her dark hair forgot the adornment of oil—
> Kaṇṇaki, whose heart had lost all means of action.” (4:47–57)

This is delightful because Iḷaṅkō does not merely say she did not wear each item; he says that each part had “lost” something. He also varies the words—left, departed, fled, forgot, lost—within one meaning. Because there was no union with her husband, her face lost the perspiration of intimacy.

A description proceeding from the feet upward is called *pādādi kēśam* in Sanskrit; one proceeding from hair downward is *kēśādi pādam*. In Tamil we may simply say “from foot to head” and “from head to foot.” Here Iḷaṅkō follows the foot-to-head order, beginning with the feet losing the anklet and ending with the hair forgetting oil. The method is particularly appropriate to a work centred upon an anklet: “the anklet departed” rightly receives first place.

I have interpreted “the white shining smile lost Kōvalaṉ” to mean that the smile itself lost him. Commentators instead say that Kōvalaṉ lost Kaṇṇaki’s smile. That does not fit the pattern of the surrounding lines and destroys the refinement of the conception. Her smile appeared only before Kōvalaṉ. Since he was absent, the smile itself no longer existed; it had lost Kōvalaṉ and had no work to perform. When Kōvalaṉ later returns after leaving Mādhavi, the poem explicitly says Kaṇṇaki showed him a smiling face:

> “She displayed a face with a graceful, auspicious smile.” (9:72)

This passage also helps resolve a debated question. In the marriage canto, Aṭiyārkkunallār glosses “the auspicious ornament arose throughout the broad city” as “the auspicious decoration appeared everywhere.” One hesitates to criticise Aṭiyārkkunallār—whose very name means “good to all devotees”—but the explanation is unclear. Some interpret it as the marriage-thread being carried in procession through the city. Whether Kōvalaṉ actually tied a *tāli* around Kaṇṇaki’s neck is disputed. Some use Aṭiyārkkunallār’s commentary to argue that he did not; others use another commentary to say he did. The latter is more fitting. The internal evidence is the line already quoted:

> “She rejoiced in no ornament except the auspicious one.” (4:50)

She wore no ornament other than the *tāli*, which by nature remained upon her.

A second internal proof occurs in the “Canto Establishing Domestic Virtue,” where Kōvalaṉ praises Kaṇṇaki:

> “O garlanded one of fragrant flowers,
> when the stainless auspicious ornament itself adorns you,
> why have those who praise your beauty
> obtained the right to place other ornaments upon you?” (2:62–64)

This too makes clear that the *tāli* had been tied.

In the “Canto in Which the City Celebrated the Indra Festival,” Iḷaṅkō’s imaginative description of public women moving through the streets during the festival is rich in flavour. One must read the poem itself; elaborating it here would make this chapter too long.

## The Canto of Seeing the Country

Farmers’ labour sustains both the households of those who beg and the victory of the kings who protect. Since agriculture becomes possible through the wealth of the Kāviri, the farmers are honoured as “children of the Kāviri”:

> “The children of the maiden Kāviri of spreading waters,
> who through cultivation sustain
> the households of petitioners
> and the victory of protectors.” (10:148–150)

The conception of the Kāviri protecting her agricultural children like a mother has been discussed more fully under another heading.

## The Canto of Seeing the Forest

### The Command of the Eyes

The Brahmin of Māṅkāṭu says he set out because his eyes commanded him to show them Śrīraṅgam and Tiruvēṅkaṭam:

> “The reclining form of him whose chest is occupied by the goddess,
> and the standing form of the red-eyed Tall One—
> my heart was troubled as my eyes demanded,
> ‘Show them to us!’
> So I came from Māṅkāṭu in the Kuṭaku hills.” (11:40, 51–53)

The personification of the eyes insisting that they be shown the sights is charming. A comparable imagination occurs in the novel *Teyvikat Tirumaṇam*:

> “But the young man could not answer his eyes. At first they reminded him that they wished to see her; he ignored them. They continued to plead that he give them an opportunity; he dismissed them. At last they became obstinate that they must see her—and they did.”

The Māṅkāṭu Brahmin spent money to show his eyes Śrīraṅgam and Tiruvēṅkaṭam. A related saying of Shakespeare comes to mind: “Make the hand poor and the eye rich.” To make the hand poor is to spend the money in it; to make the eye rich is to see many sights, take pleasure, and gain general knowledge. Through the Māṅkāṭu Brahmin, Iḷaṅkō indirectly urges us to travel and gain knowledge of the world.

### The Orb Worshipped by the World

The sun, which opens the lotus, appeared and awakened Ceḻiyaṉ’s Madurai:

> “The orb worshipped by the world, which opened the flower’s enclosure,
> awakened lofty Kūṭal from sleep.” (14:4–6)

The sun is called “the orb worshipped by the world” because the world worships it and many planets surround it. In the “Canto of the Water Consecration” it is called “the red-rayed god of morning” (27:137). Sun worship exists in many places. Iḷaṅkō himself says, “Let us praise the sun.” There are temples to the sun and a village named Sūriyanār Kōyil. Egypt too possessed sun temples.

Nakkīrar begins the *Tirumurukāṟṟuppaṭai* by praising:

> “The much-praised sun that moves on its rightward course,
> making the world rejoice.” (1–2)

People appeal to the sun as a witness for justice: “There goes Sūriya Nārāyaṇaṉ burning across the sky—he will question you.” The sun is also the mother from whom our earth was born.

## The Canto of the Legal Plea

### Mistress of Speech

Hanumān is called “master of speech” because of the verbal skill with which he briefly introduced himself to Rāma and Lakṣmaṇa. When the Pāṇḍya asks, “Who are you, vine-like woman?”, Kaṇṇaki becomes a mistress of speech.

She answers in compact form: “My city is Pukār, ruled by Cibi, who removed a dove’s suffering, and by Manu Nīti Cōḻa, whose heart was burned by a cow’s tears and who killed his precious son beneath his chariot wheel. I am the wife of Kōvalaṉ, son of the great merchant Mācāttuvāṉ of that city—Kōvalaṉ, who came to your Madurai to sell my anklet and was killed by you. My name is Kaṇṇaki.” May we not call the woman who narrates her whole history so concisely a mistress of speech?

In the “Canto of the Garland of Vows,” Kaṇṇaki recounts the histories of seven chaste women and declares that she was born in the Pukār that gave birth to them. The passage deserves to be read and enjoyed.

## The Canto of the Vision

### Yama’s Astonishment

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ alone fought five hundred Aryan kings. Yama is said to have watched the battle in astonishment, his terrible eyes wide open without blinking:

> “Cruel-eyed Death watched it with eyes opened wide.” (25:64)

This is a piece of literary delight.

When Pāṇḍya’s queen Kōpperuntēvi saw her husband die, she immediately gave up her own life, as though using it to search for his:

> “As though taking her own life to seek his life,
> the great queen too died at that very moment.” (25:85)

This too is literary personification.

### Political Strategy

Villavaṉ Kōtai advised Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ to send advance messengers to the northern kings announcing his expedition. But Aḻumpil Vēḷ said that no messengers were necessary: spies of all the world’s rulers would already be present in Vañji. If the announcement were made by public drum within the city, the spies would hear it and send the news home. This is a delightful piece of political strategy.

### Loss of the Shoulder-Companion

The following proclamation was drummed through Vañji under royal order:

“O kings of the north! Since the Cēra has already claimed the Himalaya, you owe him tribute. Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ now comes north to obtain a stone for Kaṇṇaki. Pay tribute when he passes. Otherwise, renounce your wives, your companions at the shoulder, and live as ascetics.”

> “Live in the renunciation that abandons the shoulder-companion.” (25:190)

The fuller passage says:

> “If you come before him bearing the tribute due,
> live after hearing the great report
> of the fierce war that felled the sea’s *kaṭampu*,
> and the vast report of the bow engraved upon the mountain cleft.
> If you will not listen,
> live in the renunciation that abandons the shoulder-companion.” (25:186–195)

The Cēras had crossed the sea to fell the *kaṭampu* tree and had reached the Himalaya to engrave their bow. The passage carries the refined implication: if you do not fear them, flee beyond the sea or beyond the Himalaya and live there.

“The companion at the shoulder” means the wife. Compare Valluvar’s title for the wife: “companion of life.”

## The Canto of Taking the Stone

### The Mountain’s Back Bends

Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ travelled through the Nīla Mountains with a vast army. Under the multitude of troops, “the mountain’s back bent” (26:82). This is literary delight. Only living beings normally possess backs that bend; the action is transferred to the inanimate mountain.

According to the *Taṇṭiyalaṅkāram*:

> “Joining an action belonging to its proper object
> to another object that resembles it
> is called *samādhi*.” (25)

In Tamil, this figure may be called “the ornament that joins a comparable action.”

### The Enemies’ Disguises

Except for Kanaka, Vijaya, and others taken captive in Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s northern war, many enemies escaped in disguise. Some wore matted hair; some dressed in ochre; some smeared sacred ash; some carried a Jain monk’s seat and peacock-feather whisk; some posed as singing bards; some bore many instruments upon their shoulders like actors. Casting away their swords, they ran to whatever places they wished, disguised as practitioners of arts they happened to know. In modern language, perhaps they “withdrew successfully”:

> “Some wore matted hair and garments; some were smeared with ash;
> some became great ascetics with seats and peacock feathers;
> some became singing bards, some actors
> with many instruments upon their shoulders.
> Abandoning their uplifted swords,
> in the skilled forms of chosen disguises,
> they scattered wherever they wished.” (26:225–230)

The passage is rich in humour. Similar scenes occur in Kampaṉ’s *Rāmāyaṇa* and Cayaṅkoṇṭār’s *Kaliṅkattupparaṇi*.

In the “Slaying of Akṣa Kumāra” in the *Sundara Kāṇḍa*, Kampaṉ humorously describes demons escaping Hanumān in many disguises. Some became fish and entered the sea; some took the form of cattle and grazed along the road; some became carrion birds and pecked at corpses; some became Brahmins; some took female form and parted their hair; some begged Hanumān for refuge; some stood motionless reciting Viṣṇu’s names like devotees. When wives and relatives embraced them weeping, some pushed them away and cried, “We are not your kin—we are gods who came to witness the war!” Others claimed to be human, became bees in groves, lay as though unconscious, broke their demonic fangs to resemble human teeth, or darkened their fiery hair:

> “Some became fish and reached the sea;
> some, as cattle, grazed along the roads;
> some took the form of flesh-eating birds;
> some became men of the four Vedas;
> some became young doe-eyed women
> and stood before him parting their hair;
> some cried, ‘Lord, we seek your refuge!’
> and some who stood there called upon Hari.” (40)

> “Whenever wives and close kin embraced them,
> some cried, ‘We are not your relatives;
> we are gods who came here!’ and went away;
> some shouted that they were human;
> some became bees in mandāra-filled groves;
> some feigned bewilderment;
> some broke their fangs,
> and some darkened hair that shone like fire.” (41)

In the “Singing of the War” in the *Kaliṅkattupparaṇi*, Kaliṅga warriors fleeing the Cōḻa army ran through thorny scrub. The thorns tore away their clothes and hair, so, now naked and shaven like Jain monks, they escaped by claiming not to be soldiers but ascetics (63). Some folded their bowstrings into sacred threads and said they were Brahmins travelling to bathe in the Gaṅgā (64). Some wrapped themselves in blood-soaked banners like ochre robes, shaved their heads, and claimed to be Buddhist monks (65). Others beat elephant bells as cymbals and said they were not Kaliṅgas but Telugu bards (66).

The enemies of the Cōḻa and Cēra were human, so the imagination remained within limits. Those who fought Hanumān were demons, allowing Kampaṉ’s imagination to exceed all bounds.

Literary comparison suggests one conclusion: Kampaṉ and Cayaṅkoṇṭār, both later than *Cilappatikāram*, may have poured Iḷaṅkō’s earlier conception into their own works. The scenes may of course have arisen naturally in each poet’s imagination, but it remains reasonable to regard the latter two as extracts from the flavour of *Cilappatikāram*.

## The Canto of the Water Consecration

### Eighteen as the Duration of War

The war of gods and demons lasted eighteen years; the war of Rāma and Rāvaṇa lasted eighteen months; the Bhārata war lasted eighteen days; Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ’s war defeating Kanaka and Vijaya lasted only eighteen *nāḻikai*:

> “Count the total of lives consumed as twice nine:
> years, months, days, and time-units—
> bring them together and reckon them in this watery world.” (27:8–10)

Nine doubled is eighteen. Thus: eighteen years for the divine war, eighteen months for the Rāmāyaṇa war, eighteen days for the Bhārata war, and eighteen *nāḻikai* for the Cēra’s victory. Two and a half *nāḻikai* make roughly one hour, so the Cēra won in about seven and a quarter hours.

### Ascending the Crown

After narrating Kaṇṇaki’s history to Ceṅkuṭṭuvaṉ, Māṭalaṉ concludes:

> “O king of the Kuṭavar...
> she ascended the jewelled crowns of the northern kings.” (27:64–65)

Kanaka and Vijaya carried upon their crowned heads the stone from which Kaṇṇaki’s image would be made. Māṭalaṉ expresses this elegantly as Kaṇṇaki herself ascending their crowns. Even before the statue existed, certainty of purpose transformed the stone into Kaṇṇaki.

If one thinks “stone,” Kaṇṇaki disappears; if one thinks “Kaṇṇaki,” the stone disappears. The temple images we see today are no longer perceived as stone but appear as gods.

Tirumūlar’s *Tirumantiram* expresses a comparable idea through a wooden elephant. If one thinks of it as an elephant, awareness of wood disappears; if one thinks of it as wood, the elephant disappears:

> “The great rutting elephant concealed the wood;
> within the wood the great rutting elephant disappeared.” (2290)

The proverb “When we see the dog, we do not see the stone; when we see the stone, we do not see the dog” conveys the same point. Some houses place a stone dog in the courtyard to deceive thieves.

### One Anklet and a Hand

When the gatekeeper announced Kaṇṇaki’s arrival to the Pāṇḍya, he said, “She has in her hand one anklet wrought in gold” (20:42). The emotional refinement lies in the reversals: an anklet meant for the foot is held in the hand, and of a matched pair she possesses only one.

If every line of *Cilappatikāram* is examined closely, countless such special details emerge.

### The Weapon of Tears

Some people use tears as a weapon to obtain what they need. Nature gives this weapon to children. Women in families sometimes shoot it as well; so do certain poor people and petitioners.

Beyond satisfying needs, tears may also act as a weapon that destroys the power of the cruel. Valluvar says under “Tyrannical Rule”:

> “The tears shed by those who suffer and cannot endure
> are the army that wears away the tyrant’s wealth.” (555)

Tears may even become a weapon that kills. Was it not Kaṇṇaki’s tears that killed Pāṇḍya Neṭuñceḻiyaṉ? The “Canto of Blessing” proclaims:

> “Long live the king of kings who gave his life to be slain
> by the tears of the woman who suffered through ancient karma.” (13:1–2)

The phrase does not say merely that he “lost” his life but that he “gave” it, a refinement that raises the Pāṇḍya into a king of kings.

The *Naṟuntokai* emphasises that tears can become a killing sword:

> “The tears shed in anguish by those
> who lost their case when Manu’s law was violated,
> even if the three gods guard the offender according to rule,
> become a sword that cuts through generation after generation.” (75)

Such tears bring great disgrace even upon descendants. Their power is immense.

> “The tears of the poor are equal to a sharpened sword.”

Many such delightful and significant details occur in the epic. Let us consider one more group.

## The Two Great Kuravais

*Cilappatikāram* contains two great *kuravai* dance-sections: the Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai* and the Hill-People’s *Kuravai*. The former may be called a miniature epic of Tirumāl and the latter a miniature epic of Murukaṉ. Both excel in the art of making a large subject shine through compression. One wonders where and from whom Iḷaṅkō learned to write such passages.

### 1. The Cowherd Women’s Kuravai

#### The Sevens

Because many evil omens appeared in their settlement, Mātari told Aiyai that performing a *kuravai* would prevent misfortune. Seven bulls had been raised in the pen, one for each of seven maidens. Mātari described which girl would belong to the man who subdued each bull:

1. The maiden wearing a honey-filled flower garland desires the man who leaps upon the black bull without fearing its rage.
2. The shoulders of the gold-bangled woman belong to the man who mounts and subdues the bull marked upon the forehead.
3. The maiden with jasmine in her hair belongs to the man who masters the powerful young bull.
4. The shoulders of the vine-like woman belong to the man who subdues the white bull covered in tiny speckles.
5. The breasts of the good vine-like maiden belong to the man who subdues the white bull with golden spots.
6. The woman with *koṉṟai* flowers in her hair belongs to the man who mounts the victorious young bull.
7. The maiden wearing a fresh *pūvai* flower belongs to the man who embraces and masters the pure white bull.

The verses read:

> “This honey-flower-garlanded woman desires
> the man who leaps upon the raging black bull without fear.”

> “The shoulders of this gold-bangled maiden belong
> to the man who subdues the forehead-marked bull.”

> “This jasmine-flower-haired woman belongs
> to the man who rides the mighty young bull.”

> “The shoulders of this vine-like woman belong
> to the man who subdues the finely speckled white bull.”

> “The soft breasts of this good vine-like woman belong
> to the man who subdues the gold-speckled white bull.”

> “This *koṉṟai*-flower-haired woman belongs
> to the man who rides the victorious young bull.”

> “This fresh-*pūvai*-flower woman belongs
> to the man who subdues the pure white bull.”

After saying this, Mātari gave the seven maidens new names in addition to their old ones: Kural, Tuttam, Kaikkiḷai, Uḻai, Iḷi, Viḷari, and Tāram:

> “Then, looking upon her daughter, she said:
> ‘These are the seven young maidens
> who raised the bulls of the pen for mounting.’
> Placing them in the ancient order,
> the senior cowherd woman created and assigned names:
> from the central Kural in order—Kural, Tuttam,
> Kaikkiḷai, Uḻai, Iḷi, Viḷari, and Tāram—
> names desired for the women of spreading flower-like hair.”

“To create and name” means inventing new names for them.

Some researchers identify these ancient Tamil seven notes with today’s *sa, ri, ga, ma, pa, dha, ni*—Ṣaḍja, Ṛṣabha, Gāndhāra, Madhyama, Pañcama, Dhaivata, and Niṣāda. Vipulānanda Aṭikaḷ, however, says in the *Yāḻ Nūl* that no exact one-to-one equation can be made. The matter requires research.

#### “Shall We Not Hear It, Friend?”

My desire urges me to give three passages from the Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai* without explanation so that they may simply be enjoyed:

> “If Māyavaṉ, who used a calf as a club to bring down the fruit,
> should come today among our cattle,
> shall we not hear from his lips
> the sweet flute of *koṉṟai*, friend?”

> “If Māyavaṉ, who used the serpent as rope to churn the sea,
> should come here among our cattle,
> shall we not hear from his lips
> the sweet flute of water-lily, friend?”

> “If Māyavaṉ, who broke the *kuruntu* trees
> on the slopes of the lovely pasture,
> should come within our boundary among the cattle,
> shall we not hear from his lips
> the sweet flute of jasmine, friend?”

These deserve to be sung and savoured repeatedly. The cowherd women singing them while performing the *kuravai* is like a rain of honey falling upon a pavilion of sugar.

Three lines under “Praise in the Third Person” are also noteworthy:

> “What kind of ears are ears that have not heard the warrior’s glory?
> What kind of ears are ears that have not heard Tirumāl’s glory?”

> “What kind of eyes are eyes that have not seen the dark one?
> What kind of eyes are the eyes of those
> who blink while seeing him?”

> “What kind of tongue is a tongue that does not praise
> the one who walked as messenger for the five Pāṇḍavas?
> What kind of tongue is a tongue that does not say, ‘Nārāyaṇa’?”

Out of overflowing love, the ear must hear Tirumāl’s greatness, the eye must see his form, and the tongue must praise him.

The poet does not stop with attacking eyes that fail to see Tirumāl. He attacks even eyes that blink while seeing him: they must gaze continuously without closing at all. This is a fine conceit.

Since the cowherds were Vaiṣṇavas who worshipped Tirumāl, these passages belong naturally in their *kuravai*. Iḷaṅkō’s greatness lies in becoming the appropriate actor in each setting.

For literary comparison, consider Tirunāvukkaracar’s commands to his bodily members:

> “O head, bow!”
> “O eyes, behold!”
> “O ears, listen!”
> “O nose, murmur!”
> “O mouth, praise!”
> “O heart, remember!”
> “O hands, join and worship!”
> “What use is the body otherwise?”
> “What use are the feet otherwise?”

In a Tēvāram hymn he assigns duties to nine organs among the senses of knowledge, organs of action, and inner faculty.

Iḷaṅkō names only the duties of ear, eye, and tongue because he is not speaking in his own authorial voice. He measures his words according to the situation of the cowherd women. Nāvukkaracar, addressing his own organs, could issue commands generously and without thrift to many of them.

The Cowherd Women’s *Kuravai* deserves to be sung repeatedly and enjoyed.

### 2. The Hill-People’s Kuravai

#### “People of the Little Clan! People of the Little Clan!”

The hill people saw Kaṇṇaki standing beneath a *vēṅkai* tree in their mountains and then ascending to the heavenly world. Since they believed they had no other deity like her, they called upon their clan to worship her. The passage is delightful and needs no explanation:

> “People of the little clan! People of the little clan!
> Take a deity for yourselves, people of the little clan!
> Beneath the good shade of the fragrant-branched *vēṅkai*,
> on the lower slope of the mountain where the waterfall
> descends in many colours,
> take a deity for yourselves, people of the little clan!
> Beat the *toṇṭakam* drum; beat the little drum;
> sound the curved horn; ring the fierce bell;
> sing the *kuṟiñci* mode; raise fragrant smoke;
> make flower-offerings; establish the protective gateway;
> praise her again and again; scatter mingled flowers,
> so that for the lady who lost one breast
> the great mountain may never sleep
> but pour forth prosperity.”

The instruments and forms of worship follow one upon another, delighting the reader.

#### “This Is Laughable!”

A heroine loves a man, but because marriage is delayed she wastes away. Her mother, ignorant of the true cause, assumes that a deity has seized and frightened the girl. Believing the deity to be Murukaṉ, she arranges a *vēlaṉ veṟiyāṭṭu*, a possession-ritual. She worships Murukaṉ and summons a possessed priest called the *vēlaṉ* to diagnose and cure the illness. In Tamil love poetics this is the conventional situation called “the *vēlaṉ*’s frenzy.”

The heroine says to her friend: “My mother thinks the illness caused by my lover was caused by Murukaṉ and has sent for the frenzied *vēlaṉ*. This is laughable, friend!”

She elaborates four times. The priest who comes to cure the illness caused by her mountain-lover is ignorant because he does not know its true source. If Murukaṉ, destroyer of Krauñca Mountain, possesses that priest, Murukaṉ too must be foolish. If the son of Śiva comes upon a priest trying to cure an illness caused by the fragrant mountain-man, the god is even more foolish than the priest. If Murukaṉ of the *kaṭampu* garland comes to cure the burning illness given by the hero’s chest, he is the most thoroughly foolish of all.

The verses say:

> “Good straight-bangled friend, this is laughable!
> My mother does not know that the cool-hill man
> where pepper grows caused my illness;
> she calls it the work of the flowering *kaṭampu* lord,
> desires the frenzy, and says, ‘Let the *vēlaṉ* come!’”

> “Fine-bangled friend, this is laughable!
> The *vēlaṉ* who comes to cure the illness of the great mountain-man
> is a fool; and if he comes,
> the one who destroyed Krauñca Mountain is a fool beyond him.”

> “Good close-bangled friend, this is laughable!
> The *vēlaṉ* who comes to cure the illness caused
> by the fragrant mountain-man is a fool;
> and if the son of the lord beneath the banyan comes upon him,
> that god is more foolish still.”

> “Good straight-ornamented friend, this is laughable!
> The *vēlaṉ* who comes to cure the burning illness
> given by the mountain-man’s chest is a fool;
> and if our dark-*kaṭampu*-garlanded god comes,
> he is more foolish than the *vēlaṉ* himself.”

Iḷaṅkō appears to have mastered Tamil love grammar completely.

The heroine then says they will turn the ritual to their own purpose:

1. If Murukaṉ comes upon his blue peacock with Vaḷḷi, they will ask him to grant marriage with her mountain-lover.
2. If the son of the mountain goddess comes, they will worship him and Vaḷḷi, the maiden of their hill community, and beg him to prevent a marriage with a stranger and grant union with the beloved.
3. They will ask that the marriage with the lover be publicly known.
4. They will worship Murukaṉ and Vaḷḷi, “our own clan’s daughter,” and ask the god to reject the erroneous marriage and grant the right one.

The verses say:

> “If, in the fierce ritual-ground where the *vēlaṉ* comes in frenzy,
> the son of the lord beneath the banyan arrives
> upon the blue bird with the straight-ornamented woman,
> we shall ask for the marriage-ornament
> with the lord of the great mountain.”

> “O moon-browed maiden, we worshipped the flower-like sacred feet
> of the mountain people’s daughter
> and the son of the lord of good Mount Kailāsa:
> ‘End the alien marriage; grant marriage with him.’”

> “O moon-browed maiden, we worshipped the paired feet
> of the exalted god, son of the mountain goddess,
> and the daughter of the hill people dwelling in our ancestral mountain:
> ‘May she obtain marriage with him for all to know.’”

> “She is a daughter of the hill clan—our own clan’s daughter.
> O six-faced one, we worship your paired feet with hers:
> ‘Let the man who touches your sacred feet at the ford
> receive the good marriage; release us from the mistaken one.’”

The refined appeal is that Murukaṉ’s wife Vaḷḷi belongs to their own hunter community; this kinship is used to draw and persuade the god. Many such flavours may be enjoyed in the Hill-People’s *Kuravai*.

In both great *kuravais*, a common dramatic structure appears: the second line of a stanza is repeated as the third. One example from each follows.

**Cowherd Women’s Kuravai**

> “He entered the three waters and felled the deathless *kaṭampu*;
> the king of kings, the Cēra, ruler of prosperous Vañji;
> the king of kings, the Cēra, ruler of prosperous Vañji—
> they say he lifted his sugarcane-like shoulders and churned the sea.”

**Hill-People’s Kuravai**

> “We see no fault with which to reproach him;
> the fresh flood that came touching the stone of his mountain—
> the fresh flood that came touching the stone of his mountain—
> if other women bathe in it, friend, our heart will suffer.”

I remember with pleasure reading in *The Art of Play Writing* that immediate repetition of the same word or phrase is a device that adds dramatic flavour.

Thus *Cilappatikāram* contains many notable and delightful details.


---

# 30. Was Kaṇṇaki the Pāṇḍya King’s Daughter?

## The Canto of Blessing

The women of Vañji sing of Kaṇṇaki as the Pāṇḍya king’s daughter:

> “Women of Vañji! Women of Vañji’s middle streets!
> Soft-footed companions of the king with the unfailing spear,
> come—all of you, come!
> Let us sing of the woman who destroyed Kūṭal with her breast
> and defeated the king with her red anklet;
> come, let us sing of the southern king’s daughter!
> Our king’s tongue of justice proclaimed,
> ‘Pāṇḍyas do not live when the just sceptre bends,’
> and she, the green-bangled maiden, reached this land;
> come, let us sing of the Pāṇḍya’s daughter!” (29:11)

The meaning is that all the women of Vañji are summoned to sing of Kaṇṇaki: she who burned Madurai with her breast, she who was the Pāṇḍya’s daughter, and she who reached the Cēra land after their king praised the Pāṇḍyas as rulers who would not remain alive when the sceptre departed from justice.

The women sing further: “Because the Cēra built Kaṇṇaki a temple, restored her to new life, and made her a deity through a new divine birth, we women of Vañji called her the Cēra king’s daughter. But Kaṇṇaki, now a goddess, declared in an audible voice, ‘I am the Pāṇḍya’s daughter.’ Let us praise our Cēra! Let the divine daughter Kaṇṇaki praise the Pāṇḍya!”

> “We said, ‘She is the daughter of our celestial Cēra king’;
> but the vine-like woman said,
> ‘I am the daughter begotten by the lord of the Vaigai.’
> Let us praise the celestial Cēra;
> let the divine daughter praise the king of the Vaigai.” (29:12)

Kaṇṇaki was the daughter of Mānāykaṉ, a merchant of Pukār. We must therefore untangle the difficulty created by calling her the Pāṇḍya’s daughter. What new complication is this? We need the help of an older folk story.

## Was Kaṇṇaki Kālī?

An old story says that Kālī came as Kaṇṇaki. I had thought about this even in my youth. On 11 May 1949, under the auspices of the Tiruvalluvar Association of Tiruchirappalli, I delivered a lecture lasting two and three-quarter hours at a choultry in the fruit-market street on the subject “What Is Chastity?” in relation to *Cilappatikāram*. On the fourteenth of the same month, at the Murukaṉ temple in Vaṇṭippāḷaiyam, Cuddalore, I spoke for two and a quarter hours on “Was Kaṇṇaki Kālī?” Many of the ideas expressed in one town naturally appeared again in the other.

My conclusion was that Kaṇṇaki was not Kālī but an ordinary human woman like others; Kālī was not born in her form. The elder who presided over the meeting rejected my conclusion in his closing speech and insisted that Kālī herself came as Kaṇṇaki. I would not yield and rose again to assert my view. He would not yield either; nor did I. Somehow the meeting ended. There had been no such opposition in Tiruchirappalli.

A performance detail about Kōvalaṉ that I saw or heard in youth ran as follows: Mādhavi placed a garland around his neck, and he could not remove it. I remember one line of the associated song:

> “The garland that fell upon my neck cannot be removed—
> maiden, whose trick is this?”

Children, imitating adults, would playfully mispronounce and sing it; I too have sung it. We also sang the line attributed to Kaṇṇaki in her plea before the Pāṇḍya:

> “O blameworthy Pāṇḍya, repay blame for blame!”

Several related lines were discussed earlier under “Omens.”

## Summary of the Old Story

The complete folk tale may be compressed still further. A Pāṇḍya king without children prayed intensely to Kālī to grant a child in his queen’s womb. Kālī herself was born as the baby, with a garland upon her chest and anklets upon her feet.

The king summoned astrologers to ask the child’s future. They warned that she would bring destruction upon him and Madurai and advised him to remove her. The king placed the infant in a box and had it set afloat far away in the Kāviri. A childless merchant of Pukār found the box on the riverbank, rejoiced at the baby within, named her Kaṇṇaki, and raised her.

When she came of age, her foster-father married her to Kōvalaṉ of the same city. A courtesan named Mādhavi of Tirukkaṭaiyūr placed a garland upon Kōvalaṉ’s neck, and he could not remove it. He remained with her. When the time came that Mādhavi rejected him, he returned to Kaṇṇaki. They travelled to Madurai, where Kōvalaṉ was killed. Kaṇṇaki, who was Kālī, destroyed the Pāṇḍya and Madurai.

This is the folk story in extreme abbreviation. What we should take from it is only that it supplies evidence for the claim that Kaṇṇaki was the Pāṇḍya’s daughter. We need not accept that she was Kālī; that is not true.

What evidence enabled Iḷaṅkō to make both the women of Vañji and Kaṇṇaki herself call her the Pāṇḍya’s daughter? If he drew upon this folk story, why does the wedding canto call her the daughter of Mānāykaṉ of Pukār? If the folk tale preserves an actual event, we can find peace only by assuming that Iḷaṅkō called her simply “daughter” without specifying “foster-daughter.” Since he may have known that this story was performed in the country before he wrote the epic, he may have inserted the phrase “Pāṇḍya’s daughter” at a suitable point. Kaṇṇaki may also have called herself his daughter out of compassion because he died on account of her anklet.

Long stories that later appear in literature were probably often performed in the country before being written down. Such drama might have been *vēttiyal*, court theatre performed before kings and other elites, or *potuviyal*, public street theatre performed before ordinary people.

For comparison, consider Homer’s Greek epic the *Iliad*. One view holds that many parts of it were sung by ordinary people in villages of Yugoslavia. Could *Cilappatikāram* have taken shape in a comparable way?

## The One-Breasted Woman

A poem in the *Naṟṟiṇai*, concerning a hero, heroine, and other woman, uses as a simile a woman who cut off one breast:

> “Tirumāvuṇṇi, who cut off one breast
> because the distress caused by an alien man tormented her.” (216:8–9)

Some infer that this Tirumāvuṇṇi, who acted after cutting off a breast because of grief caused by a man, may have been Kaṇṇaki.

The goddess Bhagavati of Koṭuṅkōḷūr is even today called “the one-breasted woman.”

It is therefore natural for epics to arise upon the foundation of stories circulating among the people.

## Pēkaṉ and Kaṇṇaki

A poem by Kapilar in the *Puṟanāṉūṟu* says that the generous Pēkaṉ, famed for covering a peacock with a cloak, had abandoned his wife. She stood at the palace gate and wept until her breasts were wet:

> “O fierce-horsed Pēkaṉ of open-handed generosity...
> Who is this pitiable woman?...
> Unable to restrain the tears she poured forth,
> sobbing until her breast was wet,
> she wept greatly, like a flute lamenting.” (143)

The poem seems to plead: “Who is she? Show her grace.” Her name too is said to have been Kaṇṇaki. Some claim that the story of Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki arose from this account. That is not fitting. That story is one thing; this is another.


---

# 31. Solutions to Several Problems

When a history is written as an epic—especially an ancient epic—we cannot expect events to be recorded year by year, month by month, day by day, and hour by hour as in formal history. An epic may mention only the rising and setting of sun and moon; broad seasons such as summer, rain, and cold; an occasional month; and shorter periods such as dawn, morning, noon, evening, night, and midnight. We must not treat an epic like a diary. With this principle in mind, let us approach *Cilappatikāram* and attempt to resolve several problems.

## 1. The Alleged Chronological Contradiction

Some scholars say that *Cilappatikāram* contains a chronological contradiction. The issue is as follows.

Kauṇti Aṭikaḷ, Kōvalaṉ, and Kaṇṇaki came to stay in the outskirts of Madurai. One morning Kōvalaṉ toured the city and returned to the outskirts, perhaps a little before noon. Kauṇti then entrusted Kaṇṇaki and Kōvalaṉ to the cowherd woman Mātari, who happened to pass that way.

Mātari led the couple to her dwelling, bathed and auspiciously adorned Kaṇṇaki, and—because Kōvalaṉ was a Jain lay-observer who ate before darkness—ordered that the materials required for cooking be supplied quickly. Kaṇṇaki prepared the meal, fed Kōvalaṉ, and gave him betel and areca nut.

After eating, Kōvalaṉ lamented his earlier wrongs to Kaṇṇaki, embraced her, concealed his tears, took one anklet, and went to Madurai’s market street to sell it. There he was falsely accused and killed. Some suppose that the death occurred that evening or around seven in the early night. The “Canto of the Place of Killing,” however, gives no explicit hour. Perhaps because he was a Jain layman who ate before darkness and went immediately to the market, some have inferred an evening death.

In the later “Canto of Circling the City,” Iḷaṅkō clearly marks an evening. The sun withdrew its rays and disappeared behind the great mountain; bewildering dusk arrived. At that evening hour, after others showed her the place, Kaṇṇaki saw Kōvalaṉ’s lifeless body. The poem says that in the morning she had taken the flower garland from his hair and worn it in her own; in the evening she embraced his bloodstained corpse and lamented:

> “The red-rayed sun contracted its beams
> and went to hide beyond the great mountain,
> filling the prosperous wide world with darkness.
> As the flower-vine woman cried aloud
> in the dim bewildering evening,
> the city raised a resounding roar.
>
> In the morning she had taken the garland
> from her lord’s dark, bee-thronged hair,
> placed it upon her flowing tresses, and embraced him;
> in the evening she saw him,
> blood from his wounds streaming over his body—
> the unbearable grief of seeing him
> who would never again see her.” (19:31–38)

Cīrāmulu Reṭṭiyār and Mu. Ku. Cakannātarācā argue that Iḷaṅkō makes Kōvalaṉ die in the evening in the “Canto of the Place of Killing” but then says in the “Canto of Circling the City” that Kaṇṇaki embraced him in the morning and saw his corpse that evening. They therefore identify a chronological error in the epic.

### Is There an Error in the Epic?

R. Vīrapattiraṉ, formerly professor of Tamil at the University College of Kerala University, discussed the matter in his book *The Faultless Cilampu*, written against Cakannātarācā’s *A Small Error in Cilampu*.

Vīrapattiraṉ states that Dr Va. Cu. Māṇikkam, Te. Po. Mīṉāṭcicuntaraṉ, and Ma. Po. Civajñāṉam accepted that a chronological contradiction existed. He also rejects Mo. Turai Araṅkaṉār’s claim that Kōvalaṉ’s killing and the *kuravai* dance occurred on the same day.

My own view is that Iḷaṅkō’s statement—that the woman who embraced her husband in the morning saw his corpse that very evening—is perfectly appropriate. It is surprising that Va. Cu. Mā., Te. Po. Mī., and Ma. Po. Ci. accepted the alleged contradiction and then tried to explain it away.

### An Invented Timetable

Vīrapattiraṉ imagines the following schedule:

- Kōvalaṉ returned from touring Madurai at noon on the first day—about fifteen *nāḻikai*, roughly twelve o’clock.
- Mātari took the couple under protection at twenty-two and a half *nāḻikai*, about three in the afternoon.
- Kōvalaṉ ate and left to sell the anklet at twenty-seven and a half *nāḻikai*, around five.
- He was killed at roughly thirty-two and a half *nāḻikai*, about seven in the evening.
- The *kuravai* was performed the next morning between about nine and eleven.
- Mātari bathed in the Vaigai, heard of Kōvalaṉ’s death on the way, and told Kaṇṇaki at noon of the second day.
- Kaṇṇaki saw the body at thirty *nāḻikai*, around six that evening.

### “The Hour of the Departing Sun’s Fading Ray”

Vīrapattiraṉ places Mātari’s taking the couple home at three in the afternoon. But Iḷaṅkō says that she led them away when the sun was setting:

> “With the mature maiden of young white teeth,
> at the hour when the departing sun’s ray was fading,
> as the cow seeking its calf raised its full cry...
> she passed through the entrance and entered her house.”
> (*Canto of Refuge*, 202–204, 218)

“The departing sun,” “the fading ray,” and “the cow seeking its calf” all indicate evening at sunset. That cannot be three in the afternoon. Vīrapattiraṉ’s timetable is clearly only an approximate construction.

He therefore makes Kōvalaṉ die on the first evening and Kaṇṇaki see the corpse on the second evening. The reasons he gives, with my objections, follow.

### Arguments and Replies

**Argument:** When Kōvalaṉ left to sell the anklet, a bull crossed his path as an evil omen. Cattle return from the herd only in the evening; therefore he departed on the first evening.

**Reply:** Might the cattle not have been leaving for pasture in the morning? Moreover, Mātari’s house is called “the house of the cowherds of many cattle”; at least one animal might have been moving between house and street. Near my own house in Puducherry, other people’s cattle can be seen from morning until evening.

**Argument:** The goldsmith came surrounded by many members of his profession. They may all have finished palace work and been returning home in the evening.

**Reply:** Why could they not have been setting out together for work in the morning?

**Argument:** Only at evening would they finish work and return as a group; in the morning they might go individually.

**Reply:** Must they all return as one group even in the evening? Would they not leave work separately for their respective homes? Did they all live in one place?

**Argument:** It is easier to kill someone after dark without others knowing.

**Reply:** Is royal punishment administered only at night? Could it not occur in daylight?

**Argument:** Kōvalaṉ needed money immediately and could tolerate no delay; therefore he left as soon as he ate on the first evening.

**Reply:** Could he not have waited one night and sold the anklet next morning? In a strange city, selling jewellery in daylight would be safer than selling it at night.

**Argument:** Because the killing occurred on the first day, evil omens appeared in the cowherd settlement the next morning.

**Reply:** Most traditions describe evil omens as appearing before the misfortune, not after it.

Vīrapattiraṉ’s claim that Kōvalaṉ went out and was killed on the first evening is therefore not convincing.

Both the anonymous early commentator and Aṭiyārkkunallār say that the killing occurred the next day. Aṭiyārkkunallār divides the “Canto of the Place of Killing” into two days: up to line 21, “Give them without delay,” belongs to the first day; from line 22 onward belongs to the next. After commenting on line 21, he writes: “Now the poet tells what occurred on the following day.”

Aṭiyārkkunallār says that after Mātari instructed the cowherd women to give the materials for cooking, she went to the palace to measure and deliver ghee, and the women did not provide the ingredients immediately but only the next day. Vīrapattiraṉ rightly asks: if so, did Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki fast through the entire first night? Would Mātari have left them like that? His objection here is correct.

The provisions must have been given on the first afternoon, and the couple ate that evening. But Vīrapattiraṉ, who says the killing occurred that first evening and the body was seen the next evening, interprets the opening lines of the quoted stanza to suit his theory. Since *kālai* can mean morning, time, or day, he takes it as “daytime” and says Kōvalaṉ died during the first day. Yet if *kālai* can mean daytime, why must it refer only to the first day and not the second? His argument does not hold.

### Sexual Union in Daylight?

Vīrapattiraṉ advances an even more comic suggestion. He says that after eating on the first evening and before leaving at five to sell the anklet, Kōvalaṉ had sexual intercourse with Kaṇṇaki. In the daze of union, he supposes, the garland upon Kōvalaṉ’s head slipped into Kaṇṇaki’s hair. Their union would therefore have occurred between four and five in the afternoon.

What an oddity! Must they unite in daylight? Might they not have intended to wait until Kōvalaṉ returned from the market and then unite at night? Would a flood carry away the water in the well? There is a proverb: “Like a starved cow leaping at the feeding-trough.” Was Kōvalaṉ such a starved cow? He had immersed himself in the ocean of desire and emerged exhausted.

Were Kōvalaṉ and Kaṇṇaki staying in a modern lodge? Today a man may bring an unrelated woman by day, rent a room in a lodge, and complete the affair for which they came. Their situation was nothing of the kind. On the road, a divine woman had come in the form of Vayantamālai and offered Kōvalaṉ an opportunity to be seduced, yet he rejected it. Must such a man unite by day? When both he and Kaṇṇaki were lying in grief, was daylight intercourse their urgent need? It is like a man asking another for a light for his cigarette while that other’s beard is burning. Should one spread a bed for them in daylight amid their misery? Night was available.

### The Times of the Meal and the Killing

Vīrapattiraṉ assigns Kōvalaṉ’s death to seven in the early night of the first day. According to Iḷaṅkō, however, seven would have been the time when he ate.

Mātari took the couple home at “the hour when the departing sun’s ray was fading.” Let us call that six in the evening. The provisions were quickly supplied; Kaṇṇaki cooked and fed Kōvalaṉ. That meal could reasonably have occurred around seven. How can the hour of eating be called the hour of death?

### “Red Eyes”

In the Indra-festival canto, Iḷaṅkō says “Kaṇṇaki’s dark eyes” (line 237); in the “Garland of Sorrow” he says that she “wept until her red eyes reddened” (line 33). Vīrapattiraṉ argues that the eyes were first red because of sexual intercourse and became still redder after Kōvalaṉ’s loss.

If intercourse at about 4:30 on the first afternoon made her eyes red, did that redness remain until the next evening? I do not know anyone in ordinary life whose eyes remain red for twenty-four hours after one act of union. In the “Canto of Seeing the Forest,” Iḷaṅkō describes a woman’s eyes as “rainy eyes with rich corners through which red lines flow” (line 184). Why should Kaṇṇaki’s “red eyes” not similarly mean eyes streaked with red?

Vīrapattiraṉ’s account therefore fails. What is the proper solution to “morning” and “evening”? A solution exists.

### The Proper Solution

The garland did not aim itself and fall into Kaṇṇaki’s hair in the daze of sexual union. Aṭiyārkkunallār and Vēṅkaṭacāmi Nāṭṭār both explain that Kaṇṇaki received it from Kōvalaṉ and wore it. Whether Kōvalaṉ actually gave her the garland, or whether one was even present that morning, is secondary. Could this not be an epic device used by Iḷaṅkō to create tragic feeling—to say that Kaṇṇaki sent her husband out in an auspicious morning setting and saw him as a corpse that evening?

If she embraced her husband in the morning and saw his body in the evening, the killing could have occurred around noon, between those times. Why then invent a death on the previous evening and a viewing on the following evening? Would the corpse have been left there for twenty-four hours? This was an official royal execution; the state would not have allowed the body to remain so long. The auspicious morning is a form of epic foreshadowing.

If he had meant “he died yesterday evening and she saw him this evening,” Iḷaṅkō should have written “yesterday” and “today” rather than “morning” and “evening.” Valluvar uses the explicit terms in:

> “This world possesses the greatness of saying:
> ‘One who existed yesterday does not exist today.’” (336)

### A List of Faults

After eating on the first evening, Kōvalaṉ lists his former failings to Kaṇṇaki. Among them he says:

> “I failed to obey the commands of my two aged elders.” (16:67)

That means that in Pukār his parents repeatedly rebuked and advised him, but he ignored them and did not reform. Iḷaṅkō had not previously narrated those parental admonitions. From this later line, we infer for ourselves that they occurred. In the same way, from the statement that one who left in the morning became a corpse by evening, we should infer that he was not killed on the first evening but around noon the next day.

### Epic Flavour

Why does Iḷaṅkō write continuously that, after the first evening’s meal and exchange of grief, Kōvalaṉ went to sell the anklet and was killed? He wishes the reader to feel the tragedy that the man who said, “I shall take one of your little-foot anklets, exchange it, and return,” never returned.

Had he written, “They ate in the evening, slept at night, and on the following morning Kōvalaṉ left to sell the anklet,” the epic flavour would have weakened and the urgency of pathos been blunted. Hence his narrative continuity.

### A Dramatic Device

This method must also be remembered as a dramatic device. Cinema often inserts into the middle of a scene something that happened earlier—either as a flashback or as an account told to others. A dream may similarly show in advance what will happen later.

The same principle applies to the dramatic epic *Cilappatikāram*. The fact revealed in the nineteenth canto—the “Canto of Circling the City”—that the killing occurred between morning and evening is anticipated in the sixteenth canto, the “Canto of the Place of Killing.” Like a dream that shows the future early, the event of the following day is narrated continuously after the events of the first evening. The structure should therefore be accepted as dramatic epic, not treated as a dated historical diary.

### A View to Be Rejected

Some give yet another account: the Pāṇḍya went to end the queen’s estrangement in the evening or early night; the goldsmith inflamed him and caused Kōvalaṉ to be killed; the king was then seated with the queen upon a bed when Kaṇṇaki came and pleaded her case; king and queen immediately fainted and died one after the other.

They cite the line:

> “He sat upon the *amaḷi* borne by lions.” (20:22)

They say *amaḷi* means a bed. But they ignore “borne by lions.” A seat supported by lions is the royal throne—a lion-seat—not a bed. This is therefore a view to be rejected, not accepted.

What should be learned from the discussion is this: Cīrāmulu Reṭṭiyār was wrong to identify a chronological error in *Cilappatikāram*; Cakannātarācā’s book *A Small Error in Cilampu* was unnecessary; and although Vīrapattiraṉ rightly argued that no chronological contradiction existed, the method he used to establish his conclusion was not appropriate.

Nevertheless, his effort through *The Faultless Cilampu* to uphold the epic’s excellence and Iḷaṅkō’s fame deserves praise. My thanks and respects to him.

## 2. Why Did Kōvalaṉ Go to Madurai?

The next problem concerns the reason for Kōvalaṉ’s journey to Madurai. Some say he feared that Mādhavi would somehow enchant him and draw him back; if he remained in Pukār, he might return to her, so he decided to leave immediately for Madurai. This is unconvincing.

Some modern film actresses stop acting after marriage. Some continue acting while living with their husbands. Some change husbands after several years of marriage. To which of these three types did Mādhavi most resemble?

For a time she behaved like the second. Even while living domestically with Kōvalaṉ, she performed song and dance at Pukār’s Indra festival and delighted everyone. Kōvalaṉ disliked this. The wound in his mind was then intensified, like oil poured upon fire, by her *Kāṉal Vari* song, which appeared to imply that she held another man in her heart. The truth is that Kōvalaṉ, deeply hurt, left Mādhavi and went to Kaṇṇaki.

### Water at the Bank and the Mirage

I remember a song from a film seen in youth, in which P. U. Chinnappa played Kōvalaṉ. As Kōvalaṉ walks away from Mādhavi toward Kaṇṇaki, he sings:

> “Like a deer that seeks a mirage
> while water lies beside the bank.”

The water beside the bank is Kaṇṇaki; the mirage is Mādhavi. The Kōvalaṉ of *Cilappatikāram* was in the same condition.

After he left, Mādhavi sent a letter through Vayantamālai asking him to return. He refused. On the road to Madurai, a divine woman took Vayantamālai’s form and tried to seduce him; he was not deceived. Mādhavi sent another letter through Kaucikaṉ; he still did not return. He therefore did not flee for Madurai in fear that Mādhavi would enchant him again. He went back to Kaṇṇaki with a firm mind.

Kaṇṇaki said, “There are anklets; take them.” From this we understand that she had already given away her other ornaments. Yet Kōvalaṉ did not take the anklet and return to Mādhavi as before. Having lost the wealth of the household, he was ashamed to face his parents and feared the ridicule of the townspeople. He therefore resolved to go to Madurai, earn wealth, and return. Without informing anyone, he took Kaṇṇaki and departed in darkness.

That is the true reason for his journey. Iḷaṅkō, like many others, developed the habit of blaming everything upon karma. Kōvalaṉ’s killing in Madurai was an unexpected and accidental event.

## 3. Were Gaṅgā and Kanni Vayantamālai?

The third problem concerns Kōvalaṉ and Vayantamālai.

### A Whole Book

The scholar Pāvalar Maṇi Ā. Paḻani wrote a book titled *Kāṉal Vari or a Line of Tears?* Its entire argument is that Kōvalaṉ had a sexual relationship with Vayantamālai, Mādhavi’s companion. One wonders whether a whole book was needed to say this.

The first two songs of the *Kāṉal Vari* have been subjected to extraordinary strain:

> “O Kāviri, live long! Though the king beneath the white umbrella,
> whose garland is the moon, wields his red sceptre
> and unites with Gaṅgā, do not grieve.
> O fish-eyed one, I know that your not grieving
> though he unites with Gaṅgā
> is the great chastity of women. Live long, Kāviri!”

> “O Kāviri, live long! Though the king beneath the enduring white umbrella,
> wielding the unbending red sceptre,
> unites with Kanni, do not grieve.
> O fish-eyed one, I know that your not grieving
> though he unites with Kanni
> is the great chastity of enduring women. Live long, Kāviri!”

The songs say that even if the Cōḻa king joins Gaṅgā in the north and Kanni—the Kumari river—in the south, Kāviri will neither grieve nor become estranged.

The other songs in the canto concern themes of Tamil love poetics and were discussed under “The Place of the Kāṉal Vari in the Epic.”

Te. Po. Mīṉāṭcicuntaraṉ says that Kōvalaṉ sang the first two songs with Kaṇṇaki in his mind: even if he joined Mādhavi or other women, Kaṇṇaki would not become estranged. It is also said that he wished to teach Mādhavi Kaṇṇaki’s lofty character.

But if Kaṇṇaki is metaphorically Kāviri and the songs say that she will not grieve even if Kōvalaṉ joins other women, they should be sung before Kaṇṇaki. What use is singing them before Mādhavi? Will the message ever reach Kaṇṇaki? Must her excellence be explained to Mādhavi by declaring, “I may unite with whomever I please”?

### A New Discovery

Ā. Paḻani offers a new interpretation of Kāviri, Gaṅgā, and Kanni. He says Kōvalaṉ also had sexual relations with Vayantamālai, which Mādhavi disliked. The meaning of Kōvalaṉ’s song, he argues, was: “Just as Kāviri does not become estranged when the Cōḻa joins Gaṅgā and Kanni, you, Mādhavi, should not become estranged when I unite with Vayantamālai.”

Other scholars have made related claims. Ku. Tirumēṉi writes in *Kōvalaṉ* that he lived without limits and had relations with people like Vayantamālai; this, he says, is why the forest deity assumed Vayantamālai’s form and offered a plausible reason for approaching him. S. Rāmakiruṭṭiṇaṉ writes in *Iḷaṅkō’s Characterisation* that Iḷaṅkō makes us infer an erotic relationship between Kōvalaṉ and Vayantamālai.

Drawing upon these views, Ā. Paḻani identifies Vayantamālai with both Gaṅgā and Kanni. His reasons, and my objections, follow.

### Arguments and Replies

**Reason 1:** Kōvalaṉ was intensely lustful, went astray in youth, spent time among courtesans, did not spare even Mādhavi’s attendants, and lingered in the courtesans’ streets while touring Madurai. The epic says:

> “Kōvalaṉ, accustomed to wandering
> with flute-mouthed bards and city courtesans.” (5:200–201)

and:

> “I joined the deceitful woman of corrupt conduct,
> lost the mountain of great wealth inherited from my lineage,
> and have brought upon myself the shame of poverty.” (9:69–71)

A man with such habits could have had relations with Vayantamālai.

**Reply:** Even if Kōvalaṉ indulged in love’s games, how can one say that he sang specifically with Vayantamālai in mind? Might he not have thought of other women, or sung generally without referring to anyone? Does imagining that he spent a long time in the courtesans’ streets establish his appetite? His “horoscope” was already known before that.

**Reason 2:** On the road to Madurai, a forest deity desiring Kōvalaṉ came in Vayantamālai’s form. She chose that form because Kōvalaṉ and Vayantamālai had an intimate relationship and she believed he would not reject her.

**Reply:** When a stranger seeks access to a distinguished person, it is common to introduce oneself by naming a relative, friend, or associate of that person and explaining the connection; sometimes one brings the known intermediary along. The divine woman’s attempt to approach Kōvalaṉ in the form of someone familiar is no more than this ordinary practice.

Even if Kōvalaṉ knew many women, the epic’s audience knows Vayantamālai by name. Iḷaṅkō may have selected her form because it gave the narrative recognisable flavour. The divine woman would know that if she came as Mādhavi—who had renounced him—Kōvalaṉ would reject her, and if she came as a completely unknown woman he might not dare unite with her.

**Reason 3:** The verse announcing the divine woman says:

> “The deity dwelling in the forest came through desire,
> thinking, ‘He will grant himself through the love he has cherished,’
> and appeared in the form of Vayantamālai.” (11:171–173)

The word *nayanta*, “cherished,” is a past participle. It proves, according to the argument, that Kōvalaṉ had previously desired and loved Vayantamālai; therefore she is the woman indicated in the *Kāṉal Vari*.

**Reply:** The early commentator glosses the phrase generally as “because he possesses cherished love.” Aṭiyārkkunallār says “through the love cherished for Mādhavi,” and Vēṅkaṭacāmi Nāṭṭār gives the same sense. None of these three glosses is necessary here.

Why not understand that the divine woman thought: “Kōvalaṉ will receive me lovingly—and indeed with deeply cherished love”? *Nayanta* can intensify and confirm the quality of the love she expects. It need not mean that he will accept her because he previously loved Vayantamālai.

The scene may simply be one among the epic’s many mythological inventions. If one accepts other divine episodes, why reject this one? It therefore does not follow that Kōvalaṉ sang with Vayantamālai in his heart.

### The Proper Solution

Vayantamālai is Mādhavi’s companion. In *Maṇimēkalai*, Cāttaṉār says that Mādhavi’s mother Cittirāpati sent Vayantamālai to Mādhavi. She also appears to have served as an attendant. Mādhavi’s sending a letter to Kōvalaṉ through her is well known.

If Mādhavi disliked a relationship between Kōvalaṉ and Vayantamālai, she could simply have removed or dismissed Vayantamālai. There was no need to allow matters to reach the point where Kōvalaṉ could sing, “As Kāviri does not become estranged when the Cōḻa joins Gaṅgā and Kanni, you, Mādhavi, must not become estranged when I unite with Vayantamālai.” Driving Vayantamālai away was not a task requiring an axe; it was as easy as pinching something off with a fingernail. The *Kāṉal Vari* therefore contains no place at all for Vayantamālai.

### A Habit of Men

Whom, then, did Kōvalaṉ hold in mind while singing? No one. He sang merely to tease Mādhavi, play a mischievous joke, and lightly threaten her. Men have such a habit. Consider one example.

A friend told his wife: “When I ride my scooter and see beautiful women, my scooter slows down.” He said it only to provoke and tease her playfully. Did his wife reply in equal fashion, “When I walk in the street and see handsome men, my legs slow down”? No, not at all. Many such examples could be given. Women of respectable households have not, until now, generally spoken like that—what the future holds, who knows?

Mādhavi, however, answered in rivalry by singing as though she too held another man in her heart, thereby displaying the inherited temperament of her courtesan community.

Her song was not true either. Her pure mind is evident from her renunciation after Kōvalaṉ’s death. We should therefore rest with the conclusion that the *Kāṉal Vari* heightened the epic’s flavour and, through an accidental chain of events, became a natural cause that eventually placed the Pattini stone upon the crowned heads of Kanaka and Vijaya.

The more deeply one examines *Cilappatikāram*, the more its epic flavour becomes sweet.

**O anklet, anklet that seizes the heart!**
